ABSTRACT 
BRIDGING THE GAP: MANAGERS' EXTERNAL RELATIONSHIPS 
AND THEIR EFFECTS ON SUBORDINATES 
Neil Andrew Cohen 
Supervisor: Dr. Katherine J. Klein 
In this dissertation, I develop a conceptual model for how managers' roles as 
boundary spanners within their organizations affect the perceptions, attitudes, 
relationships, and performance of their subordinate employees. Drawing on literatures in 
social networks, status characteristics theory, social identity theory, and resource 
allocation theory, I suggest that managers' external relationships—the connections that 
managers make to organization members outside of their subordinate work group—have a 
material impact on their subordinates' perceptions of their work environment and their 
managers. These perceptions, I argue, serve as four specific mechanisms in an indirect 
effects model that connect managers' external relationship to their employees' task 
performance and work attitudes (e.g. organizational commitment) and the Leader 
Member Exchange (LMX) relationships between manager and subordinate. I apply the 
theoretical model to two salient types of managerial relationships: those with their bosses 
and those with their horizontal peers. 
I test the hypotheses in a field study across three sites encompassing 200 front line 
managers, their bosses, and their front line subordinate employees. I find that managers' 
viii 
external relationships serve as both conduits of information and perceptual cues of 
managerial status in the eyes of their subordinates. When managers have more extensive 
peer networks and deeper relationships with their bosses, their subordinates have greater 
access to organizational information and perceive the managers as higher status within 
the organization. Status is, in turn, related to subordinate performance, organizational 
commitment, and LMX with the focal manager. Information access is associated with 
subordinate organizational commitment. In addition, when subordinates perceive that 
they have the necessary resources to adequately perform their roles and when they 
perceive the manager as being "one" with the group—both potential aspects of the 
manager's boundary spanning role—they experience greater organizational commitment 
and LMX relationships. The framework and study shed considerable light on the nature 
of managers' boundary spanning roles and the manner in which their external 
relationships may shape these roles. 
The theoretical model and results make contributions to the literatures on 
leadership, social networks, and boundary spanning. I discuss these contributions as well 
as the implications of my findings for practicing managers.

SECTION I: INTRODUCTION 
When leadership researchers study managers, they generally study the manner in 
which managers supervise their subordinates and the effects these choices have on 
subordinate performance and work attitudes (Yukl, 2006). Within this rich stream of 
research, researchers have documented that managers may have tremendous effect on 
subordinate performance and work attitudes through the relationships they build with 
individual employees (see Graen & Uhl-Bien, 1995, for a review) and through the 
subordinate focused behaviors and leadership styles they choose to employ (Bass, 1990; 
Stogdill, 1974; see Judge & Piccolo, 2004; Judge, Piccolo, & Hies, 2004 for recent meta­
analyses). In this research, managers have an effect on subordinates, their work attitudes 
and their performance through either one-on-one/dyadic relationships or through the 
actions that managers take toward the work group or team as a whole (e.g. Balkundi & 
Harrison, 2006; Edmondson, 1999; Hackman, 1987). 
While this body of work is critical to our understanding of managerial roles and 
processes, it tells only a portion of the story about the role of a manager within an 
organization. For years, both the academic (Minztberg, 1973; Yukl, 2006) and 
practitioner (Davis, 1996; Kotter, 1999) literatures have recognized that managerial 
responsibilities extend beyond the direct management and supervision of subordinates. 
Formal managerial roles are seen as boundary spanning (Ancona & Caldwell, 1992) or 
brokerage roles (Mehra, Dixon, Brass, & Robertson, 2006) with the manager situated, 
within an organizational social system, as the critical, and often only, point of connection 
between his or her subordinates and the broader organization beyond the work group 
1 
(Mintzberg, 1973; Likert, 1961; Graen, Cashman, Ginsburg, & Scheimann, 1977). In 
these roles, managers are called upon to transfer resources and information in and out of 
the group (Whetten, 1978; Likert, 1961) and to serve as the face of the organization in the 
eyes of subordinates and the face of the work group in the eyes of the broader 
organization (Pfeffer, 1981; Mintzberg, 1973). To deliver on this brokerage role, 
managers must build and maintain relationships with stakeholders beyond than their 
subordinates—-e.g. peers, superiors, customers, investors, regulators, and others. 
Yet, leadership research rarely considers this web of external relationships that 
managers have with others throughout the organization, most notably their peers and their 
bosses1, or the effect these relationships have on the manager's subordinate employees. 
To be sure, there are rich and growing literatures which explore the impacts of 
individuals' relationships with their direct supervisors (e.g. Gerstner & Day, 1997; Graen 
& Uhl-Bien, 1995; Dienesch & Liden, 1986) and their network of relationships 
throughout the organization (e.g. Ibarra & Andrews, 1993; Mehra, Kilduff, & Brass, 
2001; Sparrowe, Liden, Wayne, & Kraimer, 2001). However, this research does not 
explicitly address those members in managerial roles and the connection of these upward 
and horizontal relationships to the delivery of downward supervision. Recognizing this 
gap in our understanding of managerial processes, two recent studies do explicitly study 
the impact of a manager's external relationships on providing early evidence that 
employees benefit from working for well connected managers (e.g. Tangirala, Green, & 
Ramanujam, 2007; Venkataramani, Green, & Schleicher, 2010). While encouraging, 
1 Throughout this document I will refer to the superior of the focal manager as the "boss." 
2 
these studies only begin our exploration of the issues of managers' boundary spanning. 
In particular, these studies leave open the question as to how the effects of managers' 
external relationships are transferred to subordinates. What are the mediating or linking 
mechanisms involved here? More pointedly, what are the aspects of the manager's 
boundary spanning role that benefit or potentially hinder their subordinates? In addition, 
research to date has yet to explore a full array of both external relationships and relevant 
individual subordinate level outcomes. 
In this dissertation, then, I explore managers' external relationships, their 
boundary spanning roles, and their effects of each on subordinates, focusing on three 
interrelated research questions. First, and foremost, do managers' external relationships 
affect the nature of the work life for their subordinates? In effect, how do managers' 
external relationships provide benefits to managers and subordinates alike? To what 
extent do managers' external relationships shape subordinate perceptions, attitudes, 
relationships, and performance? Second, how do external relationships affect more distal 
subordinate level outcomes such as performance and commitment? More pointedly, what 
are the more proximal effects through which managers' external relationships shape more 
distal outcomes. Third, and finally, do these effects and mechanisms differ by type of 
external relationships, in this case vertical relationships, i.e. between manager and boss, 
as compared to lateral relationships between managers and their peers? 
I construct and test theory that explores three types of effects of managers' 
external relationships. First, external relationships may create benefits for managers and 
subordinates. Following Podolny (2001), I draw a distinction between two manners in 
3 
which relationships and networks create benefits for organizational participants. First, 
under social network theory, managers use their external relationships to gain access to 
key task related resources1 and organizational information which they then make 
available to their subordinates (Tushman & Katz, 1980; Tushman & Scanlan, 1981). 
Second, drawing on status characteristics and expectation states theory, I argue that 
managers' external relationships serve as status markers (Berger, Cohen, & Zelditch, 
1972) in the eyes of their subordinates, such that subordinates use their managers' 
external relationships as signals of the manager's status within the organization. 
The second set of potential effects follow Adler & Kwon (2002) and consider 
potential downsides or costs of managers' external relationships. Drawing on social 
identity theory, I argue that managers' external relationships build cohesion and solidarity 
for them with external groups (Adler & Kwon, 2002; Coleman, 1990). But, this external 
solidarity may come at the expense of their cohesion and solidarity with their 
subordinates, weakening their identities as members of the subordinate work group 
(Tajfel & Turner, 1986; Hogg, 2001). Second, drawing on resource allocation theory 
(Becker, 1965), I argue that managers with extensive networks will be time constrained 
(Jansenn, 2001), leading them to be less available and accessible to their subordinates. 
2 As described in the theory section, in this study, I follow Spreitzer (1996) and Kanter (1986) and define 
"resource" as the things people need within and from an organization to get the job done. I note, however, 
that Foa and Foa (1974) define resources more broadly as "anything that can be transferred from one 
person to another (Brinberg & Castell, 1982, pg 260 )." Within the Foa & Foa framework, resources 
include six categories: money, goods, services, information, love, and status. I position information and 
status as distinct constructs as they are tend to occupy unique roles in social networks theory, and hence the 
theory of this study. Following Spreitzer and Kanter, then, resources generally refers to money, time, 
physical space, and materials and tools. 
4 
The final set of effects is more distal to the managers' external relationships and 
reflects the fundamental interests of organizational scholars and practitioners alike. 
Organizational scholars are fundamentally concerned with two types of outcomes at the 
subordinate level: performance and work attitudes (Katz & Kahn, 1978; Labianca & 
Brass, 2006; Harrison, Newman, & Roth, 2006). As such, I examine the effect that 
managers' external relationships have on subordinate task performance and 
organizational commitment common and sought after exemplars each of employee 
performance and work attitudes (Chiaburu & Harrison, 2008; Harrison et al., 2006). In 
addition, following the lead of Tangirala et al. (2007) and Venkataramani et al. (2010), I 
include the Leader Member Exchange (LMX) relationship between manager and 
subordinate as a key outcome of interest (Liden & Maslyn, 1998). The theory I build 
regarding these more distal outcomes takes two forms. First, I build an indirect effects 
model (Mathieu & Taylor, 2006) which suggests that managers' external relationships 
create the benefits and costs described above which in turn shape organizational 
commitment, performance, and LMX. Second, I integrate the notion of the costs and 
benefits to propose a curvilinear relationship between managers' boundary spanning 
relationships and these three subordinate outcomes. Figure 1 provides a summary of the 
theoretical model developed here. 
I use the theoretical framework to examine managers' relationships with two key 
types of stakeholder—peers and bosses. Peers and bosses reflect two of the most 
prominent managerial relationships (Mintzberg, 1973) and highlight differences between 
vertical, hierarchical relationships vested with power and control and rich in 
5 
organizational resources (Brass, 1984; Sparrowe & Liden, 2005; Graen et al., 1977) and 
horizontal, discretionary relationships potentially rich in knowledge and know-how 
(Mehra et al., 2006; Hansen, 1999). In addition, while other relevant stakeholder groups 
(e.g. customers, suppliers, shareholders, etc.) exist for some types of managers, bosses 
and peers are the stakeholders most common across managers in general. 
I test my hypotheses in a field study encompassing three US based service 
organizations. In these settings, I examined the external relationships of front line 
managers and their impact on the front line workers (subordinates) in those organizations. 
In conducting these tests, I controlled for the managers' display of transformational 
leadership behaviors. As already noted, researchers have compiled a wealth of evidence 
of the value of the downward or internal managerial role. In examining the external or 
boundary spanning roles, a strong test must account for these internal role effects 
(Chiaburu & Harrison, 2008). Research shows that transformational leadership is a 
strong predictor of subordinate attitudes and performance and is strongly correlated with 
other downward leadership behaviors (Judge and Piccolo, 2004). In addition, recent 
research suggests that transformational leadership may be a key factor in determining a 
leader's network configuration (Bono & Anderson, 2005). In the absence of controlling 
for transformational leadership, then, we would be left wondering the extent to which 
external relationships and roles add value beyond the well established internal 
perspective. 
The results of the empirical study paint a complex and nuanced picture of the 
effects of managers' external relationships and boundary spanning roles. The analyses 
6 
support a nuanced indirect effects model (Mathieu & Taylor, 2006) in which (a) 
managers' vertical and horizontal relationships predict subordinate information access, 
perceptions of managerial status, and manager accessibility and (b) these three effects 
are, in turn, related, in varying degrees, to subordinate organizational commitment, 
performance and LMX. At the same time, the results do not reveal substantial costs or 
downsides of managers' external relationships, highlighting instead the benefits that 
accrue to managers and subordinates. In addition, the analyses reveal a focused set of 
direct effects, primarily linking the managers' upward relationships with their bosses to 
the organizational commitment and performance of their subordinates. This combination 
of results, then, focuses attention on the intervening mechanisms, shedding considerable 
light on the manager's boundary spanning role, how external relationships may shape the 
role and how enactment of the role benefits subordinates. 
In building the theoretical framework and conducting the empirical tests of the 
hypotheses I make several contributions to the literatures on leadership, social networks, 
and boundary spanning. First, while this is not the first study to explicitly explore the role 
of the manager's external networks in bridging between his or her subordinate work 
group and the group's external environment (e.g. Tangirala et al., 2007; Venkataramani et 
al., 2010), it brings a more holistic framework to bear, which provides a more nuanced 
look at the phenomenon. By examining a wide range of effects, both proximal and distal, 
I build an integrative theory and analysis which highlights the subtle differences between 
mechanisms and the effects they may have on subordinate outcomes. Studies of 
managers' boundary spanning roles have yet to examine many of the potential 
7 
mechanisms through which the role may have an impact or the integration among them. 
In addition, this study encompasses a sample of distal subordinate outcomes more fully 
representative of the interests of micro-organizational scholars. Most notably, the study 
provides insight into both the subordinate level task (e.g. performance) and socio-
emotional (commitment) outcomes of managers' boundary spanning roles. The 
exploration of these cross level network effects is relatively new and, as such, the 
combination of multiple mechanisms and multiple outcomes in this study substantially 
extends our understanding of how the managers' boundary spanning role is formed and 
operates. 
Second, while the primary focus of this study is on managers' external activities, I 
theorize that the external relationships and their resulting effects stand as distinct from 
more traditionally explored internal or downward leadership dimensions and behaviors. 
To account for this, distinct from prior studies, in the empirical section, I control for the 
managers' display of transformational leadership. This creates a high theoretical and 
empirical hurdle for the external effects that are the focal point of the study. More 
broadly, this study paints a theoretical picture of the integration of manager's internal and 
external activities and their internal and external reputations. 
Third, by invoking and testing an intervening effects model, this study contributes 
to the social networks literature by continuing to unbundle the mechanisms through 
which social networks have their effects. The study explicates not just whether and in 
what form managers' boundary spanning roles matter, but how and why, articulating the 
key mechanisms that link, in complex ways, the managers' external ties and the work life 
8 
of their subordinates. For the most part, previous network studies do not quantify these 
effects (e.g. Mehra et al., 2006) nor do they theorize across them in an integrated fashion. 
In addition, following Adler & Kwon (2002), this study is one of the few that explicitly 
simultaneously considers the benefits and risks inherent in the construction of social 
networks, exploring the tensions and the possibility of tradeoffs between them. In not 
finding significant costs, the empirical portion of my study contributes by placing 
considerations of cost in a secondary position. Finally, this study provides practical 
implications for managers and those involved in their professional development, by 
illuminating the value in the trade-offs involved in building and maintaining interpersonal 
networks throughout the organization. 
The remainder of this dissertation unfolds as follows. In Section II, I identify and 
define the inputs and outcomes of my research question: the types of relationships 
(bosses and peers) under consideration and the choice and definition of the three 
outcomes of interests. In Section II, I also describe the hurdle posed by previous research 
focused on downward leadership factors and the relationships of managers internal to the 
work group. In Section III, I build theory and hypotheses, depicted in Figure 1, 
describing how managers' external relationships are associated with four aspects of the 
managers' boundary spanning role and how these mechanisms, in turn, may shape 
subordinate level outcomes. I build this theory for managers' positive external 
relationships with peers and bosses. In Section IV, I describe the empirical and approach 
I take to testing the hypotheses. In Section V, I describe the results from the empirical 
9 
tests. In section VI, I discuss the theoretical and practical implications and conclusions 
from the study. 
SECTION II: 
INPUTS AND OUTPUTS OF THE CONCEPTUAL MODEL 
The purpose of this study is to examine the effects that managers' external 
relationships with peers and bosses have on their direct subordinates, how the managers' 
external relationships shape their boundary spanning roles, and how the intervening 
effects that come from behaving as boundary spanners may shape the performance and 
attitudes of their subordinates. As depicted in figure 1, at the core of my conceptual 
model, I argue that the effects of managers' external relationships are best understood by 
examining several key intervening effects (Mathieu & Taylor, 2006) that combine to 
influence subordinate level outcomes. In this section, prior to building the model itself, I 
lay the groundwork by (a) defining the external relationships under consideration, (b) 
identifying and defining the subordinate outcomes of interest, (c) positioning external 
relationship effects as distinct and incremental from traditional downward leadership 
factors. 
II.l Inputs: External relationships with bosses and peers 
II. La Boss and peer relationships as distinct external relationships 
In this study, I examine two salient types of managers' external relationships, 
those with their boss and those with their managerial peers. The boss is defined as the 
10 
manager's direct superior with whom the manager has a formal reporting relationship. I 
define peers as those individuals at the same level in the organization performing roles 
that are similar to or interdependent with those of the focal manager (Chiaburu & 
Harrison, 2008). Each of these relationships (or types) is a key factor in the work life of 
the manager. Managers may spend a substantial portion of their time interacting with 
peers (Mintzberg, 1973; Ferris and Mitchell, 1987), and thus, peers represent likely 
sources of diverse interpersonal relationships and the resources that flow from them. 
Boss relationships, while consuming less time than peers (Mintzberg, 1973), are a 
specific form of a manager/subordinate relationship. Voluminous research documents 
that superiors have impacts on subordinate effort and behaviors, attitudes and emotions, 
and performance through the behaviors and leadership styles they enact (e.g. Bass, 1990; 
Stogdill, 1974) and the nature of the relationship they build with their subordinates (e.g. 
Graen & Uhl-bien, 1995). Thus, the relationship a manager enjoys with his or her boss 
should have substantial bearing on the manner in which the manager affects the work 
environment of his or her subordinates. 
II.Lb Conceptualizing boss relationships vs. peer networks 
The boss relationship is a single dyadic relationship between the boss and the 
manager. I capture the nature of dyadic relationships between managers and their bosses 
through the concept of Leader Leader Exchange (LLX), where LLX is a specific 
application of Leader Member Exchange or LMX (Liden & Maslyn, 1998). LMX 
reflects the mutual trust and respect that employees and their supervisors have for each 
11 
other and the resulting reciprocal influence that each has in the relationship (Graen & 
Uhl-Bien, 1995). The LMX relationship is rooted in two related processes. Under role 
theory, supervisors and their subordinates test different types of relationships that may 
define the dyad, ultimately arriving at or negotiating the roles, expectations and behaviors 
of both supervisor and subordinate (Graen, 1976; Katz & Kahn, 1978). And, as 
described by social exchange theory, supervisors and subordinates engage in an exchange 
of valued resources. For example, subordinates provide a level of effort and task 
performance in exchange for assignments, promotions, autonomy, and status (Graen & 
Scandura, 1987; Liden & Maslyn, 1998). Managers in high LMX dyads harvest 
influence with their subordinates not through the transactional doling out of rewards and 
consequences, but rather through establishing bonds of affection, mutual obligation, and 
expertise (French & Raven, 1959; Liden & Maslyn, 1998). As a result, in high LMX 
dyads, subordinates tend to exhibit higher levels of satisfaction (Graen & Novak, 1982; 
Duchon, Green, & Taber, 1986), subordinates have more attractive career paths 
(Scandura & Shriesheim, 1994), and subordinates tend to deliver higher levels of 
performance (Liden, Wayne, & Stillwell, 1993; Graen & Novak, 1982). 
In this study, there are two levels of LMX relationships: relationships between 
front line workers and their focal managers and relationships between the focal managers 
and their bosses. In order to differentiate the two, I follow the precedent set by Tangirala 
et al. (2007) and Venkataramani et al., (2010), using the term Leader Member Exchange (LMX) 
to reflect the relationship between subordinates and managers and using the term Leader Leader 
Exchange (LLX) to reflect the relationship between the focal manager and his or her boss. LLX 
12 
represents managers' external vertical relationships in my conceptual model. LMX is an 
outcome variable in the conceptual model. 
Unlike boss relationships, which usually only involve one other individual in a 
dyad, managers find themselves embedded in networks of relationships of multiple peers 
(and others) throughout the organization. While the strength of any one relationship or 
even the aggregate will have a bearing on the manager, peer relationships present benefits 
and challenges for managers due to their volume and the structure of the entire network 
of relationships (Brass, 1984; Ibarra, 1993; Sparrowe et al., 2001). In this study, I focus 
on the volume of relationships, captured in the social networks literature as degree 
centrality (Freeman, 1979). Degree centrality is simply a count of the number of 
relationships or ties of a specific type or content that an individual has. A manager's 
degree centrality in the peer network captures the extent to which the manager has direct 
relationships that provide instrumental or affective resources. 
II.l.c Dimensions of workplace relationships 
In the empirical portion of the study, I examine two fundamental aspects of the 
relationships that managers have with their bosses and peers. Within the organizational 
literature, researchers distinguish between two types of content that characterize ties 
within social networks: instrumental and affective. People have instrumental 
relationships with each other for the purpose of providing work or task related resources, 
such as technical and organizational information, budget allocations, and staffing for key 
projects (Blau, 1962, Lincoln & Miller, 1979; Ibarra, 1992; Tushman & Katz, 1980; 
13 
Balkundi & Harrison, 2006; Hansen, 1999). Individuals also have relationships, such as 
friendships, through which they build or exchange affective resources, such as social and 
emotional support, trust, and displays of positive affect (Lincoln & Miller, 1979; Ibarra, 
1993; Balkundi & Harrison, 2006). Affective relationships also often encompass trust 
relationships (Balkundi & Harrison, 2006). 
Like peer networks, the LLX relationship between managers and their bosses may 
have multiple dimensions to them. Although researchers initially conceptualized LMX as 
a purely instrumental construct (e.g. Dansereau, Graen, & Haga, 1975), more recently 
researchers have positioned LMX as a multidimensional construct that more holistically 
evaluates the nature of the relationship between manager and subordinate (Liden & 
Maslyn, 1998). Both theoretical foundations of LMX stress a multi-dimensional 
conceptualization. Under role theory, some dyads will be more task oriented, while 
others more socially focused, and others may encompass both dimensions (Katz & Kahn, 
1978; Liden & Maslyn, 1998). Similarly, as described above, network researchers have 
identified several different dimensions under which social exchange may occur. Beyond 
theory, recent empirical research also suggests that this multi-dimensional perspective 
adds to the significance of LMX as an explanatory variable (Greguras & Ford, 2006). 
I employ this more expansive definition of LMX in examining the LLX 
relationships in the empirical portion of this study, focusing on its four constituent parts: 
affect, loyalty, contribution, and professional respect. Affect is defined as "the mutual 
affection members of the dyad have for each other based primarily on interpersonal 
attraction, rather than work or professional values." Loyalty is "the expression of public 
14 
support for the goals and personal character of the other member of the dyad." 
Contribution reflects the "perception of the current level of work-oriented activity each 
member puts forth toward the mutual goals.. .of the dyad." And, professional respect is 
the perception of the degree to which each member of the dyad has built a reputation, 
within and/or outside the organization of excelling at his or her line of work. (Liden & 
Maslyn, 1998, pg 50) 
Although researchers find fine grained differences in outcomes (Baldwin, Bedell, 
& Johnson, 1997; Podolny & Baron, 1997; Greguras & Ford, 2006) and antecedents 
(Klein, Lim, Saltz, & Mayer, 2004) between the types of ties, recent research also 
suggests a degree of overlap between them. Instrumental and affective ties may be 
highly correlated and interdependent (Casciaro & Lobo, 2008; Liden & Maslyn, 1998) 
and instrumental resources may flow through affective ties (Hansen, 1999; Ibarra, 1992; 
Mehra et al., 2006; Podolny & Baron, 1997; Mehra et al., 2001). As such, in building 
theory, I treat the content of manager's relationships as uni-dimensional, but in the 
empirical study, I differentiate between instrumental and affective content.3 
II.2 Subordinate level outcomes 
J Recently, a third major distinction has emerged. A small portion of relationships or ties may be 
characterized as negative, centered on feelings of "dislike" (Labianca & Brass, 2006). Negative ties are 
generally considered to be affective in nature Qlabianca & Brass, 2006), but may also manifest themselves 
in the negative instrumental behaviors of actors (Sparrow et al., 2001). Negative relationships may be 
important as they do not simply have the opposite effect of positive ties (Labianca, Brass, & Gray, 1998) 
and may have an amplified effect on outcomes relative to positive ties (Labianca & Brass). As described in 
the discussion section, I did gather data on negative ties as well, in the form of "difficult relationships." In 
the interest of theoretical parsimony, I discuss the insights from these data in the form of post hoc analyses. 
15 
Within the voluminous research on individual employee's work experiences, 
organizational and leadership researchers often focus on three broad categories of work 
outcomes: employee performance, employee work attitudes, and the nature of 
relationships between employees and their leaders (Labianca & Brass, 2006; Harrison et 
al., 2006; Katz & Kahn, 1978). As such, I have selected three outcomes for this study 
that represent the more common outcomes of interest in each of these categories: task 
performance, organizational commitment, and leader-member exchange (LMX). I briefly 
define each of these outcomes next. 
II. 2. a Subordinate performance 
For the purposes of this study, I define performance as in-role or task 
performance, "the degree to which an individual meets or exceeds expectations about 
focal role requirements (Harrison et al., 2006, pg 307)." This definition is one of the 
more narrow definitions of performance, excluding extra-role behaviors, most notably 
organizational citizenship behaviors (e.g. Organ, 1988). I choose to focus on in-role 
performance as it provides the opportunity for objective, third party measurement and, in 
its narrowness, provides the most conservative test of the theory. 
II. 2. b Organizational commitment 
In this study, I follow Meyer & Allen (1984) and define organizational 
commitment as "emotional attachment to, identification with, and involvement in the 
organization (Meyer & Allen, 1984, pg. 373)." This definition, reframed as affective 
16 
commitment by Allen & Meyer (1990) is similar to Mowday, Porter, & Steer's (1979) 
conception of attitudinal commitment as "the relative strength of an individual's 
identification with and involvement in a particular organization, (pg. 226)" 
Organizational commitment is a key individual employee attitude and has strong 
correlations with employee performance, withdrawal and turnover behavior, and general 
well being (see meta-analyses by Meyer et al., 2002; Mathieu & Zajac, 1990). In 
addition, there is strong evidence to suggest that managers play a critical role in 
facilitating and nurturing the organizational commitment of their subordinate employees 
(Meyer et al.; Mathieu & Zajac, 1990)4. 
II. 2. c Leader Member Exchange—Manager/subordinate interpersonal relationships 
As described above, I capture the nature of dyadic relationships between 
managers and subordinates through the concept of Leader Member Exchange or LMX 
(Liden & Maslyn, 1998). 
II. 3 Influence of external relationships as distinct from established leadership 
effects 
4 Allen & Meyer's (1990) model of organizational commitment includes two additional components. 
Continuance commitment reflects the instrumental reasons and factors, often conceptualized as costs, that 
an individual may remain with an organization (Meyer, Stanley, Herscovitch, & Toplonystsky, 2002). 
Normative commitment refers to an individual's perceived obligation to remain with an organization 
(Meyer et al., 2002). Researchers observe, however, that these factors tend to have weaker observed 
empirical effects and may represent behavioral intentions rather than the attitudes of organization members 
(Solinger, van Olffen, & Roe, 2008). 
17 
For over 50 years, scholars have investigated the leadership factors that affect 
such outcomes as subordinate performance, work attitudes, and leader/follower 
processes. Research shows that managers' traits (Kenny & Zaccaro, 1983; Judge, Bono, 
Hies, & Gerhardt, 2002) and behaviors (Judge & Piccolo, 2004; House, 1977; Bass, 1990; 
Fiedler, 1967; Stogdill, 1974) have substantial impact on the work attitudes, productivity, 
and ongoing relationships of individuals within organizations. An inherent premise of 
this study is that managers' external relationships affect subordinates in ways that go 
beyond the effects of downward leadership behaviors. I address this set of issues in two 
ways. First, in the theory section, where appropriate, I articulate how managers' external 
relationships may contribute to intervening processes or outcomes above and beyond 
traditional leadership dimensions such as transformational leadership, initiating structure 
and consideration, Or internal leader-member exchange. These effects may represent the 
same mechanism with a magnified or incremental effect or they may represent different 
mechanisms. Second, in my empirical study, I explicitly control for extant leadership 
dimensions as potential explanatory variables, thus isolating the true incremental effects 
of managers' external relationships. In all cases, the hypotheses I generate presume that 
the effects exist above and beyond those associated with downward leadership 
dimensions. 
SECTION III: THEORY AND HYPOTHESES 
In this section, I articulate the theory of how managers' external relationships may 
shape subordinate level outcomes, both proximal and distal. Figure 1 displays a 
18 
graphical representation of the model, highlighting the four proximal outcomes which 
serve as intervening mechanisms through which external relationships may function and 
which represent the demands of the manager's boundary spanning role. After a brief 
overview (section III.l), this section is organized around the four processes (sections III.2 
to III. 5. For each mechanism, I build theory that explains the boundary spanning effect 
of the mechanism and then how that effect contributes to the distal subordinate outcomes. 
In section III.6, having discussed each effect individually, I integrate the theory to 
develop hypotheses linking external relationships directly to the distal subordinate level 
outcomes. Finally, in section III.7,1 discuss the potential differential effects of vertical 
boss relationships versus horizontal peer relationships. 
III.1 The benefits and costs of managers' external relationships 
Following Podolny (2001), I draw a distinction between two manners in which 
relationships and networks may create benefits for organizational participants. First, the 
central tenet of social networks theory is that interpersonal relationships function as 
conduits of resource exchange, in which the ties between two people facilitate the flow of 
information, affect, and tangible resources, among others (Brass, Galaskiewicz, Greve, & 
Tsai, 2004). Managers, by design, serve as conduits of information and resources 
between their workgroup and the organization beyond it, often finding themselves in a 
brokerage position (Mintzberg, 1973; Mehra et al., 2006). Managers use their external 
relationships to gain access to key task related resources and organizational information 
which they then make available to their subordinates (Tushman & Katz, 1980; Tushman 
19 
& Scanlan, 1981). Second, networks serve as informational or perceptual cues regarding 
the identity and reputation of the organizational member (Podolny, 2001). Drawing on 
status characteristics and expectation states theory, I argue that managers' external 
relationships serve as status markers in the eyes of their subordinates. Subordinates use 
their managers' external relationships as signals of the manager's status within the 
organization (Ashforth & Humphrey, 1995), assessing managers with broader, more 
positive connections as higher status within the organization (Kilduff & Krackhardt, 
1994; Brass, 1984). 
At the same time, theorists suggest that social networks may also come with 
potential costs or risks. Following Adler & Kwon (2002), I identify two categories of 
potential cost. First, while external relationships may facilitate perceptions of status and 
power for managers, they may also serve to weaken their solidarity with their subordinate 
work groups. Drawing on social identity theory, I argue that managers' external 
relationships build cohesion for them with external groups (Adler & Kwon, 2002; 
Coleman, 1988). But, this external solidarity comes at the expense of their cohesion and 
solidarity with their subordinates, weakening their identities as members of the 
subordinate work group (Tajfel & Turner, 1986; Hogg, 2001). Second, theorists suggest 
that extensive networks of relationships require investment and active maintenance 
(Adler & Kwon, 2002; Burt, 2001). Drawing on resource allocation theory (Becker, 
1965), I argue that managers with extensive networks will be time constrained (Jansenn, 
2001), leading them to be less available and accessible to their subordinates. 
20 
III.2 Managers' external relationships as conduits of resource exchange 
III. 2. a Managers' external relationships and subordinate access to information and 
resources 
That networks and interpersonal relationships serve as conduits of resource exchange is at 
the core of the social networks literature (Brass et al., 2004; Lincoln and Miller, 1979). Valued 
resources—information, affect, money, etc.—are passed from one individual to another through 
the ties that exist between these individuals. Ties as conduits of resource exchange frame the 
discussion of managers' external relationships in two ways. First, that managers are a conduit of 
information and resources is the centerpiece of the external portion of the manager's role (e.g. 
Tushman & Katz, 1980; Hansen, 1999; Mehra et al., 2006; Tsai & Ghoshal, 1998). In this role, 
managers act as brokers, filtering and synthesizing the flow of information and resources in and 
out the group (Mintzberg, 1973). Second, the portfolio of relationships that the manager 
maintains external to the group enhances his or her ability to play the boundary spanning role. 
In their role as boundary spanners, managers facilitate the movement of 
information in multiple directions. For example, they communicate with their bosses 
about the current performance and morale of their subordinates, thus providing their 
bosses with critical information about the well being of employees throughout the 
organization. Armed with this information, more senior executives are able to make 
changes to organization policies and procedures and re-allocate resources such that 
employee performance and well being are enhanced or optimized (Kanter, 1986). At the 
same time, managers are expected to use their connections with their bosses and their 
peers to bring information and resources from external sources to the subordinate 
employee (Graen et al., 1977; Spreitzer, 1996). I define access to resources and 
information as subordinates' having the tools and assets necessary to do their jobs and the 
21 
organizational knowledge that puts their jobs and tasks in context (Spreitzer, 1996). 
Relevant information includes technical task information and best practices (Mehra et al., 
2006) or organizational and competitive information (Lawler, 1992). For example, 
managers harvest information related to how the group's task fits into the organization as 
a whole or how it integrates with other neighboring sub-units. In effect, managers have 
access to the organizational context of their subordinates' task (Bowen & Lawler, 1992). 
In addition, managers must look to their bosses to provide tangible resources such as staff 
and budgets to allow their groups to perform optimally (Graen et. al, 1977; Kanter, 1986; 
Spreitzer, 1996). Further, managers may share information with each other that 
represents tacit knowledge and know-how that ultimately allows each group to execute 
their task more efficiently and effectively (Hansen, 1999). For example, sales managers 
who cover different geographic regions may share tactics with each other regarding to 
how to sell or position a particular product. Having gained this information from their 
peers, managers may then share it with their subordinates (Mehra et al., 2006; Tushman 
& Scanlan, 1981; Mintzberg, 1973; Graen et al, 1977). 
Although managers generally have access to both peers and bosses through the 
formal structure of the organization, managers with deeper and broader interpersonal 
relationships will have greater access to mission critical information and resources. 
Because of time constraints, bosses establish differentiated relationships with their 
subordinate managers and tend to select a small group of their managers to implicitly 
serve as their lieutenants (Graen, 1976). These lieutenant managers provide the boss with 
managerial leverage by taking on tasks and responsibilities that might otherwise be 
22 
performed by the boss himself. In so doing, bosses grant these lieutenant managers more 
autonomy and empower them by granting them access to much of the information 
available to the boss (Dansereau et al., 1975). It is necessary for bosses to provide this 
information so that the subordinate manager may effectively play the surrogate role. 
Over time, the relationships between superiors and their lieutenants solidify, with each 
member displaying greater affect, trust, and respect for each other (Graen & Uhl-bien, 
1995; Liden & Maslyn, 1998). Those subordinates who serve as lieutenants are viewed 
as part of the boss's "in-group." In order to maintain the subordinate as a trusted 
lieutenant, bosses tend to favor them, allocating disproportionate shares of such resources 
and spoils as assignments, bonuses, promotions, and task related resources to them 
(Dansereau et al., 1975; Scandura & Shriesheim, 1994). 
Managers may also take advantage of networks of peer relationships to access key 
information and resources. A key tenet of the social networks literature is that individuals 
who are central in intra-organizational social networks gain access to key information and 
resources that in turn enable performance, power and influence (Freeman, 1979; Burt, 
1992; Brass, 1984; Sparrowe et al., 2001; Mehra et al., 2001). A manager's peers within 
the organization perform similar (Mehra et al., 2006) or interrelated (Wageman, 1995) 
functions. As a result, when managers build and take advantage of interpersonal 
relationships with their peers they gain access to important task specific knowledge and 
potential best practices approaches (Hansen, 1999; Ancona & Caldwell, 1992; Szulnaski, 
1996). In addition, through their relationships with their peers, managers may learn the 
tacit rules of the road within the organization (Hill, Kogler, Bahniuk, Dobos, & Rouner, 
23 
1989) that shine a light on to whom to go for critical information or situation specific 
resources. Interpersonal relationships among peers create a form of social exchange 
(Blau, 1964) in which members feel compelled or obligated to assist each other in 
enhancing their performance (Seers, 1989; Ensher, Thomas, & Murphy, 2001; Chiaburu 
& Harrison, 2008). One manner in which peers may assist each other is by providing 
critical information, best practices, and resources. Although much of the transfer 
discussed here is instrumental in nature, managers may benefit the most when the 
relationships in question are affective. Often friendships that encompass mutual 
investment and trust and extend beyond transactional work relationships are necessary for 
the transfer of high stakes or sensitive information (Mehra et al., 2006; Graen & Uhl-
bien, 1995). At the limit, individuals who are part of cohesive groups built on a 
foundation of affective support feel compelled by group norms to favor those in their 
group or network (Coleman, 1988; Hogg, 2001). 
Managers' external ties, then, differ in their effect from manager's downward ties 
and behaviors. Where a leader's internal ties allow for sharing of information within the 
group (Balkundi & Harrison, 2006), external relationships expand the pool of information 
and possibilities (Ancona & Caldwell, 1992; Balkundi & Harrison, 2006; Mehra et al., 
2006). Internal ties and behaviors govern how information and resources are put to use, 
but external ties govern what information and resources are available. 
The preceding arguments suggest that when managers have extensive external networks, 
their subordinates will enjoy higher access to resources and information. Throughout this section, 
I use "network" to refer to both the peer network and the upward LLX relationship. Similarly, I 
24 
use "extensive" to refer to both the strength of the LLX relationship and the degree centrality 
within the peer network. 
Hypothesis 1: The more extensive a manager's network of external 
relationships, the greater will be his or her subordinates' access to 
information and resources. 
III.2.b The effects of access to information and resources on subordinates 
A variety of extant theories provide the link between information and resource 
access, on the one hand, and subordinate performance and organizational commitment, 
on the other. Self efficacy theory suggests that individuals are motivated to engage in 
task behaviors when they believe that they are capable of performing the focal task 
(Bandura, 1986). Enhanced resources, in the form of equipment, funding for events, 
training, etc., allow subordinates to successfully complete their tasks. And, having the 
right tools allows subordinates to perceive themselves as more capable to deliver on the 
task or of having greater task efficacy (Gist & Mitchell, 1992; Spreitzer, 1996; Eden, 
2001). Self-efficacy also creates the potential of greater fit between the individual 
subordinate and the organization (O'Reilly, Chatman, & Caldwell, 1991). Information 
access is also linked to theories of motivation from job design (Hackman & Oldham, 
1976). When subordinates have more information from the organization, they are better 
positioned to understand how their specific task fits into the overall mission of the 
organization (Bowman & Lawler, 1992; Hackman & Oldham, 1976) and the future 
direction of the work (Mehra et al., 2006). When workers understand the significance of 
25 
their task and how it contributes, they experience greater motivation and satisfaction 
(Hackman & Oldham, 1976; Spreitzer, 1996). 
Access to information also activates processes of organizational identification. 
The information that comes through external relationships extends well beyond that of 
simply the focal work group and serves to define the boundaries of the larger 
organization itself (Ashforth & Mael, 1989). Managers' bosses and peers are closer, than 
subordinates, to the strategy and integrated views of external environment of the 
organization (Yukl, 2006; Ancona & Caldwell, 1992), thus the information the manager 
may harvest provides the subordinates with a more holistic view of the organization, its 
mission and its sources of distinctiveness (Ashforth & Mael, 1989). In addition, 
managers with strong external ties may internalize the values of the organization 
themselves (Graen & Cashman, 1975; Tyler & Blader, 2000) and are more vocal 
champions of organizational goals and objectives (Shamir, Zakay, Popper, & Breinin, 
1998; Tangirala et al., 2007). Armed with such information, subordinates will tend to 
adopt the organization, its identity and values as their own (Ashforth & Mael, 1989; 
Tajfel & Turner, 1986). 
Drawing upon self efficacy theory and job design theory, when subordinates 
experience greater levels of information and resource access, they experience greater 
levels of task motivation, ultimately leading to higher performance. 
Hypothesis 2: The greater subordinate access to information and resources, 
the greater subordinate performance will be. 
26 
Similarly I draw upon the logics of self efficacy theory, job design, and 
organizational identification to suggest that greater access to information and resources 
enhances the subordinates psychological connection to the organization as a whole 
leading to greater organizational commitment. In addition, when subordinates see that 
the necessary resources have been provided by the organization, they perceive the 
organization as more supportive (Tangirala et al, 2007; Spreitzer, 1996; Eisenberger, 
Huntington, Hutchinson, & Sowa, 1986). When employees feel as if they fit and are 
being supported by the organization, they become more loyal and committed (Meyer et 
al., 2002). 
Hypothesis 3: The greater subordinates' access to information and resources 
is, the greater their levels of organizational commitment will be. 
III.3 Managers' external relationships as signals of status 
III. 3. a Managers' external relationships and their perceived status within the 
organization 
In describing institutional actors within markets, Podolny (2001) highlights the 
role of network ties as signaling the "quality" of a particular individual within a market or 
network. A third party observer perceives the network ties and position of a target actor 
and, based on the observer's assessment of the target actor's position and partners, 
makes an assessment of the hierarchical status of the target actor within the relevant 
market. While Podonly's conception operates on a macro, institutional level, we can 
apply it to a micro, intra-organizational context by appealing to Status Characteristics and 
27 
Expectant States Theory (Berger et al., 1972). Under this theory, individuals rank order 
other individuals and ascribe status to them based on status characteristics (Anderson, 
Berger, Cohen, & Zelditch, 1966; Berger et al., 1972). Status characteristics are visible 
and observable attributes of individual and include demographic characteristics, e.g. race, 
gender, age, as well as attained characteristics such as education, occupation, or 
hierarchical rank with an organization (Anderson et al., 1966; Berger et al., 1972; Blau & 
Duncan, 1967). In this conceptualization of status, individuals who occupy higher rungs 
in the rank ordering, e.g. EVPs over front line workers, are perceived by others as more 
prestigious and having greater levels of power within the relevant domain (Berger et al., 
1972; Adler & Kwon, 2002). Status, then, in Gould's definition is "the prestige accorded 
to individuals because of the abstract positions they occupy rather than because of 
immediately observable behavior. (Gould, 2002, p. 1147)" While in some instances the 
rank ordering of status may very well correlate with real power, e.g. more senior 
executives within an organization, in other situations, people may use the status 
characteristic independent of any other objective criteria to ascribe power to others. 
Expectant States Theory also holds that, having ascribed high status to a particular 
individual, the perceiver also comes to expect higher competence and performance from 
this individual (Ridgeway & Berger, 1986). 
Using Podolny's metaphor in this context, one's social network ties and his or her 
network position become status characteristics that others may use to make status 
assessments about the focal individual. Wilhin the Status Characteristics framework, 
network position may be conceptualized as an attained status (Blau & Duncan, 1967), 
28 
much like higher position in a formal organizational hierarchy (Cross & Cummings, 
2004). And, as with many positions of attained status, the relationship between power 
and status is co-evolutionary—power breeds performance (or performance perceptions) 
which breeds status which breeds power (Gould, 2002). Social network research—which 
focuses on individual relationships (or ties) between members and the broad network (or 
pattern) of ties that are created throughout organizations—demonstrates that the nature 
and pattern of an individual's connections creates differential status within organizations. 
More specifically, people who are more central in organizational networks achieve higher 
status, as perceived by others, in the form of reputations for influence, power, or 
leadership (Brass, 1984; Mehra et al., 2006, Kilduff & Krackhart, 1994). In addition, 
individuals who have relationships with high status others are perceived as high status 
themselves (Bonacich, 1987; Brass, 1984), are thought to have higher performance 
(Kilduff & Krackhardt, 1994) and have the opportunity to bestow their status upon others 
(Sparrowe & Liden, 2005). Therefore, managers who maintain large numbers of external 
relationships or who have relationships with high status peers will be perceived as high 
status themselves. Similarly, when individuals enjoy "in-group" status with their boss, 
they are viewed as higher status by those around them (Dienesch & Liden, 1986; Duchon 
et al., 1986; Rhoades & Eisenberger, 2002). 
Managers with strong ties to their bosses and peers, then, develop reputations as 
being of higher status in the eyes of others. In this study, I define perceived manager 
status as the subordinates' assessment that the manager commands respect and influence 
throughout the broader organization (Eisenberger et al., 2002). While two managers may 
29 
occupy the same position formally within the organizational hierarchy or organization 
chart, differences in their effectiveness are partially a result of differences in how much 
power and influence their subordinates believe they curry throughout the organization. 
Status effects from a manager's external relationships are substantially different 
from the benefits of the managers' internal ties and downward leadership behaviors. 
Although engaging in transformational leadership behaviors may trigger pre-conceived 
notions of leadership, and thus status (Lord, Foti, & de Vader, 1984), external ties 
provide direct proximal signals of status within the broader organization by connecting 
the manager to high status others (Sparrowe & Liden, 2005). 
Hypothesis 4: The more extensive a manager's external network, the higher 
status subordmates will perceive the manager to be. 
III.2.b The effects of managers' perceived status on subordinates 
Theories of social identity suggest that, when subordinates perceive their 
managers as high status, subordinates themselves will experience and enact a tighter 
connection to the organization. The manager represents the "face" of the group 
(Mintzberg, 1973; Pfeffer, 1981), such that subordinates translate their manager's higher 
status to higher status for the group as a whole and of the individual members in it 
(Sparrowe & Liden, 2005; Bonacich, 1987; Cialdini, Borden, Thorne, Walker, Freeman, 
& Sloane, 1976). But, social identity theory, a theory of intergroup and intra-group 
differences, suggests that people strive for status and desire to be members of high status 
groups (Tajfel & Turner, 1986). As such, they are more likely to attempt to remain in 
30 
high status groups (Terry, Carey, & Callan, 2001; Van Knippenberg & Ellemers, 1993) 
and are more likely to attempt to leave low status groups (Ellemers, van Knippenberg, de 
Vries, & Wilke, 1988). Membership in high status groups confers confidence and self 
esteem (Hogg, 2001). Further, people in high status groups come to expect higher levels 
of support from the organization (Eisenberger et al, 2002) and the prospects of enhanced 
career success (Podolny & Baron, 1997). As a result, members of high status groups 
experience greater levels of organizational attachment and commitment (Terry et al., 
2001). 
Hypothesis 5: The higher status subordinates perceive their managers to be, 
the greater the subordinates' organizational commitment will be. 
When subordinates perceive their managers as high status, the subordinates are 
motivated to build strong relationships with the manager and deliver more on behalf of 
the manager. Expectant States Theory suggests that once an individual ascribes high (or 
low) status to another, the perceiver comes to have higher performance expectations of 
the target (Ridgeway & Berger, 1986). Subordinates who work for high (perceived) 
status managers, then, expect that their relationship with the manager may be more 
valuable to the subordinate. As with other positions of attained status, managers who are 
high status are able to do more for their subordinates, to wield their influence on behalf of 
their subordinates. Well connected managers are in a better position to harvest resources 
and rewards for their subordinates (Graen et al., 1977). And, managers with strong 
connections and high perceived status make powerful sponsors (Sparrowe & Liden, 
2005) or mentors (Podolny & Baron, 1997) and may be, therefore, instrumental in their 
31 
subordinates' future career success. Even if, under Status Characteristics Theory, the 
manager only appears higher status, subordinates will still come to expect greater 
influence and performance from him or her. Subordinates will perceive that the manager 
can be more helpful to them in the future, i.e. can show more in-group favoritism, so 
subordinates afford the manager latitude now. Thus, subordinates of a high status 
manager will afford the manager more influence and will be willing to exert greater effort 
for the manager, because of the prospects of future social exchange (Blau, 1964). 
Subordinates with high status managers will be more likely to strive for high LMX 
relationships, expecting that the spoils will be that much richer (Sparrowe & Liden, 
2005). 
Hypothesis 6: The higher status subordinates perceive their manager to be, 
the higher the LMX relationship will be between the manager and 
subordinate. 
III.4 Managers' external relationships as signals of ingroup identity 
III. 4. a Managers' external relationships and their ascribed work group identities 
Dating back to Simmel (1908 [1955]), researchers and theorists have held the 
view that our identities (as viewed by self and others) are partially determined the 
relationships we keep. Identity is defined as 'a self-referential description that provides 
contextually appropriate answers to the question "Who am I?" or "Who are we?" 
(Ashforth, Harrison, & Corley, 2008), and, as such, refers to an intra-psychic 
phenomenon. In contrast, in this study, I examine the external view or the perception of 
32 
others (Fiske & Taylor, 1991), i.e. how others perceive and define a target individual. 
This construct is less consistently labeled, sometimes referred to as reputation (Dutton, 
Dukerich and Harquail, 1994; Mehra et al., 2006; McFarland & Pals, 2005) and at other 
times simply referred to as a categorization (Stangor, Lynch, Duan, & Glass, 1992; 
Kulik, Roberson, & Perry, 2007). Indeed, in social identity research (e.g. Hogg, 2001) 
the categorizations made by others are often referred to implicitly but are not defined, 
labeled, or measured. In this study I will refer to the perceptions of others or a target 
individual as an individual's ascribed identity, i.e. the answer to the question "who are 
you? (as seen by me)." The argument in this section is that individuals' interpersonal 
relationships serve to define their ascribed identity, i.e. who they are in the eyes of others. 
Social Identity Theory (SIT) suggests that, over time, individuals develop their 
identities and their assessments of others based on the social and organizational groups of 
which they are members (Tajfel & Turner, 1986; Ashforth & Mael, 1989). The stronger 
the group definitions and salience are, the more group membership influences how a 
manager perceives and identifies him or herself and how others perceive and identify the 
manager. Members of groups, or dyads, develop a prototype of what it means to be part 
of the focal group, and individual members come to identify themselves and categorize 
others based on the prototypes of the group as a whole (Tajfel & Turner, 1986; Hogg, 
2001). Individuals come to be perceived as "one" with group because they are similar to 
the group prototype (Hogg, 2001), because they exhibit in-group favoring behaviors 
(Haslam & Platow, 2001; Haslam, Platow, Turner, Reynolds, Megarty, Oakes, Johnson, 
Ryan, & Veenstra, 2001), or because they have extensive social connections with the 
33 
group in question (Ashforth & Mael, 1989). The more an individual is perceived as "one 
of us" or "one of them", the more his or her identity (self or ascribed) is associated with 
the group in question. For example, if a manager shares the same functional background 
and career path of his or her subordinates—e.g. coming up through the ranks of the sales 
force—both the manager and the subordinates will perceive the manager as one of "us," 
one of the subordinate group. Once an individual is deemed a member of a salient group, 
his or her identity is increasingly defined by the attributes of the group. Further, the 
behaviors of the member toward the group and of the group toward the member are 
increasingly a function of group membership rather than individual beliefs and identity. 
In my example, the manager who shares a salient background in sales with his or her 
subordinates will be defined as a "salesperson" rather than by his or her idiosyncratic 
background. People who identify with a particular group will favor the in-group in 
resource allocation decisions (Tajfel & Turner, 1986). Similarly, the more members 
perceive the leader or manager as one with the group, the more they expect him or her to 
engage in in-group favoring behavior. Further, the more members perceive a particular 
individual as being "one of us" the more power and influence accrues to that member, 
including the leader (Hogg, 2001). 
Although theorists in the social identity tradition make reference to the concept of 
perceiving others as being "one" with a particular social group, they do not frequently 
define the construct and even less frequently explicitly measure it. Rather, researchers in 
the SIT tradition experimentally manipulate group member proto-typicality, assuming the 
construction of identities (self and ascribed) as intervening variables (e.g. Hogg, Hains, & 
34 
Mason, 1998; Duck & Fielding, 1999). In this study, I explicitly define ascribed identity 
as the perception that subordinates hold of a focal manager that the manager exhibits 
"oneness" with the subordinate workgroup and that the subordinates perceive the 
manager to be a member of the work group. 
Managers who have strong relationships with external stakeholders, whether 
bosses or peers, will be less likely to be seen as subordinate in-group members, i.e. to 
have weaker ascribed identities. As such, external ties, which may be a benefit in 
creating opportunities for the manager outside the subordinate work group, could become 
a cost for the manager inside the group (Adler & Kwon, 2002). There are several 
possible reasons for this. First, at the core, social identity theory is a theory of intergroup 
conflict (Tajfel & Turner, 1986). In-group membership and proto-typicality, and in-
group bias, are evaluated relative to a salient out-group, with whom the in-group vies for 
status (Tajfel & Turner, 1986) or resources (March & Simon, 1958; Sherif, 1967). When 
managers have strong relationships with peers and bosses, subordinates may perceive 
them as being one with some salient out-group, perhaps "management" or an "upper 
echelon." Although these dynamics will vary by organization, industry, and national 
culture, if people in upper tiers of organizational hierarchies are viewed as in conflict 
with lower tiers, managers with strong ties to upper tiers may be perceived as distant 
from or not "one of the subordinate group. Two recent studies speculate that these 
5 Within this definition, the concept of "oneness" is implicitly left to the perceiver to determine what 
constitutes "oneness". This is consistent with the manner in which social identity theorists define self 
targeted social identity (Tajfel & Turner, 1986; Ashforth & Mael, 1989), although the concept is more fully 
bounded when operationalized in measures of social identification (Mael & Ashforth, 1992; Hogg et al., 
1998) in which identity is measured as a function of (a) feelings of belongingness, (b) assessments of 
interpersonal fit, and (c) beliefs that external assessments of the group transfer to the individual member as 
well. 
35 
dynamics are at play for select groups of middle managers. Shamir et al. (1998) found 
that when squad leaders in the Israeli army enacted transformational leadership behaviors 
involving invoking superordinate goals, their subordinate troops viewed them less 
favorably. Follow up interviews revealed that enlisted personnel had great distrust of 
senior officers and that when squad leaders invoked vision and mission statements, 
soldiers perceived the squad leaders as trying to act like senior officers. Similarly, Mehra 
et. al (2006) examined the friendship ties of sales managers in a financial services 
company. When sales managers were central in the network of their bosses' peers, the 
managers' subordinates viewed them less favorably. Follow up interviews revealed that 
subordinates perceived senior management as hoarding compensation for themselves and 
not sharing it equitably with employees in the lower ranks. Thus, managers who were 
aligned with senior management were perceived as one of them where "them" 
represented a distinctly negative out-group. 
In the event that external relationships do not trigger a negative out-group 
categorization, subordinates are still less likely to perceive the manager who has 
extensive external ties as one of "us." An individual's identity (self or ascribed) is 
usually a function of membership in multiple categories simultaneously (Simmel, 1908 
[1955]; Kulik et al., 2007; Ashforth & Mael, 1989; Settles, Seller, & Damas, 2002). Prior 
research documents that in situations of multiple potential external or other 
categorizations, the strength of identification with any one category diminishes (Crisp & 
Hewstone, 2001). Confronted with the possibility of seeing their manager as a "work 
group member" or a "management group member," subordinates may hold neither 
36 
identity as firmly. And, as a final alternative, managers' external associations beyond the 
work group may prime the identification with the broader or superordrnate organization. 
Prior research shows that identification with the superordinate group may reduce 
identification with any one sub-group (Duck & Fielding, 1999; Gaertner, Dovidio, Nier, 
Ward, & Banker, 1999). 
The social identity mechanism described here is the same as that which operates 
inside the group, simply operating in the opposite direction. Thus, though the mechanism 
is the same, external ties may have a decidedly different effect on ascribed identity than 
internal ties. At the same time, social identity is a different mechanism than downward 
leader behaviors. Indeed, social identity theory suggests that under conditions of high 
salience, social identity factors will dilute the effect of individual differences in 
leadership behaviors and or implicit leadership theories (Hogg et al., 1998; Hogg, 2001), 
favoring group based perceptions and attributions over individualized ones. 
Hypothesis 7: The more extensive a manager's external network is, the 
weaker the manager's ascribed identity will be. 
IIIA.b The effects of managers' ascribed identity on subordinates 
Looking through a social identity lens, then, external relationships may present a 
challenge to a manager's ability to lead subordinates. The weaker a manager's ascribed 
identity, the weaker the manager's intra-group influence. The essential prediction of the 
social identity theory of leadership (Hogg, 2001, Hogg et al., 1998) is that the more 
proto-typical a leader is of the group, i.e. the more the group perceives the leader as one 
37 
of them, the more social attraction is felt between leader and follower, and the more 
latitude and influence the leader enjoys. When subordinates perceive their managers as 
full group members, they have confidence that the manager will put the subordinate 
group's interests above those of other organizational members. The manager can be 
expected to lobby for rewards for the team and invest effort in removing roadblocks. 
Proto-typicality and similarity of the manager relieves stress that subordinates may have 
felt due to uncertainty. Further, the manager is more likely to re-affirm the in-group 
identity and thus improve the self esteem of the subordinate work group members. In 
these instances, leaders gain influence over followers and commitment of the followers 
for enhanced effort. For leaders who are less prototypical or are clearly aligned with 
competitive out-groups, subordinates tread more warily, affording the manager less 
latitude and discretion. Work arrangements become more transactional and negotiated. 
Hypothesis 8: The stronger a manager's ascribed identity, the stronger the 
LMX relationships between manager and subordinate. 
When the manager is less identified with the work group, subordinate 
organizational commitment will also suffer. Managers who are more distant from the 
group enjoy less latitude and influence (Hogg, 2001). Managers are often seen as the 
face or personification of the organization for subordinates (Mintzberg, 1973; Pfeffer, 
1981; Tangirala et al., 2007). To the extent that the manager is not seen as "one of us," 
efforts they may make to reinforce the connection between a subordinate and the broader 
organization may command less attention with the subordinates. Further, when 
subordinates perceive their managers as less aligned with the group's interest, they are 
38 
likely to perceive less general support from the organization leading to lower levels of 
organizational commitment (Eisenberger, Stinglhamer, Vendenberghe, Sucharski, & 
Rhoades, 2002; Meyer et al., 2002). 
Hypothesis 9: The stronger a manager's ascribed work group identity, the 
stronger the organizational commitment of the manager's subordinates. 
III.5 Managers' external relationships as consumers of time, a scarce resource 
III. 5. a Managers' external relationships and manager accessibility 
Despite the potential benefits that accrue to managers with extensive networks, 
building and maintaining relationships also come with costs and require investment 
(Adler & Kwon, 2002). In particular, I suggest that managers who have numerous 
strong external relationships will be more busy, i.e. time constrained, and will be less 
accessible to their subordinates. I define manager accessibility as the manager having the 
time available and using the time to be physically available to interact with subordinates 
to provide direction and input and to address issues salient to them. In building the 
theory in this section, I appeal to resource allocation theory. Under resource allocation 
theory, individuals recognize time as a fixed physical constraint in their lives and make 
decisions as to how to allocate it. In particular, people make decisions regarding the 
economic and other utility trade-offs between time spent at work, a school, at leisure, and 
in sustenance (Becker, 1965). Within the work environment, people are confronted with 
a myriad of choices of how to spend their time, including working toward task 
performance, engaging in organizational citizenship behavior, socializing and 
39 
networking, and any number of others activities (Drago & Garvey, 1998). In this 
conceptualization, time spent in one activity must come at the expense of time spent in 
another (Bergeron, 2007). 
The issue of time constraints is of particular salience for managers. Managers 
have greater aggregate demands on their time and more varied options (Goode, 1960). 
This conception is consistent with age old refrains regarding managers' lack of necessary 
time to deliver on their appointed tasks (Mintzberg, 1973; Hall & Lawler, 1970). 
Networking activities are part of the managers' option set. Interpersonal relationships 
require time. Friends, as an example, demand attention and interaction. In the absence of 
interaction, friendships generally atrophy (Burt, 2001). Thus the more positive 
relationships a manager has, the more time the manager must spend tending to them 
rather than engaging in other activities. But, under time constraints, managers must make 
choices about which relationships to foster. This cost based view is espoused by 
sociologists and exchange theorists (Burt, 1992; Blau, 1964) who suggest that there is an 
optimum number and kind of relationship that balances unique or advantaged information 
and resource effects with personal cost dynamics. Similarly, Leader Member Exchange 
theory is partially premised on the manager's lack of time to invest to create high quality 
LMX relationships with each subordinate (Dienesch & Liden, 1986; Graen, 1976), and 
evidence suggests that managers with broader spans of control, i.e. more direct 
subordinates, have greater differentiation in their LMX relationships than those who do 
not (Cogliser & Schriesheim; 2000). While LMX theory is limited to the allocation of 
time among a manager's subordinates, the argument here extends the logic to a broader 
40 
set of stakeholders making claims on the manager's time. To the extent that a manager 
has rich external relationships, his or her time for the remainder of the job definition will 
be limited to some degree. 
A second form of time constraint materializes in the form of obligations and 
expectations of behavior (Coleman, 1988). Relationships, whether at a dyadic level or 
within a group, ultimately come with expectations of member behavior. For a manager 
with external relationships, these behaviors may come in the form of providing assistance 
on pet projects of their relationship partners. Following social exchange theory (Blau, 
1964), participants in interpersonal relationships will come to expect a level of 
reciprocity in behavior in effect saying, "You're my friend and I do this for you, so I 
expect you to do this for me." Managers with strong interpersonal relationships may feel 
compelled to engage in work on projects that meet relationship partners' needs but, again, 
continue to detract from their otherwise stated roles and responsibilities. 
In either case, when managers have extensive external networks, their 
subordinates will have less access to them. As theorized, however, the mechanism of 
time which constrains manager accessibility is the same whether relationships are internal 
or external. Thus, the logic that suggests that managers with broad spans of control will 
have lower LMX relationships with more subordinates (Cogliser & Schriesheim, 2000) is 
simply amplified by consideration of external commitments as well. 
Hypothesis 10: The more extensive a manager's external network, the lower 
subordinates' access to the manger will be. 
41 
III. 4. b The effects of manager accessibility on subordinates 
Managers' accessibility has an impact on subordinate performance and the nature 
of the LMX relationships between managers and subordinates. First, integrating across 
the leadership and job demands research suggests that manager accessibility will have a 
negative impact on subordinate performance but only at low levels of access. When 
managers have less time available, they focus more readily on the core task and actually 
deliver better performance up to a limit (Janssen, 2001). Indeed, when under time 
pressure, managers make more frequent use of directive and task oriented behaviors than 
otherwise (DeCoster & Fertakis, 1968). Paradoxically, then, moderate levels of time 
pressure, or moderately constrained accessibility, focus managers' attention on 
subordinate task performance. As a result, subordinate performance may very well 
improve. Alternatively, under periods of extreme demands and limited accessibility 
managers may get overwhelmed or burned out (Janssen, 2001), leading to diminished in-
role performance, in this case leading and directing subordinates. The job demands 
literature finds a consistent curvilinear relationship between perceived job demands and 
individual performance. Performance is highest at moderate levels of demand and lower 
at the extremes. I predict that managers' time will have the same effect on subordinate 
performance. 
Hypothesis 11: There will be a curvilinear relationship between 
subordinates' aceess to their manager and subordinates' performance. 
Subordinates will perform best under conditions of moderate manager 
accessibility and lower at very low or very high accessibility. 
42 
Previously, I argued that managers with extensive external networks harvest 
information which in turn facilitates the organizational identification and commitment of 
their subordinates. At the same time, the time commitments created by these networks 
and the resulting lack of manager access may make it difficult for managers to effectively 
communicate or transfer key information (Manev & Stevenson, 2001). Research 
suggests that the quantity of managerial communication to subordinates improves 
subordinate organizational commitment (Mathieu & Zajac, 1990). In converse, with low 
access managers, subordinates are not exposed to key commitment enhancing 
communications. 
Hypothesis 12: The higher subordinates perceive access to their manager to 
be, the higher the subordinates' organizational commitment. 
Managers' accessibility will also have an impact on their LMX relationships with 
subordinates. While managers' focus on directing subordinates and delegating work may 
improve under periods of high job demands, relationships require time and attention to 
build and maintain (Burt, 2001). The time required to maintain external relationships
