will inevitably detract from the same type of efforts with subordinates. Both are sizable 
groups of individuals with idiosyncratic needs. Indeed, although a bit mixed (e.g. Kinicki 
& Vecchio, 1994), previous theory and empirics suggest that manager job demands lead 
to more differentiated subordinate relationships (see Cogliser & Schriesheim, 2000, for a 
review of prior research). 
Hypothesis 13: The more access subordinates have to their manager, the 
stronger the LMX relationship will be. 
43 
III.5 Integrating across the four effects: The curvilinear relationship between 
manager external ties and subordinate level outcomes 
The theory thus far has outlined two stages of an indirect effects model. First, 
manager external relationships (with bosses and peers) predict four benefits and costs 
(access to information and resources, perceived manager status, ascribed identity, and 
manager accessibility). Second, the four benefits and costs predict the distal effects for 
subordinates (performance, organizational commitment, and LMX). The next question, 
then, is how do the four intervening mechanisms integrate to chart the connections 
between managers' external relationships and subordinate level outcomes? 
I argue that the benefits and costs of managers' networks counterbalance each 
other at different levels of external relationships such that the relationship between a 
manager's external network and outcomes for his or her subordinates is curvilinear. At 
moderate levels of external network, subordinate performance, subordinate organizational 
commitment and the quality of the LMX relationship between manager and subordinate 
are high. But, when managers have either limited or very extensive networks, 
subordinate outcomes suffer. This inverted U-shaped effect occurs, I argue, because the 
benefits of external relationships are particularly pronounced at lower levels of external 
relationships but yield diminishing marginal returns at higher levels, while in contrast, the 
costs of external relationships have a minimum threshold before they are truly 
experienced. 
This may be best illustrated by comparing the tradeoffs between resources and 
time and between status and ascribed identity. Structuralists suggest that direct 
44 
relationships become redundant as sources of resource (Burt, 1992; Mehra et al, 2001). 
Within a particular network, having strong relationships with a specific subset of actors 
provides access to most of the relevant resource, for example best practices among peers. 
Beyond a point, the next incremental contact provides redundant information. Yet, the 
next incremental contact may still require tending (Burt, 2001) and favor exchange (Blau, 
1964). Indeed, research in job demands (Janssen, 2001) suggests that people don't 
experience the negative aspects of increased workload until very high levels of load are 
reached. And, from a practical standpoint, a manager's time may need to be balanced in 
order to allow resources to flow from outside to inside the group. If external 
relationships constrain a manager's time, he or she may have access to plenty of 
information with no capability to disseminate it (Manev & Stevenson, 2001). 
High quality relationships with bosses also create asymmetric effects between 
instrumental benefits and costs. At the core of LMX theory, superiors make decisions as 
to which direct report managers will and will not be in their in-group, serving as trusted 
lieutenants (Dansereau et al., 1975). This decision, or role making, is thought to be 
binary. The subordinate manager is either "in" or "out", participating in either an 
increasingly affect laden relationship or one that is purely transactional. Presumably, 
once in the in-group, managers achieve most of the relevant benefits, particularly in the 
form of information and resource access. While tighter relationships with the boss may 
add value on the margin, it seems that the first cut is the deepest and is likely to have the 
largest effect on the manager's ability to act as a conduit of resources back to his or her 
subordinates. Yet, this cut or decision is likely to have a non-linear effect on the 
45 
demands placed upon the manager by the boss, increasingly the demands for reciprocity 
(Gouldner, 1960) fairly dramatically. Indeed, at high levels of LMX, individuals may 
even experience high levels of personal stress from the demands placed upon them from 
above (Harris & Kacmar, 2006). 
Perceived status and ascribed identity mechanisms may also counteract each other 
similarly and combine to have a curvilinear effect as well. Undoubtedly, achieving high 
perceived status requires a minimum level of connection to others, but is also a function 
ofwhich others, as status is a function of whether the focal actor's ties are of high status 
as well (Bonacich, 1987; Sparrowe & Liden, 2001; Ibarra, 1993). With no connections, a 
manager's intra-organizational status suffers, but with excessive connections, the 
manager has invested in relationships that may not confer additional benefit. And, as 
suggested above, the in-group out-group distinction with the boss implied in LMX theory 
is more likely to be viewed by subordinates as closer to binary. Hence, incremental 
investment in the boss relationship will not likely enhance the lieutenant manager's 
perceived status. Conversely, a manager generally starts with a minimum level of 
identification with his or her work group. If nothing else, the manager's performance is 
usually a function of the performance of the people underneath him or her (Mintzberg, 
1973; Mehra et al., 2006), and thus, the manager's identity and reputation likely begin at 
least partially tied to the subordinate group. In contrast, alternative groups, such as peers 
or management teams, may not immediately appear as salient (Hamilton, 2007) as a 
moderate level of external interaction is presumed in the manager's role (Mintzberg, 
1973). As a result, subordinates will accept managers' external networks as taken for 
46 
granted, up to a certain level, but will develop assessments of both the salience of 
alternative groups and the managers' membership in those groups at high levels of 
network centrality. Similarly, I suggest that relationship building with the boss is 
expected up to a level and enhances perceptions of status. Beyond the level of achieving 
"in-group" status with the boss, additional efforts on the part of the manager are more 
likely to distance the manager from the subordinate group, eroding the manager's 
ascribed work group identity than to confer additional status on the manager. 
In summary, then, I expect that the benefits of external relationships are more 
pronounced at lower levels of network centrality and that the costs of external 
relationships are more pronounced at higher levels of network presence. Thus: 
Hypothesis 14: There is a curvilinear relationship between the extensiveness 
of a manager's external network and the performance of his or her 
subordinates. Subordinate performance will be highest at moderate 
levels of external managerial networks and lower at low or high levels of 
manager external networks. 
Hypothesis 15: There is a curvilinear relationship between the extensiveness 
of a manager's external network and the organizational commitment of 
his or her subordinates. Subordinate organizational commitment will be 
highest at moderate levels of external managerial networks and lower at 
low or high levels of manager external networks. 
Hypothesis 16: There is a curvilinear relationship between the extensiveness 
of a manager's external network and the LMX relationship between 
47 
manager and subordinate. LMX will be highest at moderate levels of 
external managerial networks and lower at low or high levels of manager 
external networks. 
III.6 Differences between boss and peer relationships 
The theory developed thus far makes no distinction between the effects of boss 
relationships and the effects of peer relationships. However differences in the stakes and 
stature of a boss relationship and the potential reach and breadth of a network of peer 
relationships suggest that in several instances the two relationships will have 
meaningfully different effect sizes. In this section, I highlight these differences. Where 
no discussion is offered, I theorize no difference between boss and peer relationships. 
Peer relationships differ from boss relationships in several fundamental ways. 
First, boss relationships are mandatory in that they are prescribed roles (Graen, 1976; 
Blau, 1962) and they are unequal in terms of power (Krackhardt, 1990) and demand for 
equality or reciprocity (Gouldner, 1960). Second, peer relationships are more numerous 
and result in more frequent interactions and opportunities for exchange on a regular basis 
(Chiaburu & Harrison, 2008; Ferris & Mitchell, 1987; Mintzberg, 1973). Managers will 
have more opportunities to reap the rewards of external peer relationships and 
subordinates have more opportunities to observe these relationships and experience their 
impact. This quantitative difference for peer relationships should have two effects. First, 
peer relationships should create far more variability in managers' time requirements and 
accessibility. When managers invest in building and maintaining large numbers of peer 
48 
relationships, the sum effect should be to increase the managers' time constraints and 
reduce accessibility. In contrast, although a high quality boss relationship will take 
incremental time to manage, it represents only one relationship. Thus, 
Hypothesis 17: The number of a manager's peer relationships will have a 
larger effect on manager's accessibility than will the nature of their boss 
relationships. 
The numerical nature of peer relationships should also have an impact on 
manager's ascribed identities. Managers' significant relationships with peers send a 
signal to subordinates regarding the managers' affinities (Ashforth & Humphrey, 1995). 
Unlike demographic or other social groups, organizational and subunit membership are 
fuzzier and open to interpretation (Ashforth & Mael, 1989). As such, the existence of 
discretionary connections with peers (Blau, 1962) and the time invested to maintain them 
(Burt, 2001) provide subordinates with information as to the degree of manager 
"membership" in a group distinct from their subordinate work group (Ashforth & Mael, 
1989). While boss relationships may certainly send the same signals, the unitary and 
non-discretionary nature of this relationship makes these signals less frequent and 
weaker. 
Hypothesis 18: The number of managers' peer relationships will have a 
larger impact on their ascribed identities than will the strength of their 
boss relationships. 
Finally, bosses and peers transmit different types of resources and information to 
managers. Peers occupy the same or similar roles or roles that are interdependent with 
49 
each other (Chiaburu & Harrison, 2008). As a result, managers are more likely to harvest 
task relevant information and know-how from peer relationships (Mehra et al., 2006) than 
from their boss. Subordinates who have access to enhanced task related information use 
that information to more productively execute their assigned responsibilities, turning in 
higher performance. In contrast, although extensive peer relationships will allow the 
manager to gain a broader perspective of the organization as a whole, peer organizational 
level information is fragmented (like the manager's), while the boss's, by virtue of his or 
her organization level, is likely to be more integrated (March & Simon, 1958). In 
addition, bosses, as managers of organizational identity and symbolism have greater 
incentive to provide their subordinate managers with information in an organized and 
impactful fashion (Pfeffer, 1981). Thus, bosses are more likely to be a powerful source 
of organizational level information. As such, the connection to the boss will have a 
greater impact on organizational commitment for subordinates. 
Hypothesis 19: The number of managers' peer relationships will have a 
larger impact on subordinate job performance than will the strength of 
their boss relationships. 
Hypothesis 20: The strength of the managers' boss relationships will have a 
larger impact on subordinate organizational commitment than will the 
number of their peer relationships. 
50 
SECTION IV: METHOD 
IV. 1 Empirical Setting 
I collected data for this study in three service organizations in the United States. 
As described below, three organizations were necessary in order to generate sufficient 
sample size for the analyses in the study. At the same time, by conducting the research in 
three different organizations, the results should be more generalizable. 
Organization A was the centralized customer service function of a large US 
consumer credit card lending organization. Organization A provided telephone and mail 
based customer service, problem resolution and fraud case management for consumer 
credit card holders. Customer service involved answering routine questions that 
customers had about their accounts, payment histories, credit lines, and interest rates and 
pricing. Problem resolution activities included investigating unidentified credit card 
charges, tracking of payments, and addressing issues involved with lost cards. For both 
customer service and problem resolution, the employees in this organization handled the 
more difficult questions and issues or those customers deemed by the company to be the 
most valuable. More standard questions or seemingly less valuable customers were 
handled by external companies under contract to Organization A. These companies were 
not part of the study. Fraud case management involved filing and investigating claims of 
fraudulent activity on the customers' accounts. In addition, portions of Organization A 
were engaged in sales activities to existing customers by way of inbound telephone calls. 
The company made offers for additional products in monthly statements and through 
email solicitations. Customers then called a toll free telephone number to talk to a 
51 
representative about these offers. There were approximately 900 employees in this 
organization who were located in one building in the mid-Atlantic United States. 
Organizations B and C were distinct business units of the same health care 
products distribution company. Organizations B and C were fully integrated business 
units that sold similar products but serviced different customer segments throughout the 
United States. The two organizations sold a wide variety of supplies to their customers 
ranging from low cost bulk tongue depressors to sophisticated in-office electronic 
medical testing equipment such as EKG machines. Organization B was the flagship brand 
of the parent company and sold to and serviced physicians' offices. Organization B had 
been in existence for 50 years and had approximately 1,800 employees. Organization C 
operated under a different brand name and had been recently acquired by the parent 
company. Organization C sold to and serviced clinics and extended care facilities and 
had approximately 1,200 employees. Staffs in these two organizations were organized in 
local branch offices throughout the United States. A branch office was responsible for 
sales and service within a specific mutually exclusive geographic territory. Organization 
B had 27 branches and Organization C had 12 branches. Although Organization B and 
Organization C might each have a branch in the same city or region, the branches were in 
different physical office locations and the staff in the different organizations did not work 
together. Branch staff performed three functions—sales, operations, and accounts 
receivable—all of which were included in this study. The sales function, populated 
primarily by sales representatives who called on customers directly, was responsible for 
opening new accounts with medical providers and calling on these accounts periodically 
52 
to demonstrate new products and to assist customers in optimizing their use of the 
company's offerings. Operations staff in the branch ensured that orders were received, 
processed, and ultimately delivered to the customer's satisfaction.6 These staff also 
fielded problem calls and interacted with the warehouse and delivery functions to ensure 
smooth service to the customer and to address service problems. Accounts receivable 
staff in the branches addressed issues of billing problems with customers and worked 
with customers whose statements were in arrears. 
IV.2 Pilot site 
In addition to gathering data in Organizations A, B, and C, I also gathered data in 
a fourth organization, Organization D. I gathered data in Organization D prior to A, B, 
and C using Organization D as a pre-test or pilot site. Data gathering in Organization D 
occurred approximately two (2) months prior to data gathering in Organizations A, B, 
and C. Organization D was a fully integrated company that provided online employment 
verification and other screening services to companies who were in the process of hiring 
new employees. The company was a subsidiary of a larger, publicly traded corporation. 
There were 115 employees in Organization D, from the President to the front line 
employees, all of whom were invited to participate in the pilot study. 
I conducted the pilot study for two reasons. First, I had estimated that the first 
manager survey of my study, which included the peer networks, would require 
approximately 40 minutes to complete and would require participants to complete a 
6 The physical delivery of products was handled by the Transportation and Warehouse functions which 
were not part of this study. 
53 
network roster survey with as many as 120 potential network ties on it. I used the pilot 
study to confirm that respondents would (a) respond to the survey and (b) would 
complete it without survey fatigue. The results from the pilot site were encouraging on 
these issues. 83 of 115 members of Organization D completed the survey (72% response 
rate). In follow up discussions with members of Organization D, I made slight revisions 
to the wording of the recruitment email for my subsequent studies and to the order and 
structure of the subsequent surveys. 
The second purpose for engaging the pilot site, Organization D, was to use it as a 
hold out sample to select items for several of the factors of the intervening variables in 
the model, perceived manager status, ascribed identity and manager accessibility . I 
used the participants in Organization D (and select participants in Organizations A, B, 
and C) as a separate sample on which to conduct an exploratory factor analysis (EFA) in 
developing these three scales. The process and results of these analyses are described 
later in this methods section. 
IV.3 Participants and Sample 
In each organization there were three groups of study participants—the focal front 
line managers (referred to as managers in this study), the managers' subordinates who 
were always front line workers without managerial responsibilities, and the front line 
managers' direct superiors, referred to here as bosses. I asked all managers in 
Organization A and in the branches of Organization A and B to participate in the study. 
7 As described in section IV.5, the items in manager accessibility ultimately reflected two factors or latent 
variables, manager availability and manager time constraints. 
54 
In Organizations B and C, I asked all subordinates of the focal managers to participate in 
the study. In Organization A, the sponsors of the study asked that I randomly sample 
eight (8) subordinates of each focal manager. Table 1 displays a summary for each 
participant group for each organization. The table displays the number of staff invited to 
participate, the number of respondents to each survey, and the number of participants 
whose data I ultimately used for the analyses described below. The samples for the 
analyses reflect complete groups of manager, boss, and at least two subordinates 
participating in the relevant time periods. 
As reflected in Table 1, response rates for managers and bosses were quite strong. 
In particular manager and boss response rates for survey 1, in which I gathered 
sociometric network data, all approached or exceeded 80%. Researchers view 80% 
response as a minimum threshold for network data in order to achieve an accurate view 
of network level dynamics (Wasserman & Faust, 1994). I achieved these response rates 
partially through offering each network participant a post study report summarizing his or 
her personal network. As reflected in Table 1, response rates for subordinate employees 
were 32% for survey 1 and 47% for survey 2. While the survey 2 response rate is similar 
to those reported in recent meta-analyses, the survey 1 response rate is approximately one 
standard deviation below reported mean experience in recent meta-analyses (Baruch & 
Holtom, 2006). 
IV. 3. a Assessment of Non-Response Bias 
8 Of 48 focal managers, 3 had fewer than 8 employees. For these managers, all of their subordinates were 
invited to participate in the study. 
55 
Given the subordinate response rates, I used several of Rogelberg & Stanton's 
(2007) methods to assess the potential for non-response bias. Two sets of data allowed 
me to compare respondents to non-respondents. As described below, at Time 2,1 asked 
managers in Organizations A, B, and C to rate the performance of each of their 
subordinate employees on a scale of l=low to 5 = very high. Managers completed these 
data for any of their subordinates without knowledge of who did and did not participate 
in the study. A T-test comparing the two populations revealed no significant difference 
between responders and non-responders (manager rated performance difference = .07 out 
of 5.0,p = .20). As subordinates are nested within managers, I also constructed a two 
level random intercept model with manager rated performance as the dependent variable 
and subordinate participation as a binary independent variable. The fixed effect 
parameter for subordinate participation was not significant (b = .10, p = .074). Thus, 
respondents and non-respondents did not significantly differ in their task performance. 
Organizations B and C provided me with demographic information for all invited 
study participants. These data included the employee's age, gender, ethnicity, and tenure 
with the company. T-tests revealed no differences in age (difference = .66 years,/? = .36) 
or tenure (difference = .57 years,/? = .18) between respondents and non-respondents. I 
constructed logit models to assess the degree to which responsiveness was correlated with 
gender or ethnicity. The analyses revealed that respondents did not differ from non-
respondents on the basis of age, gender (b = .0062, p = .96), but that white employees 
were less likely to respond and participate than non-white employees (b= -.37, p = .04). 
56 
In general, then, archival analysis within Organizations B and C suggest very limited 
response bias. 
Finally, Organization D, the pilot site, serves as a benchmark for response bias. 
All employees (including front line subordinates) in Organization D were invited to 
participate in the network portion of the study as well and were offered a feedback report 
summarizing their personal networks. As such, subordinate response in Organization D 
was higher, i.e. 62/95 or 65%. As Organization D is not part of the sample for the 
analyses reported in this study, it serves as an external benchmark. In addition, the higher 
response in Organization D provides a benchmark of a research site less susceptible to 
response bias. Using T-tests, I compared subordinate assessments of 8 key study 
variables, as described below, between Organization D and the other sites in the study. 
Table 2 displays the results of these T-tests, highlighting no significant differences 
between the pilot sample and the study sample on seven of eight constructs. 
The three sets of analyses described above suggest that there was limited response 
bias in the subordinate participants in organizations A, B, and C. 
IV.4 Data Collection and Survey Approach 
The bulk of the data for the study were gathered through online surveys, with the 
remainder gathered through archival methods. I asked the managers and subordinates to 
complete two surveys, separated in time by two months. The bosses were only asked to 
9 The initial proposal for this dissertation suggested three surveys in order separate independent variables, 
intervening variables, and dependent variables in time. This approach would provide some insulation from 
common source bias (Podsakoff, MacKenzie, Lee, & Podsakoff, 2003). After consulting with staff at each 
research site, I determined that three time periods presented risks of survey fatigue and response

complete survey 1 at the beginning of the study. Table 3 provides a summary of the data 
that were gathered in each survey from each participant group. 
Approximately one week prior to the beginning of the survey period, the senior 
sponsor of my study in each organization sent an email to all potential participants. This 
email articulated the goals of the study from both the researchers' and the organizations' 
point of view and clearly stated that participation was voluntary and confidential, though 
not anonymous. For all participants, this email stated that any participant would receive a 
report detailing the participant's responses to the survey and how those responses 
compared to the aggregates of his or her organization. For those participants asked to 
participate in the network portion of the study, this email also stated that these 
participants would receive a graphical picture of their personal network within the 
organization and some guidelines for how to interpret the same. For all participants, the 
email suggested that the two surveys together would require up to 60 minutes of their 
time to complete. 
The survey period in each organization for each survey was approximately three 
(3) weeks.10 On day 1 of the survey period, potential participants received an email from 
the researcher's email address outlining the study and the logistics for participating. This 
email reiterated that participation was voluntary and confidential and the offer of a report 
at the end of the study. At the beginning of the email, participants found a link that, 
degradation. I shifted the design to two time periods. While this approach maintained a separation in time 
for each respondent group that provided multiple variable types, it did require gathering transformational 
leadership, a control variable, in the same survey (timel) as the intervening variables. In order to provide 
additional insulation against common source bias, I included the Big Five Personality Inventory for 
subordinates (Goldberg, 1992). 
10 Survey periods differed slightly from one organization to the next due to the timing of holidays and of

when clicked, took them to the beginning of the survey. The surveys were customized to 
the three participant groups within each organization. Thus, in survey 1 there were six 
versions, and in survey 2 there were four versions. On the first page of the survey, 
participants were presented with the informed consent form and were asked to check the 
box at the bottom affirming their consent to be included in the study. This page and 
question appeared on both survey 1 and 2. Upon clicking on informed consent, 
participants went to the first page of the survey from which they selected their name from 
a drop down menu. From there the actual survey questions began. Survey 1 for network 
participants took about 40 minutes to complete. Survey 1 for non-network participants 
took about 20 minutes. Survey 2 required about 20 minutes for all participants. 
IV.5 Variables and Measures 
IV. 5. a Independent Variables 
The theory and hypotheses for the study contemplate two types of a manager's 
external relationships: Interpersonal ties to the manager's horizontal peers and the 
specific vertical relationship with the manager's boss. As a result, there were two sets of 
independent variables in the study. The first set captured the number of external peer 
relationships each manager had within his or her organization. The second set captured 
the nature of the specific relationship the manager had with his or her boss. 
59 
Peer networks. At Time 1, all managers completed two sociomatrices. In each 
case, on the computer screen, I provided the participant a list of all organization members 
within the hierarchical levels of interest within the organization—the front line 
managers and the level that included their bosses. Network researchers often 
differentiate network ties or relationships on the basis of whether they are instrumental or 
affective. Instrumental ties are those through which resources related to executing one's 
work, job, or tasks are passed (Lincoln and Freeman, 1979). In contrast, affective ties are 
relationships through which socio-emotional support is passed. Following Klein et al. 
(2004) and Baldwin et al. (1997), I gathered data on advice (i.e. instrumental) and 
friendship (i.e. affective) ties. For each network, I used wording adapted from Baldwin et 
al. (1997) and Klein et al. (2004). To construct the advice network, for each name 
presented to a participant, I instructed him or her to "please check the box if this person is 
someone you go to for work related or technical advice." After completing the advice 
network, participants completed a similar sociomatrix for the friendship network. Here, I 
instructed the participant to "please check the box if this person is someone you consider 
a friend, someone you would socialize with outside of work."1314 The full roster 
In order to facilitate the investigation of other research questions, bosses also completed the sociomatrix. 
Additional insights from these data are discussed in the discussion section. 
121 included bosses on the roster in order to explore research questions beyond the initial scope of this 
dissertation. 
lj I also gathered valued ties for both advice and friendship networks. When a participant checked the box 
indicating a tie, he or she was then asked to assess the intensity of the relationship on a 4 point scale. The 
analyses using the valued data return the same results as those with the binary data, so I present the binary 
data here for parsimony. 
14 The initial proposal for this dissertation contemplated a slightly different implementation of the network 
questions. Rather than the two step approach outlined here, the original proposal contemplated one 
question per roster name per network in which the respondent would evaluate the nature of the relationship 
with the alter on a five point scale. I chose the approach here in order to minimize survey fatigue in the 
face of rosters with as many as 120 names. 
60 
approach used here avoids the biases introduced by capping the number of potential 
nominations (Mehra et al., 2001) and it aids in participant recall (Perry-Smith, 2006). At 
the same time the check box approach I employed streamlined the number of responses 
each participant needed to provide, minimizing respondent fatigue. This approach 
generated 6 matrices, 2 each (advice and friendship) for the three organizations. Figures 
2-7 depict each of these six networks among the managerial peers in each organization. 
Table 4 provides a summary of the structure of these networks. 
I constructed two independent variables from these sociomatrices. Both are 
counts of the number of peer organization members who identify the focal manager as a 
tie, i.e. in-degree centrality (Freeman, 1979). In-degree centrality, an aggregation of the 
responses of all other network participants, avoids the limitations of self report data 
(Sparrowe et al., 2001). I calculated in-degree centrality for each of the two networks in 
each of the three organizations, normalizing within each network to account for 
differences in network size (Borgatti, Everett & Freeman, 2002). Advice in-degree is the 
number of a manager's peers who identify him or her as a source of work related advice. 
Friendship in-degree is the number of a manager's peers who identify him or her as a 
friend. 
Relationship with the boss (LLX). To capture the nature of the manager's 
relationships with his or her direct superior, the boss, I used Liden & Maslyn's (1986) 
LMX-MDM scales. The LMX-MDM is drawn from the Leader Member Exchange 
(LMX) literature, a literature whose focus is the measurement and assessment of the 
dyadic relationships between managers and their subordinate employees. In order to 
61 
maintain clarity of the relationships and organization levels in this study, I follow 
Tangirala et al. (2007) and Venkataramani et al. (2010) and refer to the relationship 
between a manager and his or her boss as Leader Leader Exchange or LLX. The 
relationship between a manager and his or her subordinate measured along the same 
dimensions retains the label Leader Member Exchange (LMX). 
The LMX-MDM is a 12-item set of scales which is organized into four three-item 
factors or subscales. The four factors are: (a) Affect, the degree to which there is 
affection and friendship between the boss and the manager, (b) Loyalty, the extent to 
which the respondent believes that his or her partner in the relationship would support 
and defend him or her to others throughout the organization, (c) Contribution, the extent 
to which the manager would expend extra effort in order to deliver results to the boss, 
and (d) Respect, the extent to which the respondent looks favorably upon the target's job 
related skills and accomplishments. Although the LMX-MDM has been developed more 
recently than other more established LMX measures, it has two distinct advantages in this 
study. First, recent studies establish that the LMX-MDM has incremental explanatory 
power as an independent variable above other scales (Greguras & Ford, 2006). Second, 
the distinct dimensions of the LMX-MDM allow the researcher to differentiate two 
different interpretations of the relationship between a supervisor and his or her 
subordinate. On the one hand, the relationship may be thought of as one of social 
exchange (Sparrowe & Liden, 1997) in which the two parties engage in providing effort 
and favors on each other's behalf. This, to some degree, represents an instrumental 
flavor to the relationship. On the other hand, the supervisor/subordinate relationship may 
62 
be more affective in nature, rooted in mutual liking and trust (Graen & Uhl-bien, 1995). 
The specific items for the four factors are detailed in Appendix A. 
In the LMX literature, the evaluation of LMX is most often performed by the 
subordinate in the relationship. However, in a few instances (e.g. Sparrowe, Soetjipto, & 
Kraimer, 2006; Greguras & Ford, 2006), the superior in the dyadic relationship evaluates 
the relationship as well. For this study, I chose to have LLX assessed by the boss. There 
are two rationales for this decision. First, the boss perspective on LLX mirrors the in-
degree approach on horizontal networks, providing a view of the relationship from an 
external party. Second, as the manager is the focal player in this study, the boss 
assessment of LLX somewhat insulates the analyses from hidden variable problems and 
common source bias. To the 12 items in the LMX-MDM, I added two additional items 
designed to mirror the items in the peer network sociomatrices. I asked the boss to assess 
the degree to which (a) "you view the employee as a friend, someone you would socialize 
with outside of work," and (b) "you view the employee as a source of work or technical 
advice." Participants assessed all 14 items on a 5 point Likert scale where 
l=significantly disagree and 5 = significantly agree. 
Prior to engaging in the analyses for the study, I confirmed that the four factors of 
the LMX-MDM were distinct and reliable measures. I conducted a confirmatory factor 
analysis15 to confirm that the items were best represented by the four factors suggested by 
prior research. Table 5 displays the results of this CFA. The sample for these studies 
was all bosses or managers in Organizations A, B, and C who were in the sample for the 
15 All confirmatory factor analyses were conducted using MPlus, Version 6. 
63 
analyses for the study. In Table 5, model 1, or the "full model" captures each of the four 
factors as separate factors. Models 2-7 constrain the full model by collapsing specific 
pairs of factors to one. Model 8 models LMX-MDM as one factor. As reflected in Table 
5, the full model fit the data well %2 (48, N=66) = 103, (Comparative Fit Index = .96, 
Root Mean Square Error of Approximation = .086, Standardized Root Mean Square 
Residual = .041). I also confirmed that each of the four factors displayed sufficient 
internal reliability. Cronbach's alphas for the constructs ultimately used in the analysis 
are displayed on the diagonal of Table 8. 
As a final step, I evaluated the pairwise correlations among these four constructs. 
As reflected in the note on Table 5, there are modest to strong inter-correlations among 
these variables. Throughout this study, I constrain study variables to share no more than 
50% variance or a Pearson correlation of .707 or less. As displayed in the note on Table 
5, Affect and Loyalty have a pairwise correlation of .73, and Contribution and Respect 
have a pairwise correlation of .75. I therefore removed Loyalty and Respect from the 
analysis. The two LLX variables, then, are LLX-Affect and LLX-Contribution.16 Affect 
and Contribution remain moderately correlated, r=.64. While this correlation is still 
moderately high, I checked for multicollinearity using VIF tests and found none. 
TV. 5. b Intervening Variables: Network Benefits and Costs 
161 also captured two single item measures between manager and boss designed to mirror the friendship 
and advice network questions on the sociomatrices. These items were measured on the same 5 point Likert 
scales as the remainder of the LMX-MDM items. The friendship proxy (analogous to friendship in-degree 
centrality among peers) had a .68 correlation with the three item LLX-Affect scale. The advice proxy 
(analogous to advice in-degree centrality among peers) had a .75 correlation with the three item LLX-
Respect factor. 
64 
The theoretical model identifies two benefits of managers' external relationships 
(information/resource access and perceived manager status) and two potential costs 
(ascribed identity and manager accessibility). I used existing scales and items to measure 
the network benefits, but adapted and developed new scales to measure the two costs. I 
discuss the scales for the network benefits first below, follow that with a discussion of the 
network costs, and follow that with a confirmatory factor analysis across all four 
constructs. The items for all of these scales may be found in Appendix A. 
Resource and Information Access. At Time 1, subordinates completed a 6-item 
access to resources and information scale from Spreitzer (1996). Sample items include "I 
have access to the strategic information I need to do my job well" and "I have access to 
the resources I need to do my job well." Spreitzer's (1996) work suggests that these 6 
items represent two distinct 3-item factors, one representing information access and one 
representing resource access. I subjected the items to a confirmatory factor analysis, 
discussed below, and confirmed that they represent two distinct factors in my study as 
well. Cronbach's alphas for all individual/subordinate level constructs may be found on 
the diagonal of table 8. 
Perceived Manager Status. At Time 1, employees completed a \2-item perceived 
manager status scale adapted from Eisenberger et al. (2002). Sample items include "The 
organization holds my supervisor in high regard" and "My supervisor influences 
decisions made by upper management." I reduced the number of items in this scale to 6 
by (a) conducting an exploratory factor analysis and (b) identifying those items that had 
the most face validity with the theory constructed earlier, namely that subordinates 
65 
perceive their managers as having the ability to participate in decision making, the ability 
to influence superiors, and the autonomy to make their own decisions . I conducted an 
exploratory factor analysis using a sample of 107 study participants who would not be 
part of the subject pool for the core study. These 107 participants represent 65 front line 
employees in Organization D, the pilot site, and 42 employees in Organizations A, B, and 
C who were the only employees to participate for those given managers18. A promax 
oblique rotation yielded a two factor model with six times loading above .6 on the first 
factor. I used these six items, listed in Appendix A, for the perceived manager status 
scale. 
Ascribed Identity. Under social identity theory (SIT), people make assessments 
regarding the degree to which they and others seem like members of particular 
identifiable groups. In particular, members of a group make determinations as to whether 
they and others seem like in-group members or alternatively out-group members. 
Colloquially, members assess the degree to which an individual is "one of us" or "one of 
them." Individuals use other members' social identity markers—for example, job 
function, demographics, attitudes, and behaviors—to determine the degree to which they 
perceive them to be prototypical—or members—of the in-group, or, alternatively, 
prototypical members of salient out-groups. The degree of perceived membership in one 
group or another is an intervening variable that SIT suggests predicts a number of 
17 All exploratory factor analyses were conducted using Proc Factor in SAS v. 9.2. 
18 The core analyses for the main study in this dissertation use a multi-level modeling framework with 
subordinate employees nested within managers. In this framework, there must be at least two level-1 data 
points nested within each level-2 group (Gelman & Hill, 2007). These 42 individuals were the only 
subordinate employees to participate in the study for these managers making them unsuitable for a multi­
level framework. 
66 
individual level outcomes including member influence, emergent leadership, and in-
group bias (Hogg, 2001). However, while SIT researchers measure or manipulate proto-
typicality and often measure perceived proto-typicality, they don't directly measure 
perceptions of membership or "oneness." In this study, I directly measure this oneness 
and define ascribed identity as the degree to which an in-group member perceives 
another as being "one" with the in-group. 
To measure ascribed identity, I developed 16 potential items which might 
represent this construct. Seven of the items were adapted from measures of group and 
organizational identification by Mael & Ashforth (1992) and Hogg et al. (1998). In the 
original literature, these items measured an individual's self identification with a focal 
group or organization. The adaptation I use in this study captures the extent to which 
others identify the target individual with the target group. To these seven, I added nine 
additional items that reflected perceived similarity or which asked directly the extent to 
which a subordinate identifies the manager with the work group. Sample items are: "I 
identify him or her with [the target group]" and "when I think of [target group], I think of 
my supervisor as one of the members." Subordinates completed these items at time 1, 
evaluating their managers on a l=significantly disagree to 5=significantly agree Likert 
scale. My intention upon developing these items was to select a subset of items to 
represent ascribed identity based on factor analysis (for data reduction) and face validity. 
Using the 107 participant hold out sample described above, I conducted an exploratory 
factor analysis using an oblique promax rotation. This analysis yielded a four factor 
model of which the first factor was four items that captured the essence of being 
67 
perceived as "one of the group." These four items, listed in Appendix A, constitute the 
ascribed identity measure in this study. 
Manager accessibility. I developed 11 items to reflect the degree to which 
subordinates perceived their managers as available and accessible to them. Many of 
these items were adapted from Jansenn's (2001) time constraint scale and some were 
written new for this study. Conceptually, the items should cover two distinct factors: 
general physical availability of the manager and perceptions of the manager's physical 
time constraints. Subordinates completed these items at time 1, evaluating their 
managers on a l=significantly disagree to 5=significantly agree Likert scale. Using the 
same 107 participant hold out sample described above, I conducted an exploratory factor 
analysis using an oblique promax rotation. This EFA yielded the two factors, Manager 
Availability and Manager Time Constraints, as hypothesized and I retained them both in 
the analysis that follows. The items are listed in Appendix A. 
Confirmatory Factor Analysis.19 I subjected all of the intervening variable 
constructs to a confirmatory factor analysis. For this analysis, I used all employees from 
Organizations A, B, and C who completed survey 1 and who were not in the 42 single 
employee to manager dyads referenced above. This sample then was distinct from the 
sample for the EFA and represented the pool from which subjects were drawn for the 
19 Approximately 10% of the records in the subordinate level data sets have items that represent data 
missing completely at random. In order to account for missing data while not resorting to listwise deletion, 
and following Schafer & Graham (2002), I employed multiple imputation procedures. I implemented the 
imputation procedures in SAS 9.2 using Proc MI and Proc MIAnalyze. In particular, all the confirmatory 
factor analyses and multi-level models fit in this study used 5 imputations with results aggregated across 
these 5. I replicated the core multi-level analyses in the study using listwise deletion as well. As expected,

core analyses of the study. There were 427 observations in this analysis. The results of 
this CFA are displayed in Table 6. Following Spreitzer's findings and the exploratory 
factor analyses described above, I modeled the full model, model 1, with six factors: 
resource access, information access, perceived manager status, ascribed identity, manager 
availability and time constraints. Model 2 implements a constrained model in which 
resource access and information access are constrained to one factor. Model 3 constrains 
manager availability and time constraints to one factor. Model 4 constrains manager 
status and ascribed identity to one factor. Model 5 constrains manager status and 
information access to one factor. Model 6 constrains manager status and resource access 
to one factor. Model 7 constrains all items to one factor. Models 2-6 nest within model 1 
and model 7 nests within any of models 2-6. As displayed in table 6, the full 6 factor 
model has acceptable fit, %2 (284, N=427) = 864, (Comparative Fit Index = .91, Root 
Mean Square Error of Approximation = .063, Standardized Root Mean Square Residual = 
.070). And, in comparison to the rest of the models in Table 6, the full model exhibits the 
best model fit with all other models having substantially higher degrees of freedom 
adjusted %2 and RMSEA, and lower CFI and TLI. Thus, for the analyses that follow, I 
use each of the six factors individually. 
Table 8 displays the means, standard deviations, and intercorrelations for the 
subordinate level variables in the study. Cronbach's alpha is located on the diagonal. As 
Table 8 suggests, each of the six benefit or cost variables has an alpha greater than .7. 
TV. 5. c Dependent Variables 
69 
Organizational Commitment. At Time 2, subordinates completed Allen & 
Meyer's (1990) eight-item Affective Organizational Commitment Scale. The scale 
includes items such as "I really feel as if [organization name]'s problems are my own" 
and "[Organization Name] has a great deal of personal meaning for me." Subordinates 
evaluated the items using 5 point Likert scale, ranging from "1"= "strongly disagree" to 
"5" = strongly agree." The full scale can be found in Appendix A. As noted in the 
appendix, I reversed coded four of the eight items. 
Subordinate Task Performance. I measured subordinate task performance using 
two sources. First, at Time 2,1 asked the managers in the study to evaluate their specific 
subordinate employees. The managers completed a three item scale as listed in Appendix 
A. Although many studies have developed manager assessments of subordinate 
performance, I chose to use these three custom items for two reasons. First, in some 
instances, managers were asked to evaluate large numbers of subordinates, requiring a 
short instrument. Second, in order to generalize across 3 sites with potentially different 
cultures and metrics, this study required a reasonably generic approach. 
The second approach to measuring performance involved gathering objective 
performance data for a subset of the participants in the study. Objective archival 
performance measures were available for a specific job category within two of the 
organizations. A portion of the participants in Organizations B and C were front line 
salespeople who sold products directly to physicians, nursing homes and extended care 
facilities. Their compensation was primarily commission driven and, as such, individual 
sales performance was the primary performance metric for these two sub-organizations. 
70 
Specifically, the salespeople were compensated on a formula driven by the gross margin 
(revenue less cost of goods sold) that their customers generated. At the beginning of the 
sales year, the salesperson, his or her sales manager, and the sales manager's boss 
negotiated a baseline or target gross margin level for the upcoming year. Gross margin 
above and beyond the baseline would garner a higher commission, and profit below the 
baseline would garner a lower commission. Sales managers' bonuses were contingent on 
the overall gross margin performance of their sales people. Since each salesperson was 
assigned to a particular geographic sales territory, making sales person performance 
independent from one another, sales managers grew their personal performance by 
maximizing the individual performance of their sales people. The organization provided 
the baseline and actual gross margin generated for an annual period that coincided with 
the end of survey 2. The two organizations sold different mixes of products (given their 
different customer segments) yielding different averages of profit generated per 
salesperson. Therefore, I normalized each data set by dividing by the average profit 
generated in each organization. Actual sales and Baseline sales, then, are ratios which 
are centered at 1. 
Leader Member Exchange (LMX). As with the independent variables, I used 
Liden & Maslyn's (1998) LMX-MDM scale to measure LMX between managers and 
their subordinates. Although I measured LMX from both the managers' and 
subordinates' perspectives, the factor structure and internal reliability of LMX from the 
manager's perspective was not as strong as desired. Thus, I chose to evaluate LMX from 
71 
the subordinate's perspective. I evaluated LMX as an integrated construct. Pno? 
research suggests that the four dimension of the LMX-MDM may represent a secom, 
•vjcr factor of LiviX as a whole (Sparrowe et ai.. 2006). A confirmatory factor analysis 
of these data suggest that this factor structure is a good model fit, %2 (50, N=427) = 265, 
(Comparative Fit Index = .96, Root Mean Square Error of Approximation = .077, 
Standardized Root Mean Square Residual = .037). This measure has high internal 
reliability, (Table 8, alpha = .90), and post hoc analyses suggest that the analyses with the 
separate factors do not yield meaningfully different results. 
Confirmatory Factor Analysis. Table 7 displays the results for subordinate 
assessed dependent variables. The full model has 2 main factors: organizational 
commitment and LMX, where LMX has the four sub-factors of Affect, Loyalty, 
Contribution, and Respect. Models 2 tests the incremental value of the second order 
factor. Models 3-9 test various combinations of constraining factors and Model 10 
models the dependent variables as one. As table 7 displays, the full model with the 
second order LMX factor fits the data well y2 (df= 165, N=427) = 703, (Comparative Fit 
Index = .94, Root Mean Square Error of Approximation = .067, Standardized Root Mean 
Square Residual = .045). Model 2 suggests that the model works with or without the 
second order factor, confirming the adequacy of the integrated scale. 
IV. 5.d Control Variables 
Subordinate level controls 
201 did evaluate the stage 2 and direct effects models using LMX as reported by the manager. These 
models displayed a similar pattern of results to those reported here. 
72 
Demographics. I included control variables for the subordinates' age, gender, 
race, education, tenure with the organization, and tenure with the current manager. 
Subordinates supplied these data via survey at Time 1. Age is measured in years. Gender 
is a categorical variable with l=male and 2=female. The populations in the three sites 
were predominantly white, i.e. 85% of respondents were white. Therefore, race is a 
categorical variable where l=white and 2=non-white. Respondents recorded their level 
of education from among six choices: 1- some high school, 2- high school graduate, 3= 
some college, 4=college graduate, 5=some graduate school, 6=graduate degree. 
Participants also recorded the length of time they had worked in the organization, i.e. 
company tenure, in years. I provided participants with a set of options in one year 
increments as well as four shorter buckets—0-3 months, 3-6 months, 6-9 months, and 9-
12 months. I converted the answers in the smaller buckets to the midpoints in years. For 
example, I coded 0-3 months as .125 years. Using a similar methodology, participants 
recorded the length of time they had worked for the current and focal manager, i.e. 
manager tenure. 
Personality. Prior research documents that individual differences in trait 
personality shapes the individuals' job satisfaction (Judge, Heller, & Mount, 2002), 
organizational commitment (Erdheim, Wang, & Zichar, 2006) and task performance 
(Barrick & Mount, 1991). Thus, I chose to control for individual subordinate personality 
in order to isolate the effects of managers' external relationships on perceived benefits 
and costs and to isolate the effects of the benefits and costs on the dependent variables of 
organizational commitment, LMX, and task performance. In addition, by including 
73 
personality as a control, I account for some potential common source bias in both stage 1 
and stage 2 analyses (Tangirala et al., 2007). At Time 1, participants completed the IPIP 
version (Goldberg, 1992, http://ipip.ori.org) of the Big Five Personality Inventory which 
measures general personality constructs of extroversion, conscientiousness, 
agreeableness, emotional stability, and openness to experience. Each of these constructs 
is measured using 10 items and respondents evaluated themselves on a 5 point Likert 
scale (l="strongly disagree" to 5="strongly agree"). 
Manager level controls 
Demographics. Similar to subordinates, at time 1, managers provided their 
demographic information—age, gender, race, education, and company tenure. These 
variables are measured and coded using the same methodology outlined for subordinates 
above. 
Transformational Leadership. I included the manager's display of 
transformational leadership as a control variable. This is a group or manager level 
control reflecting the extent to which the manager displays transformational leadership 
behaviors. At Time 1, subordinates assessed their managers' display of transformational 
leadership using the 16 items in the inspirational motivation, idealized influence 
attribution, idealized influence behavior, and intellectual stimulation scales of the Multi-
Factor Leadership Questionnaire (Bass & Avolio, 1995) . To arrive at a group level 
21 Measures of Transformational Leadership may also include the individual consideration scale of the 
MLQ. As described in the next footnote, I also explore the contribution of StogdilTs initiating structure 
and consideration factors. Individual Consideration (MLQ) and consideration (Stogdill, 1963) were likely 
to measure the same construct, so I chose not to include the MLQ items in the survey. 
74 
measurement of transformational leadership, I aggregated the scales across subordinates 
within a manager. In this sample, transformational leadership has a mean rwg(j) of .98 
(minimum = .69, maximum = .99, median = .98), suggesting agreement across members 
within a group and justifying aggregating to the manager level (Klein & Kozlowski, 
2000). Transformational leadership has an ICC(l) of .28 (p < .0001) suggesting that 
variance exists between managers on this measure. 
In addition, I controlled for the manager's span of control as measured by his or 
her number of direct reporting employees (span of control). This control was necessary 
because prior research suggests that managerial spans can affect manager/subordinate 
relationships (Coglisler & Schriesheim, 2000) and manager effectiveness (Gittell, 2001). 
I gathered span of control from employee rosters provided by personnel in each 
participating organization. I also controlled for the )ob function of the manager and his or 
her group, allowing for two generic archetypes: sales (function =1) and all other 
(function = 2). Approximately 55% of the sample are sales organizations. Finally, to 
account for the three different organizations, I added dummy variables for Organization 
B and Organization C. 
IV.6 Analytical considerations 
IV. 6. a Nested data structure 
II At time 1, subordinates also evaluated their managers in terms of initiating structure—the extent to 
which the manager provides structure and direction to the subordinate's work, and consideration—the 
extent to which the manager attends to the subordinate's welfare and wellbeing (Stogdill, 1963), as other 
potential leadership style controls. Both were moderately to highly correlated with transformational 
leadership (r=.77 for consideration and r=.59 for initiating structure). Neither provided explanatory power 
above that of transformational leadership, so for simplicity, I include simply transformational leadership in 
the models. 
75 
In this study, the data have a nested structure with individual subordinates nested 
within managers. Constructs regarding the manager exist at the group level of analysis 
whereas constructs about subordinate employees exist at the individual level of analysis. 
In the first stage of the indirect effects model, the hypotheses are cross level in nature 
with manager level effects (for example, external relationships and transformational 
leadership) predicting individual level outcomes (for example, information access or 
perceived manager status). And, although the essence of the hypotheses in the second 
stage of the intervening effects model are individual level hypotheses, these analyses 
continue to control for manager level effects such as demographics, span of control and 
transformational leadership, thus rendering these models cross level as well. 
To account for the nested structure and the cross-level nature of the hypotheses, I 
used random coefficient modeling (RCM)23 in the analyses. RCM allows the researcher 
to test the relationships between group level independent variables and individual level 
dependent variables, while continuing to control for and model individual level factors. 
In particular, I modeled the hypotheses as random intercepts, allowing the absolute 
difference in outcome variables such as information access or organizational commitment 
to have a group or manager level component captured as a difference in intercepts at the 
individual level. This capability is critical because traditional individual-level analyses 
do not control for the nested structure of data and may misrepresent the between-group 
effects (Klein, Dansereau, & Hall, 1994). I grand mean centered the predictor variables, 
which provides computational efficiency and ease of interpretation (Gelman & Hill, 
23 All multilevel models were constructed in SAS 9.2 using Proc Mixed. 
76 
2007; Kreft, de Leeuw & Aiken, 1995). In addition, when implementing cross level 
models, grand mean centering allows the researcher to isolate the effects of group level 
predictors on individual level outcomes, adjusting for the effects of individual level 
predictors (Hofmann & Gavin, 1998). As noted earlier, I used a sample that included 
managers for whom at least two subordinates participated in the study. Multi-level 
analysis can be supported at group sizes as small as two (Gelman & Hill, 2007). The 
inclusion of groups as small as two grows the sample size at the manager or group level 
from 62 to 83. As noted by Hofmann (1997), variance and statistical power at the group 
level are critical for deriving empirical insights in multilevel analyses.24'25 Because 
intercepts may, and are predicted to, vary between managerial groups, all reported 
parameter estimates are unstandardized b's. 
IV. 6. b Homogeneity ofVariance/Covariance Matrices 
In order to achieve the maximum statistical power, particularly at the 
group/manager, I execute one analysis across the data in all three sites. Since I am 
aggregating across groups, I conducted Box's M test in order to assess the degree of 
similarity or homogeneity among the three covariance matrices. The test statistic is M= 
" The choice to include groups of 2 or more, while adding variance at the manager level, also raises issues 
about the validity of transformational leadership as a group level variable. To ensure that this choice was 
not material, I confirmed that the rwg, ICC1, and ICC2 for a two+ member group sample vs. a three+ 
member group sample were not meaningfully different. In addition, I ran a sample of the analytical models 
with three members per group and found few meaningful differences. 
25 The data have an additional level of nesting in that managers are nested within bosses. As the theory 
espoused functions between manager and subordinate, I report the results of two level models in this 
analysis. However, it is possible that there is some variance in intervening or dependent variables 
explained by differences at the boss level. In consideration of this, I did examine a series of three level 
multi-level models controlling for bosses through the use of random intercepts at this level. In no case did 
the results differ substantively between a two level and three level model. For parsimony, I report the two 
level results here. 
77 
131.47 at 95 df, p = .0029. Unequal group sizes may artificially inflate the power of 
Box's M test, so ap of < .001 is warranted for the homogeneity assumption to be 
violated. 
SECTION V: RESULTS 
V.l Correlation Tables 
Table 8 displays the means, standard deviations, and intercorrelations among the 
Level 1 or subordinate level variables in the analysis. Where relevant, internal reliability 
statistics, Cronbach's alpha, are displayed on the diagonal. Similarly, Table 9 displays 
the means, standard deviations, and intercorrelations among the Level 2 or manager level 
variables in the analysis, with Cronbach's alpha on the diagonal. 
Due to the cross level nature of the hypotheses for this study, examining the 
correlation tables does not provide an initial sense as to whether managers' external 
relationships do indeed shape subordinate perceptions and experience. However, an 
initial examination of the pairwise correlations does inform the study design and 
analytical strategy. In addition, it provides some initial insight into the validity of the 
stage 2 hypotheses, the extent to which the benefits and costs of managers' external 
relationships are related to subordinate attitudes, LMX, and performance. Table 8 reveals 
that moderate correlations exist between subordinates' personality and their ratings of 
their own attitudes and views of their managers. In particular, subordinate agreeableness 
displays consistent moderate correlations (e.g. r with information access of .23, r with 
organizational commitment of .21) as does emotional stability (r with information access 
78 
of. 13, r with manager time constraint of .24). These correlations confirm the benefit of 
controlling for subordinate personality, partially as an insulator from common source 
bias. 
Table 8 also reveals strong correlations between many of the intervening variables 
and the subordinate level outcomes. For example, the pairwise correlation between 
manager status and organizational commitment is .34 and the pairwise correlation 
between ascribed identity and LMX is .57. These strong pairwise correlations provide 
initial evidence for the connections in stage 2 of the theoretical model, linking the 
intervening effects of managers' external relationships to subordinate level attitudes, 
performance and LMX. 
Table 9 reveals that two of the external relationship variables, friendship in-
degree and LLX-contribution, are moderately correlated with transformational leadership, 
r=.33 and .30 respectively. This suggests that it is indeed a necessary strategy to control 
for transformational leadership to confirm that the external relationship variables are 
bringing incremental explanatory power to the models. 
Tables 8 and 9 also confirm the need for demographic control variables at various 
points in time. In particular, both tables display differences in the experiences, attitudes, 
and networks of respondents of different genders and race. Table 9 reveals that women 
and minority managers have more sparse peer networks and weaker relationships with 
their bosses. Similarly, Table 8 reveals that women and minority front line subordinates 
report less advantaged work conditions, e.g. resource and information access. 
79 
V.La Issues of Multi-collinearity 
Table 8 reveals several instances of strong correlations among the intervening 
variables. For example the correlation between resource access and information access is 
.59 and the correlation between manager availability and ascribed identity is .66. This 
suggests that multicollinearity exists among these variables. Multicollinearity can result 
in inflated standard errors (Cohen et al., 2003), thus creating problems in parameter 
estimation and interpretation. Similarly, Table 9 reveals issues of multicollinearity 
among the level 2 independent variables as well. For example, friendship in-degree and 
advice in-degree are correlated at r=.52 and LLX-affect and LLX-contribution are 
correlated at r=.64. These correlations may present either challenges or opportunities in 
analytical interpretation. On the one hand, multi-collinearity, while not affecting the 
goodness of fit of a model, can lead to inflated standard errors and instability in 
parameter estimates (Cohen et al., 2003). As I am interested in the effects of specific 
variables in my models, multicollinearity may present a problem. At the same time, 
correlations among independent variables may suggest that the independent variables act 
as suppressors of each other (Mackinnon et al., 2000). A suppression effect occurs in a 
statistical analysis when the inclusion of a third variable, z, in an analysis of the 
predictive effect of x on y accentuates the predictive relationship of x on y (Tzeglov & 
Henik, 1991). The suppression effect may occur because the correlation between z and x, 
i.e. two independent variables, may mask the effect of x on y or because the inclusion of 
z "cleans out" variance in x unrelated to y (Tzeglov & Henik, 1991). 
80 
To more formally explore the possibility of multicollinearity, I constructed OLS 
versions of the models used to test my hypotheses and examined the Variance Inflation 
Factors (VIF) between the variables in question. Although the VIFs between the highly 
correlated variables, e.g. between LLX-affect and LLX-contribution, were the highest in 
the models, none approached 5, a common threshold for concerns of multicollinearity. 
As such, I did not remove any variables from the analysis. 
In the tables that support the analysis for this study, I present the analyses in two 
forms. In the first form, referred to as single models, I enter explanatory variables one at 
a time to the models. This provides the opportunity to understand the effect of a given 
explanatory variable on the dependent variable of choice without the risk of 
multicollinearity. In the second portion of the tables, I enter all of the independent, but 
possibly correlated, variables in what I refer to as full models. To interpret the results, I 
rely primarily on the full models. These models represent a stricter test, in that a 
significant individual effect would need to account for variance after controlling for 
others and would need to overcome any lingering effects from multi-collinearity. The 
single models provide additional information and my aid in interpretation, but the full 
models are used for hypothesis testing. 
V.2 Organization of this section 
The theory and hypotheses presented earlier are organized by intervening 
variables, e.g. resource and information access, perceived manager status, ascribed 
identity, and manager accessibility. I maintain this structure in this section. In each 
81 
subsection that follows, I first examine stage 1—managers' external relationships as 
predictors of intervening variables, the benefits and costs of managers' external 
relationships. I then examine stage 2—interventing effects predicting subordinate 
outcomes. After examining the intervening effects, I examine the direct effects, both 
linear and curvilinear linking managers' external relationships to the chosen subordinate 
outcomes. 
Table 10 displays a summary of the key findings outlined below. Panel A 
displays the results for the stage 1 analyses—managers' external relationships predicting 
the intervening effects of external relationships. The upper portion of each cell displays 
the result for the full model and the lower portion of each cell displays the result for the 
single model. Panel B displays the results for the stage 2 analyses—the intervening 
effects predict outcomes of task performance, organizational commitment and LMX. 
Panel C presents the results for the direct effects analyses—managers' external 
relationships predict performance, commitment, and LMX directly. Figure 8 provides a 
graphical representation of the results. 
V.3 Access to resources and information 
As described in the methods section, access to resources and access to 
information represent two empirically separate though related constructs in this study. 
Therefore, I discuss each individually in the sections that follow.

Hypothesis 1: The more extensive a manager's network of external 
relationships, the greater will be his or her subordinates' access to 
information and resources. 
Table 11 displays the multi-level models that examine the predictive relationship 
between managers' external relationships and subordinates' perceptions of their access to

control variables, including transformational leadership, to predict resource access.2 
Transformational Leadership is a manager level control, aggregated across all 
participating subordinates of a given manager. Including transformational leadership as a 
control is necessary in order to determine whether managers' external relationships 
predict the costs and benefits to subordinates above and beyond the effects of the 
manager's downward leadership style. As displayed in model 1, managers' 
transformational leadership does positively predict subordinate resource access (b= .268, 
p =.032). The primary results testing Hypothesis 1 appear in Models 6-8, the full models. 
Model 6 examines both peer network constructs as predictors of resource access. As 
displayed in Model 6, neither of the peer network variables significantly predicts access 
to resources. Model 7 follows the same structure in examining the effects of LLX on 
Throughout the theory and hypotheses section, I used "extensive" to refer to both the strength of the LLX 
relationship between manager and boss and the degree centrality of the manager within the peer network. 
In this results section, I capture "extensive" through four measures, two (Friendship and Advice In-degree) 
that measure the manager's position in the peer network and two which capture dimensions of the 
manager's LLX relationship with his or her boss. 
28 A underlying premise of this study is that managers' external relationships shape the costs, benefits, and

transformational leadership, in order to assess whether external relationships and transformational

In summary, Hypothesis 1 received partial support from these analyses. Most 
notably, subordinates' access to information is positively predicted by two elements of 
the manager's external network—his or her quantity of peer friendships and the extent to

which he or she puts forth extra effort on behalf of the boss (LLX-eontribution). 
However, managers' external relationships do not predict subordinate perceptions that 
they have enhanced access to resources. 
Hypothesis 2: The greater subordinate access to information and resources, 
the greater subordinate performance will be. 
Table 17 displays the stage 2 analyses linking the five possible intervening 
variables to subordinate sales performance. As noted in the methods section, this 
analysis is conducted with the subset of subordinates in Organizations B and C who are 
front line salespeople and for whom I collected archival sales performance data. Model 1 
in Table 17 predicts sales performance as a function of both subordinate level and 
manager level controls. The list of subordinate controls includes baseline or target sales 
performance for each subordinate which serves a proxy for territory size/attractiveness 
and prior period sales performance. The set of manager level controls includes 
transformational leadership behaviors on the part of the manager as assessed by 
subordinates. This measure is aggregated to the manager level in these models. Models 
2-7 are the single models, in which I enter the six intervening variables—resource access, 
information access, perceived manager status, ascribed identity, manager availability, and 
manager time constraints—individually. In model 8,1 enter the hypothesized benefits 
together. I model 9 I enter the hypothesized costs together and in model 10, the full 
model, I enter all of the intervening variables at once. Table 18 displays the same 
information for Subordinate Performance (Assessed by Manager). In this table, I do not 
control for prior year sales, but do add controls for company B and job function as 
85 
described in the methods section. Otherwise, this table has the same models/column 
structure as table 17. As displayed in models 8 and 10 of Table 17, neither resource 
access nor information access significantly predicts sales performance. Similarly, as 
displayed in Table 18, neither resource access nor information access significantly 
predicts subordinate performance. Thus, Hypothesis 2 is not supported. 
Hypothesis 3: The greater subordinates' access to information and resources 
is, the greater their levels of organizational commitment will be. 
Table 19 displays the analyses linking the six intervening variables to 
subordinates' level of organizational commitment. As described above model 1 enters all 
of the controls, models 2-7 enter each intervening variable individually, and models 8-10 
integrate across the intervening variables. As displayed in Table 19, in models 8 and 10, 
resource access predicts subordinate organizational commitment (model 10, b = .446, p < 
.0001) but information access does not (model 10, b = .10,/? = .23). When viewed 
individually, in models 2 and 3, both access to resources (model 2, b = .491, p <.0001) 
and access to information (model 3, b=.417,/? <.0001) predict organizational 
commitment. As discussed earlier, the pattern of results under which the two constructs 
predict organizational commitment individually but only resource access predicts when 
integrated suggests that resource access is the more dominant effect. As a result, these 
results provide fairly strong support for Hypothesis 3, that when subordinates have access 
to information and resources, they experience greater commitment to the organization. 
The effects of access to information and resources on LMX 
86 
I did not hypothesize that subordinate access to information and resources would 
be associated with the LMX relationship between manager and subordinate. However, I 
tested these relationships as part of the analysis of LMX as a dependent variable. These 
analyses are displayed in Table 20, which has the same structure as Table 19. As 
displayed in these models, information access does not predict LMX. Models 8 and 10 
also reflect that resource access does not appear to predict LMX. 
In summary, managers' external relationships, as embodied by their peer 
friendships and the extent to which the manager puts forth extra effort on behalf of 
his/her boss, are related to the extent to which subordinates believe that they have 
enhanced organizational information and resources. The more peer relationships and the 
deeper boss relationships the manager has, the more employees feel "in the know" and 
well equipped with organizational knowledge. Yet, the managers' external relationships 
do not appear to predict resource access. Greater information and resource access, in 
turn, are related to employees' organizational commitment, but not, in this analysis, to 
their task performance. These analyses also suggest that when employees perceive that 
they have greater access to resources, they experience a more positive LMX relationship 
with their manager. 
V.4 Perceived Manager Status 
Hypothesis 4: The more extensive a manager's external network, the higher 
status subordinates will perceive the manager to be. 
87 
Table 13 displays the results examining managers' external relationships as 
predictors of subordinate perceptions of manager status. Model 1 examines perceived 
manager status as a function of the subordinate and manager level controls. Notably, 
transformational leadership does strongly positively predict perceived manager status (b 
= .563, p < .0001). In model 8, the full model, both friendship in-degree (b = .022, p = 
.009) and LLX-Contribution (b = .121, p = .019) positively predict perceived manager 
status. The other two explanatory variables do not predict perceived manager status. In 
summary, these results suggest that when managers have extensive peer friendship
