networks and engage in extra effort on behalf of their boss, subordinates perceive them as 
higher status within the organization. These results provide support for Hypothesis 4. 
Hypothesis 5: The higher status subordinates perceive their managers to be, 
the greater the subordinates' organizational commitment will be. 
As described above, Table 19 displays the results for analyses linking the 
intervening costs and benefits to subordinate organizational commitment. As displayed 
in models 10 of table 19, perceived manager status does not predict organizational 
commitment (b= -.045,/? = .60). However, when viewed in the single models, manager 
status does positively predict organizational commitment (b= .298,/? < .0001). The 
combination of these two perspectives suggest that while perceived manager status is 
associated with organizational commitment, resource access is the primary predictor, thus 
providing no support for Hypothesis 5. 
88 
Hypothesis 6: The higher status subordinates perceive their manager to be, 
the higher the LMX relationship will be between the manager and 
subordinate. 
The analyses linking subordinates perceptions of status to the LMX relationship 
between manager and subordinate are displayed in Table 20. As displayed in model 10, 
perceived manager status does not predict LMX (b= .039, p = .640) when controlled for 
by other intervening effects. However, when viewed with the proposed benefits of 
managers' external relationships, perceived manager status strongly predicts LMX (b= 
.241, p = .004) suggesting, again, that there is a positive association between manager 
status and LMX, but that the more significant predictors of LMX are ascribed identity 
and manager availability. In summary, these data provide partial support for Hypothesis 
6, that when subordinates perceive their manager as high status, they will enjoy higher 
levels of LMX with their manager. 
The effects of perceived manager status on sales performance 
I did not hypothesize an association between perceived manager status and task 
performance. However, as displayed in Table 17, model 10, manager status does 
marginally predict sales performance (b = .049, p = .085). As displayed in Table 18, 
manager status does not predict manager rated performance. 
In summary, these analyses suggest that managers' external relationships, as 
embodied by their peer friendships and willingness to engage in extra effort for their 
boss, are related to the extent to which their subordinates perceive them as commanding 
89 
status and influence throughout the organization. This perceived status, in turn, appears 
to motivate tighter LMX relationships between manager and subordinate. 
V.5 Ascribed Identity 
Ascribed identity is the degree to which subordinates perceived the manager as a 
part of the work group, e.g. "one of us." 
Hypothesis 7: The more extensive a manager's external network is, the 
weaker the manager's ascribed subordinate work group identity will be. 
The analyses examining Hypothesis 7 are displayed in Table 14. As depicted in 
Table 14, transformational leadership strongly predicts ascribed identity (b = .774, p < 
.0001), but none of the manager external relationship variables predict the manager's 
ascribed subordinate work group identity. Managers external relationships appear to be 
unrelated to whether subordinates perceive the manager as "one of us" or not. 
Hypothesis 8: The stronger a manager's ascribed work group identity, the 
stronger the LMX relationships between manager and subordinate. 
The analyses linking subordinates assessment of the manager's ascribed (in-
group) identity to the LMX relationship between manager and subordinate are displayed 
in table 20. Model 10 displays that ascribed identity positively predicts LMX (b = .264, p 
< .0001). These results provide strong support for hypothesis 8, that the more 
Given the strong association of transformational leadership and ascribed identity in these models, one 
might suspect that little variance remains for the effects of the four external relationship variables on 
ascribed identity. To test for this, I constructed models to mirror models 2-5 without transformational 
leadership and found no difference in the pattern of results. 
90 
subordinates perceived their manager as part of the work group, e.g. "one of us", the 
greater the bond on an individual basis between subordinate and manager. 
Hypothesis 9: The stronger a manager's aseribed identity, the stronger the 
organizational commitment of the manager's subordinates. 
Table 19 displays the analysis linking subordinate assessments of the manager's 
ascribed (ingroup) identity to his or her subordinates' organizational commitment. As 
reflected in model 10, ascribed identity does not predict subordinate organizational 
commitment when controlled for by the other intervening variables (b = .098, p = .188). 
These results do not support Hypothesis 9. 
The potential effects of ascribed identity on sales performance 
I did not hypothesize an association between ascribed identity and task 
performance. However, as displayed in Table 17, Model 10, there is a significant 
negative relationships between ascribed identity and sales performance (b = -.07, p = 
.002). As displayed in Table 18, the analyses reveal no association between ascribed 
identity and manager assessed performance. 
In summary, managers' external relationships appear to have no effect on 
subordinate's perceptions of the manager's identity as a member of the immediate work 
group. However, perceptions of the manager as integrated with the group, as being "one 
of us" appear to be important predictors of LMX and possibly task performance. These 
results support the notion that the manager's ingroup identity is the other edge of the 
boundary spanning sword. While boundary spanning provides the opportunity to garner 
status throughout the organization (and in the eyes of subordinates), managers must still 
91 
maintain their reputations as members of and aligned with the interests of their 
subordinate work groups. 
V.6 Manager Accessibility 
Hypothesis 10: The more extensive a manager's external network, the lower 
subordinates' access to the manager will be. 
As described in the methods section, I measured manager accessibility through 
two constructs. Manager availability measures the extent to which subordinates believe 
the manager is physically available to them when they need help or input or have a 
concern. Manager time constraints measures the extent to which the subordinates 
believe that managers don't have enough time available to do what is asked of them. 
Table 15 displays the results of analyses predicting manager availability. As 
reflected in Model 8, the full model, none of the external relationship variables predict 
availability. In contrast, this model reveals a marginally significant positive association 
between LLX-affect and availability. This finding runs counter to the hypothesis, 
suggesting that a strong manager to boss relationship is associated with more availability 
for the manager with his or her subordinate employees. Table 16 displays the results of 
analyses predicting manager time constraints and reveals no significant relationships 
between managers' external relationships and their perceived time constraints. 
Hypothesis 11: There will be a curvilinear relationship between 
subordinates' access to their manager and subordinates' performance. 
92 
Subordinates will perform best under conditions of moderate manager 
accessibility and lower at very low or very high accessibility. 
Table 17 provides the results linking the intervening variables to the sales 
performance of the sales people in Organizations B and C. As displayed in table 17, both 
availability (b = .053, p = .039) and time constraints (b= -.047, p = .048) predict 
performance in the hypothesized direction. When managers are more available to their 
subordinates and are less time constrained, sales performance is higher. In addition, 
though not reflected in the table, I also constructed models which added squared terms to 
the analysis of manager availability and manager time constraints, thus testing whether 
the relationship between time constraints and performance was curvilinear. The squared 
term was not significant in either case, lending no support for this hypothesis. Table 18 
provides the results linking the intervening variables to the manager rated performance of 
subordinates. As reflected in Table 18, there are no linear effects, though manager 
availability is marginally significant (b= .214, p = .091). As with sales performance, I 
constructed a version of this model with squared terms for manager availability and 
manager time constraints, but neither was significant. None of the network benefits or 
costs were significant predictors of manager assessed performance on in the single 
models. These analyses provide no support for Hypothesis 11 as written but suggest that 
manager availability and time management are important inputs to the downward 
managerial process. That external roles do not seem to impinge upon managerial 
availability is encouraging though unexpected. 
93 
Hypothesis 12: The higher subordinates perceive access to their manager to 
be, the higher the subordinates' organizational commitment. 
Table 19 displays the analysis linking subordinate assessments of the manager's 
availability and time constraints to the subordinate's organizational commitment. Model 
10 displays that neither availability nor time constraints predict subordinate 
organizational commitment. However, when viewed with the potential costs of external 
relationships, Model 9, manager availability does positively predict organizational 
commitment (b= AS9,p= .024). These results provide partial support for Hypothesis 
12. 
Hypothesis 13: The higher the access subordinates have to their manager, 
the stronger the LMX relationship will be. 
The analyses linking subordinates assessment of the manager's availability and 
time constraints to the LMX relationship between manager and subordinate are displayed 
in table 20. Model 10 in table 20 reflects that manager availability positively predicts 
LMX (b = .235,/? = .001), but that time constraints do not (b= -.082,/? = .238). These 
results provide partial support for Hypothesis 13. 
In summary, with one exception, managers' external relationships do not appear 
related to subordinates' perceptions of the manager's availability or time constraints. The 
exception is that when is viewed as a friend by his or her boss, he or she is more available 
to subordinates. Although contrary to the hypothesis put forth in this study, this finding 
94 
is consistent with the notion that people in high LMX relationships enjoy autonomy from 
their superiors, possible creating time to spend elsewhere. Manager availability looms 
large as a key component of the downward leadership task. The analyses presented here 
suggest that it is strongly related to all of the key subordinate outcomes, positively 
predicting organizational commitment, sales performance, and LMX. In effect, 
managers' external relationships do not impinge upon this critical managerial facet. 
V.7 Direct effects: Managers' external relationships predict subordinate level 
outcomes 
Hypotheses 14-16 addressed the possibility of a direct predictive connection 
between a manager's external relationships and the performance, organizational 
commitment, and LMX relationships of his or her subordinates. Drawing on the costs 
and benefits framework and the hypotheses that underlie the two stage indirect effects 
model, I did not hypothesize any linear effects, but rather predicted that the empirical 
relationship between managers' networks and their subordinates' outcomes would be 
curvilinear. In general, at low levels of external relationships, subordinates would 
"suffer" due to lack of network benefits, but at high levels of external relationships, 
subordinates would also "suffer" due to high levels of cost that result from having 
extensive networks. 
However, the results presented thus far suggest that there is little empirical 
support for the hypotheses that managers' external relationships materially create "costs" 
for subordinates. Therefore, a refined set of direct effects hypotheses would also include 
95 
the linear effects of networks suggesting that managers' external relationships positively 
predict subordinates' performance, organizational commitment, and LMX. Thus, in the 
sections that follow, I examine both the linear and curvilinear effects and related 
hypotheses. Also, to the extent that the analyses reveal direct linear effects, I also 
examine the extent to which these effects are mediated by the intervening effects of 
managers' external relationships. 
As a preview, few of the analyses in the tables to come yield statistically 
significant effects. As a general conclusion, there are few linear or curvilinear direct 
effects, thus supporting the two stage indirect effects model proposed throughout this 
study. Rather than report a long list of non-significant findings, I focus in this section on 
those relationships that do achieve statistical significance. 
V. 7. a. Sales/Task performance 
Hypothesis 14: There is a curvilinear relationship between a manager's 
external network and the performance of his or her subordinates. 
Subordinate performance will be highest at moderate levels of external 
managerial networks and lower at low or high levels of manager external 
networks. 
The analyses connecting managers' external relationships to subordinate sales 
performance are presented in Table 21. The analyses connecting managers' external 
relationships to their assessment of their subordinates' performance are presented in 
Table 22. Model 1 enters only the control variables, including transformational leadership 
96 
and baseline sales. Models 2-3 enter the linear and curvilinear term for advice in-degree. 
Models 4-5 enter the same friendship-in-degree, models 6-7 for LLX-affect, and models 
8-9 for LLX-contribution. Models 10-11 integrate across all peer networks. Models 12-
13 integrate across both conceptualizations of LLX. Models 14-15 integrate across all 
four measures of a manager's external relationships. 
Examining the incremental effects of adding the squared term in Models 3, 5, 7, 9, 
11, 13, and 15 of Table 21,1 find that none of the curvilinear hypotheses are supported, 
thus providing no support for Hypothesis 14 as written. However, when considering 
simply the linear terms, as reflected in models 10, 12, and 14, we find that LLX- affect 
does positively predict sales performance (model 14, b= .081, p = .011). When the boss 
and manager have a strong affective relationship, subordinates return higher levels of 
performance. None of the relationships are supported on Table 22, for manager assessed 
performance. 
What are the mechanisms that explain the connection of LLX-Affect to 
subordinate sales performance? Is this effects mediated by any of the proposed benefits 
or costs of managers' external relationships? To assess the mediated relationships, I 
employ Baron & Kenny's (1986) criteria for mediation. Three criteria must be met for 
mediation. (1) The independent variable must statistically predict the proposed mediator. 
(2) The proposed mediator must significantly predict the dependent variable. (3) When 
the mediator is added to the direct effects model, the statistical relationship between the 
independent variable and dependent variable is reduced. As revealed in Table 17, 
ascribed identity, availability, and time constraints each predict sales performance, 
97 
satisfying criterion 2. Only availability satisfies criterion 1 as it is predicted by LLX-
Affect. I examined criterion 3 with three different model configurations: (a) a fully 
integrated model with all independent and intervening variables entered, (b) all 
independent variables plus manager availability, and (c) only LLX-affect and manager 
availability. In each case, LLX-affect remained a significant predictor of sales 
performance with minimal reduction in effect size or statistical significance. The 
parameters for LLX-Affect were (a) b= .076,p = .0437; (b) b= .091,p = .021; (c) b= 
.052, p = .009. This combination of results suggests that the relationship between LLX-
Affect and sales performance is not mediated or only slightly partially mediated by 
manager availability. 
V.7.b Organizational Commitment 
Hypothesis 15: There is a curvilinear relationship between a manager's 
external network and the organizational commitment of his or her subordinates. 
Subordinate organizational commitment will be highest at moderate levels of 
external managerial networks and lower at low or high levels of manager external 
networks. 
Table 23 displays the analyses linking managers' external relationships to 
subordinate organizational commitment. The models have the same configuration as 
Table 21. As reflected in Table 23, none of the external relationship squared terms 
predict organizational commitment, thus providing no support to Hypothesis 15 as 
written. Further, as displayed in models 10,12 and 14, when reconsidering Hypothesis 
98 
15 as a linear direct effect, the analysis reveals no effects of managers' external 
relationships on organizational commitment. 
V. 7. c LMX between subordinate and manager 
Hypothesis 16: There is a curvilinear relationship between a manager's 
external network and the LMX relationship between manager and subordinate. 
LMX will be highest at moderate levels of external managerial networks and lower 
at low or high levels of manager external networks. 
Table 24 presents the results for the analysis linking managers' external networks 
to LMX. As reflected in this table none of the external relationship variables predict 
LMX on a curvilinear basis, thus providing no support to Hypothesis 16. Similarly, when 
moving to investigate the effects of the linear network terms on LMX, we find no 
significant relationships either. 
V.8 Peers vs. Boss Effects 
The final set of hypotheses explored the issues of whether peer relationships had a 
larger effect on network costs and benefits, subordinate performance, organizational 
commitment, and LMX. 
Hypothesis 17: Managers' peer relationships will have a larger effect on 
managers' accessibility than will the nature of their boss relationships. 
As previously reported, only one of the external relationship variables—LLX-
affect—predicts manager availability, albeit in the direction opposite from that 
99 
hypothesized. And, none of the external relationship variables predict time constraints. 
These finding provide no support for hypothesis 17. 
Hypothesis 18: Managers' peer relationships will have a larger impact on their 
ascribed identities than will boss relationships. 
As previously reported, none of the external relationship variables predict 
ascribed identity. Thus, these findings leave hypothesis 18 unsupported. 
Hypothesis 19: Managers' peer relationships will have a larger impact on 
subordinate job performance than will boss relationships. 
As described above, LLX-Affect predicts sales performance, but none of the peer 
network constructs do. These findings tend to contradict the hypothesis, suggesting that 
the boss relationship has a greater impact on performance than the peer relationships. 
Hypothesis 20: Managers' boss relationships will have a larger impact on 
subordinate organizational commitment than will peer relationships. 
The previously reported results from Table 23 reveal that a manager's peer 
networks do not predict the organizational commitment of his or her subordinates. In 
contrast, the single models in Table 23 suggest the manager's relationship with his or her 
boss, as reflected by LLX-Affect and LLX-Contribution, may be positively associated 
with subordinate organizational commitment. This provides tentative partial support to 
Hypothesis 20. 
Although I did not offer specific hypotheses for peer vs. boss effects for the 
remainder of the intervening (resource access, information access, or status) or dependent 
100 
variables (LMX) in the model, Tables 11-16 and 21-24 provide some insight on the 
relative strengths of boss vs. peer effects here as well. As reflected in Table 11, none of 
the external relationship variables predict resource access, so there is no need to ask the 
question regarding peer vs. boss effects. Model 8 in Table 12 suggests that peer 
networks, reflected in friendship-in-degree, are the primary predictor of information 
access. To examine this further, I examined the range of effect sizes for one and two 
standard deviations of variance for each variable in the full model and plotted the results 
in Figure 9. As displayed in Figure 9, the curve for friendship-in-degree has a steeper 
slope, suggesting a greater effect. Both friendship-in-degree and LLX-contribution 
predict manager status (Table 13, Model 8). Adopting the same approach as described 
for information access, I plot the range of effect sizes for one and two standard deviations 
of the two variables in Figure 10. The plot suggests larger effect sizes for peers over 
bosses, though the difference is quite negligible. Finally, as reflected in Table 24, there 
are no direct effects linking managers' external relationships to the LMX relationship 
between manager and subordinates. 
V.9 Summary of Results 
Table 10 summarizes the results from each of the three stages of the analysis. 
Panel A summarizes the findings related to stage 1—managers' external relationships 
predict network benefits and costs. The upper cell summarizes for the full models and 
the lower cell summarizes for the single models, for informational purposes only. The 
analyses presented here suggest that elements of the manager's external networks are 
101 
associated with boundary spanning benefits for managers and their subordinates. When 
managers have extensive peer friendship networks and are invested in the working 
relationship with their boss, subordinates perceived themselves as having greater 
organizational information and perceive the manager as higher status in the organization. 
However, contrary to predictions, managers' external relationships do not appear to 
create material costs in the eyes of their subordinates. There is only one (of 12 possible) 
significant relationship between the external relationships and potential costs and it does 
not function as a cost. A manager's affective relationship with his or her boss is 
positively associated with his or her availability to subordinates. This particular finding 
is subtly crucial as availability is consistently related to subordinate outcomes. It would 
seem that the strong affective relationship with the boss may result in autonomy for the 
manager resulting in more constructive downward management. 
Turning to Panel B, stage 2, resource access is the dominant predictor of 
subordinate organizational commitment. Consistent with hypotheses ascribed identity 
and availability predict LMX. Surprisingly, information access does not. When 
subordinates experience their manager as part of the work group and available to them, 
they experience a tighter bond to the manager. Three intervening variables—ascribed 
identity, availability, and time constraints—predict sales performance, though ascribed 
identity predicts it in the opposite direction from that which is hypothesized. 
Finally, Panel C reveals that there are few direct effects, suggesting that the 
relationship between a manager's external relationships and the work experience of his or 
her subordinates is an indirect one. Only one of the 8 possible relationships between 
102 
managers' external relationships and their subordinates' experience are significant. It 
seems that the stronger the affective relationship between manager and boss, the greater 
the performance of the managers' subordinates. 
These results suggest several inter-related summary level conclusions. First, 
managers' external relationships are associated with subordinate perceptions of their 
manager, the organization and their performance. Three of the four external relationships 
considered play a role in predicting either more proximal intervening variables and/or 
more distal subordinate outcomes. Both horizontal and vertical relationships are 
significant factors in these associations. Second, these associations are mix of direct and 
indirect effects. In particular, though, most of the connections between manager's 
external relationships and subordinate outcomes rely on the linking mechanisms 
presented here. These results shine a light not only on managers' external relationships, 
but on the intervening variables, the mechanisms that reflect the managers' execution of 
their boundary spanning roles. Adding emphasis to this point, subordinate resource 
access and the manager's ascribed in-group identity are also prominent in predicting 
subordinate outcomes. These two factors also represent potential elements of the 
manager's boundary spanning role, though neither display an association with managers' 
external relationships. 
103 
VI. DISCUSSION 
VI. 1 Overview 
The primary objective of this study was to examine the effects of managers' 
external relationships—the ties and interpersonal relationships they hold beyond their 
immediate work group—on two sets of subordinate effects (a) the subordinates 
perceptions of the manager's boundary spanning role and (b) more distal outcomes, e.g. 
their LMX with their manager and their organizational commitment and performance. 
The principle argument advanced in this study builds upon the work of Mintzberg (1973) 
and Ancona & Caldwell (1992) in framing managers as boundary spanners, connecting 
their subordinate workgroup and its members to the environment beyond the workgroup. 
I argued that managers build external relationships—in particular with their peers and 
their immediate superiors or bosses—and that these relationships yield two critical 
benefits for managers in leading their workgroups. First, drawing on social networks 
theory, I argued that rich external networks provide the manager access to key 
information and resources which he or she may then make available to subordinates. 
Second, drawing on status characteristics theory, I argued that managers who enjoy rich 
external networks are perceived by their subordinates as being high status within the 
broader organization. These two benefits correspond to the two critical external 
components of a manager's role: Serving as a conduit of information between the 
workgroup and its environment (Mintzberg, 1973; Ancona & Caldwell, 1992) and 
representing the group externally in pursuit of resources and spoils (Mintzberg, 1973; 
Pfeffer, 1981). Further, I argued that managers' external relationships shaped these 
104 
benefits above and beyond the effects of their downward or internal management 
activities and behaviors, as reflected by their displays of transformational leadership 
behaviors. 
At the same time, following Adler & Kwon's (2002) observation that social 
capital comes with both advantages and disadvantages, I also hypothesized that these rich 
external relationships may come with costs or downsides for focal managers. Research in 
Social Identity Theory documents that leaders enjoy more power and influence with their 
subordinates when subordinates perceive them as sharing an in-group affiliation with the 
workgroup (Hogg, 2001; Hains et al., 1997; Fielding & Hogg, 1997). But, when leaders 
have rich networks outside of the group, the manager may feel beholden to organization 
members outside of his or her workgroup (Adler & Kwon, 2002; Uzzi, 1997; Krackhardt, 
1999). I theorized that these managers may come to be perceived as distant from their 
subordinates, having a weakened workgroup ascribed identity, with the risk of not being 
aligned with the interests of their subordinates, e.g. not "one of us." If so, the manager's 
external relationships could erode trust and influence for the leader with his or her 
subordinates. 
Finally, drawing on resource allocation theory, I argued that managers' external 
relationships might also create a more tangible and physical cost for them (Becker, 1965, 
Bergeron, 2007). Network theorists suggest that interpersonal relationships require 
tending, investment, and maintenance (Burt 1997; Mayhew & Levinger, 1976). And, 
similarly, a preferred relationship with the boss often comes with extra demands and 
responsibility for the manager (Harris & Kacmar, 2006). These maintenance activities, I 
105 
argued, require the allocation of time that must come at the expense of other activities. 
Thus, I theorized that when managers have dense external relationships they are more 
time constrained and less available to their subordinate employees. 
These four intervening effects, representing key elements of a manager's 
boundary spanning role, provide the links between managers' external relationships and 
key subordinate outcomes of task performance, organizational commitment, and LMX. 
As such, I built theory that explicitly articulates an intervening or indirect effects model, 
suggesting that external relationships predicting the intervening effects which, in turn, 
predicted the subordinate outcomes. At the same time, I argued that the effects might 
counterbalance each other leading to a curvilinear direct effect. Too sparse a network, I 
hypothesized, fails to allow the manager to deliver the boundary spanning role, yielding 
insufficient resource and information access and perceptions of the manager as low status 
within the organization. Conversely, too dense a network creates excessive claims on 
the manager, reducing the in-group connection to subordinates and limiting time and 
physical availability. 
I tested these ideas in a field study involving three services organizations. The 
results presented here suggest that managers' external relationships do appear to function 
as both conduits of resource and signals of the managers' organizational status. When a 
manager has a broad friendship network and/or a rich LLX relationship with his or her 
boss, the manager's subordinates have greater access to information and perceive the 
manager as more powerful and influential throughout the broader company. These two 
effects are consistent with Mintzberg's (1973) conceptualization of the manager's 
106 
external role. Perceived manager status, in turn, predicts LMX. These effects occur even 
after controlling for the manager's display of transformational leadership, suggesting that 
both the managers' internal and external roles provide value for subordinates. At the 
same time, the analyses presented here reveal no evidence of the hypothesized costs or 
risks of managers' external relationships. There is no evidence to suggest that these 
external relationships erode the manager's in-group identity with his or her subordinates 
or materially impinge upon his or her availability to subordinates. In contrast, the 
analyses presented here suggest that a strong LLX relationship between manager and 
boss is positively associated with the manager's availability to subordinates. This is 
encouraging for managers as the analyses reveal the manager availability looms large in 
positively predicting subordinate attitudes, performance, and relationships. The analyses 
also reveal that resource access and ascribed identity are strong predictors of subordinate 
outcomes. While these two factors are not predicted, in this analysis, by manager's 
external relationships, they are additional elements of the manager's boundary spanning 
role and provide guidance to managers for how management of the boundaries, both 
instrumentally and in perception, may fuel the subordinate experience. In general, the 
results highlight the critical role that the intervening processes outlined here may play in 
shaping the subordinate worker experience and in channeling the effects of managers' 
external relationships. In the absence of costs, the analyses do not reveal a curvilinear 
relationship between external relationships and subordinate outcomes. However, the 
results reveal several positive associations between managers' external relationships and 
107 
subordinate outcomes with a mix of direct and indirect effects. I summarize the findings 
and conclusions in the following sections. 
VI.2 The potential benefits of managers' external relationships 
VI. 2. a Stage 1: Manager's external relationships as resource conduits and status 
markers 
My analysis reveals that managers' external relationships—their interpersonal 
relationships beyond their immediate work group—appear to serve as resource conduits 
and status markers for subordinates. As conceptualized in social network theory, the 
managers' relationships function as conduits of resource exchange. When managers are 
central in the peer friendship network and have high quality dyadic relationships with 
their immediate bosses, their subordinate employees experience greater access to relevant 
information. Centrality in friendship networks allows managers to tap into individuals in 
the organization who have relevant task know-how (Hansen, 1999), e.g. best practices, 
and organizational context (Cross & Cummings, 2004; Yukl, 2006). Thus, highly central 
managers are better positioned to keep their subordinates "plugged in." My findings also 
suggest that the key relationships are workplace friendships, reinforcing the notions that 
people first go to others whom they find warm (Casiciaro & Lobo, 2008) and that friends 
often represent the source of sensitive or high stakes information that is most valuable to 
organization members (Hansen, 1999; Mehra et al., 2006; Ibarra, 1992). That 
interpersonal relationships, in general, and networks, more specifically, function as 
conduits of information and resource exchange is at the heart of social network theory 
108 
(Freeman, 1979; Wasserman & Faust, 1994; Brass, 1984). However, this premise is 
seldom tested directly, but rather is assumed as part of broader hypotheses where 
information access is implicitly a mediator. 
Equally as critical is the upward relationship with the manager's boss. In these 
results, when managers put for extra effort for their boss, subordinates perceive 
themselves as having enhanced access to information. This suggests that the LLX 
relationship—the relationship between manager and boss—functions as one of social 
exchange. The manager's efforts provide a benefit to the boss, who reciprocates by 
welcoming the manager into the boss's inner circle and making him or her privy to 
selected information (Dienesch & Liden, 1986). This finding is consistent with the main 
thrust of the theories of both Mintzberg (1973) and Ancona & Caldwell (1992), who 
suggest that a primary role of a manager or leader is to be the point of information 
exchange between a work group and the group's external environment. 
Curiously, although both peer networks and LLX enhance subordinate access to 
information, neither is associated, in this analysis, with subordinates' access to resources. 
This is not surprising for peer networks as peers do not control the resources that allow 
managers and their teams to perform. Rather, peers, as suggested here, are potential 
sources of information. In contrast, the boss should be a critical source of resources that 
allow subordinates to perform their tasks. Within organizations, more senior managers 
and executives control the flow of funds and priority for equipment, technology, training, 
and other resources that might enhance a worker's experience or performance (Cross and 
Cummings, 2004; Yukl, 2006). Yet, in this study, managers' upward relationships with 
109 
their bosses do not appear to have an impact on subordinate perceptions of resource 
access. A likely explanation for these results is that they are limited by the setting and 
sample in this study. This study examines front line employees and their managers in 
large, well articulated, functional organizations. The employees are salespeople, 
customer service representatives, and accounts receivable processors. The potential 
resources in question—technology, equipment, information services, training, etc.—may 
be significant enough that allocation decisions may be made centrally and/or by a more 
senior member of the organization. In general, front line managers have more control 
and influence over the people and interpersonal dynamics of the organization than they 
do the resource allocations (Yukl, 2006). As such, front line manager's external 
relationships may have limited effect on resource availability, yet resource availability 
(ore perceptions thereof) may continue to affect subordinate attitudes. A more robust test 
of the resource access hypothesis may include managers higher in the organization with 
more implicit influence over resource allocation decisions. 
The same two dimensions of the manager's external relationships—peer 
friendship centrality and LLX-contribution—also predict subordinate perceptions of their 
manager's organizational status. This finding is a natural outgrowth of LMX theory 
under which the tight relationship between manager and boss places the manager in the 
boss's in-group or inner circle (Duchon, Green, & Taber, 1986). Subordinates perceive 
this and ascribe greater status to their manager. In a similar fashion, centrality in the peer 
friendship network also confers status on the focal manager. Centrality among one's 
peers may connote that the focal manager is accepted by them and that the manager has 
110 
the potential to exert influence throughout this particular group (Brass, 1984). 
Subordinates, these results suggest, see their manager as enjoying the camaraderie of his 
or her peers and ascribe to him or her higher likelihood of being part of this power 
structure of this level in the organizational hierarchy. The manager's relationships seem 
to function as an additional set of attained status characteristics (Blau & Duncan, 1976). 
Beyond the instrumental value, e.g. information, that external relationships provide, they 
act as signals to the subordinates of the manager's intra-organizational power and 
influence. 
The analyses of external relationships as resource conduits and status 
characteristics are also intriguing because of two non-findings. First, although peer 
friendships are strongly positively associated with information access and status, the 
analyses do not reveal strong associations for advice relationships. On the surface, this is 
counterintuitive, as one would expect that managers who engage with each other in the 
exchange of advice should be in the position to access key information for their 
subordinates. Certainly, prior research documents that individuals with rich advice 
networks are in a position to deliver better performance (e.g. Sparrowe et al., 2001; 
Baldwin et al., 1997) and greater influence through information access and control 
(Brass, 1984). And, prior research suggests that when a manager is a frequent source of 
advice and counsel for others it serves as a marker of organizational status (Venkatarimi 
et al., 2010; Krackhardt, 1990; Sparrowe & Liden, 2005). 
To be clear, that friendships are stronger predictors than advice relationships of 
information access or status perceptions is not surprising. As mentioned earlier, prior 
111 
research suggests that friendships may be the source of the most valuable, high stakes 
information (Hansen 1999; Mehra et al., 2006; Ibarra 1992), thus creating the possibility 
that advice relationships, while plentiful, may not be viewed as powerful sources of 
information. And, friendships are discretionary and, in principle, not tied to the existing 
work, suggesting that they are a more enduring marker of status among horizontal peers. 
More perplexing is that advice networks are not statistically significant in their own right 
in predicting information access or status. One possible explanation is that advice 
relationships among managers may not be visible to subordinates. Status perceptions, I 
argue, are derived when subordinates may form an opinion based on observed behaviors. 
Friendships, in contrast, may be embodied by who managers spend time with and thus are 
more visible to subordinates. Methodologically, I measured friendships in this study by 
asking with whom participants would socialize outside of work. This approach has the 
potential to make managerial friendships salient and visible to subordinates. Finally, it is 
conceivable that subordinates have their own work and technical related advice networks 
such that managers' networks are not incrementally valuable to them. However, in 
contrast, the high stakes information passed between (more) senior level members who 
are in the know may not be otherwise available to subordinates. Thus, it could be exactly 
that information that passes through managerial friends. 
The second interesting non-finding is that the affective relationship between 
manager and boss (LLX-Affect) does not predict the identified benefits in the same 
manner that peer friendships or LLX-Contribution do. One would think that being a 
friend of the boss would also place the manager in the boss's in-group, opening channels 
112 
of information and creating the aura of high status in the eyes of subordinates. The 
analysis suggests, then, that the mechanisms through which the two types of external 
relationships (peer vs. boss) function are substantially different. A manager's peer 
friendships are affective in nature and exist, I suspect, as a resource that is available to the 
manager, even in a latent state. In contrast, the LLX relationship as displayed in this 
study is more instrumental, rooted in the delivery of effort and work and, thus, likely 
functioning through more active and conscious social exchange. 
VI. 2. b. Stage 2: The effects of status, information, and resources 
The analyses presented here suggest that subordinate perceptions of the manager's 
status may be a key determinant for a manager in leading and directing his or her 
subordinates. When managers enjoy high status perceptions, their subordinates 
experience a greater LMX relationship with the manager and return higher performance. 
As framed by Minztberg (1973) and Pfeffer (1981), part of the manager's external role is 
to represent the group externally, to negotiate for favors and to cast its performance and 
value creation in the most favorable manner possible. High status allows managers do 
this by commanding the attention of others (Pfeffer, 1981). Further, Expectation States 
Theory suggests that regardless of the managers "true" power, when subordinates 
perceive them as high status, they will expect greater influence and results (Ridgeway & 
Berger, 1986). Thus, when subordinates believe they are working for a high status 
manager, they have confidence that he or she can access organizational resources and 
spoils at the appropriate times (Graen et al., 1977; Sparrowe & Liden, 2005). These 
113 
spoils might include job related resources, compensation, or assignments and promotions. 
And, when subordinates believe that their manager commands this level of influence on 
key decisions, they are motivated to put forth higher levels of effort in order to ingratiate 
themselves with the manager in hopes of the manager using his or her influence to gain 
spoils and benefits for the subordinate. 
Subordinates may also derive their own sense of status, and the status of the 
group, from working for a high status manager. The manager's status may reflect on the 
work group and its members (Sparrowe & Liden, 2005). Thus, the manager's status may 
transfer to the subordinate who may derive self esteem (Cialdini et al., 1976) or enjoy 
higher status perceptions of themselves (Kilduff & Krackhardt, 1994) by being connected 
to a high status manager. When individuals perceive themselves as being in the high 
status group, they are motivated to work harder in order to preserve their status 
differential (Van Knippenberg, 2000). 
On the other hand, the results also reveal a less prominent role for information 
access in predicting key subordinate outcomes. Information access does not predict any 
of the distal outcomes in the full models and only displays a single association to 
organizational commitment. This is a potentially critical finding suggesting that the 
managers' external relationships may serve more as a status signal than as a conduit of 
resource. While the analyses here suggest that information does flow through managers' 
relationships, they also suggest that the primary benefit to subordinates comes from the 
status attainment that the relationships reflect rather than from the resources that flow 
through the relationships. This distinction is a subtle but important contribution of this 
114 
study. Few, if any studies explicitly explore the proposed mediators of network effects 
and fewer still compare one potential mediator versus another. These analyses, thus far, 
present a picture of status rather than information and resource access as a more 
significant and relevant outgrowth of managerial networks. 
Resource access is strongly associated with subordinate outcomes in this study, 
and, at higher levels than those for information access. In particular resource access is 
strongly positively related to subordinate organizational commitment. Resource access 
may enhance commitment by allowing subordinates to feel that they are well positioned 
to succeed in their roles. The analyses presented here suggest that resource access 
dominates all other intervening effects (including information access) in predicting 
commitment. The analyses also suggest that there is a mild relationship between 
subordinate resource access and LMX; that when subordinates believe they have 
adequate resources, they engage in tighter relationships with their manager suggesting 
that they attribute the enhanced resources to their relationship with their manager. 
However, this empirical relationship does not hold in the presence of ascribed identity 
and availability. This combination of findings is intriguing as it suggests that resources, a 
decidedly externally controlled factor, are related to subordinate attitudes and 
relationships. This finding is consistent with research in perceived organizational support 
(e.g. Eisenberger et al., 1986) which suggests that when employees perceive high levels 
of organizational support, they are more committed to the organization. Yet, in these 
115 
analyses, the manager's external relationships appear to have little impact on resources. 
This leaves open the question as to whether managers, even in these settings, could have 
an impact on resources or not. 
Of significant note, resource and information access do not predict performance in 
these analyses. This is surprising given the wealth of theorizing on this topic in prior 
research (e.g. Mehra et al., 2006; Sparrowe et al., 2001). Researchers speculate that 
highly central individuals or groups gain access to information and resources to that 
group which in turn spurs performance. However, these researchers have not explicitly 
tested this mediating factor. Rather, they use it instead as a point of logic to link 
networks to individual and team performance, and find the emprirical relationship 
between networks and performance (e.g. Mehra et al., 2006; Sparrowe et al., 2001; 
Balkundi & Harrison, 2006). While the logic seems strong, my results here call it into 
question. 
VI.3.The potential costs of manager's external relationships 
VI. 3. a Stage 1: Manager's external relationships risks to identity and time 
While highlighting managers' external ties as a potential source of information 
and status, the analyses conducted here do not suggest that external relationships create 
counterbalancing costs for managers. In particular, the analyses do not support the 
notions that extensive external managerial relationships either erode the manager's 
identity with the subordinate workgroup or create substantial time constraints that render 
30 In addition, the proportion of manager level variance (ICC1) is lowest for resource access among all of 
the intervening variables. 
116 
managers unavailable to subordinates. There are no significant negative relationships 
between managers' external relationships and the potential costs of reduced in-group 
identity {ascribed identity), manager availability, or manager time constraints. Yet, as 
highlighted in the results section and discussed below, both ascribed identity and 
manager availability are strong predictors of subordinate organizational commitment and 
performance and of the LMX relationship between the manager and the subordinate. 
As previously noted, there is little prior research exploring the intervening effects 
of intra-organizational networks. Rather, researchers consider the intervening effects 
when making predictions regarding their ultimate dependent variables (e.g. Balkundi & 
Harrison, 2006). When cited, potential downsides of network relationships invariably 
play a secondary role to the theorized benefits. Consistent with this theoretical 
positioning, empirical research to date fails to find a negative direct effect between 
network centrality or rich LLX/LMX relationships and preferred individual and group 
outcomes, suggesting that, indeed, the costs of networks are subordinate to the benefits in 
most cases. It seems, therefore, consistent with prior theorizing, that these costs, while 
theoretically possible, do not manifest themselves in ways that are particularly salient, 
particularly to one's subordinate employees. 
VI. 3. b. Alternative Explanations 
Before concluding that managers' external relationships do not create materially 
costs fro them or their subordinates, we must also consider alternative theoretical or 
empirical explanations. I offer four. 
117 
Vl.S.b.i: Alternative Explanation One: External relationships enhance rather 
than detract from identity, availability, or time constraints 
First, it may be that managers' external relationships may actually improve their 
identity, availability, and time dynamics. Indeed, in the preceding analyses, I found one 
empirical relationship that operates in the alternative direction, e.g. managers' external 
relationships positively predict the theorized costs, a positive association between LLX-
Affect and manager availability. The analyses suggest that when the manager enjoys a 
strong affective/friendship relationship with his or her boss, the manager is even more 
available to his or her subordinates. This result highlights the complexity of the dyadic 
relationship between supervisor and subordinate. While being in the inner circle may 
bring more demands on the subordinate's time (Harris & Kacmar, 2006), strong LMX 
relationships are rooted in trust (Graen & Uhl-bien, 1995). As such subordinates often 
enjoy autonomy in their day to day operations (Gomez & Rosen, 2001). The affective 
bond between manager and boss, then, may provide the manager confidence that he or 
she is looked upon favorably by the boss, that his/her future is bright, and that the 
channels of communication are open when he or she needs the boss. In turn, this may 
free up time for the manager up to be more physically, mentally, and emotionally 
available for his or her subordinates. 
VI 3. b. ii Alternative Explanation 2: Implicit Leadership Theories 
A subordinate employee may form an overall or general impression of his or her 
manager that tends to shape or color more specific assessments that the subordinate may 
118 
hold of the manager. If the subordinate generally feels positively (negatively) toward the 
manager, then most assessments he or she makes of the manager will be positive 
(negative). This phenomenon is consistent with Lord and colleagues' concept of Implicit 
Leadership Theories or ILT (e.g. Lord, Foti, & DeVader, 1984; Cronshaw & Lord, 1987, 
Phillips &, Lord, 1986). Under ILT, individuals hold personal cognitive representations 
or schema of what constitutes a leader and leadership. These schema include attributes 
and behaviors of potential and actual leaders, such as intelligence, charisma, verbal skills, 
organizational skills, etc. When perceiving someone in a leadership role, individuals 
assess the target relative to the perceivers' own leadership schema and arrive at a general 
impression of that person, essentially answering the question "do I see this person as a 
leader?" Lord's research documents that when people perceive a particular individual as 
a leader, i.e. when he or she fits the perceiver's schema, the perceiver attributes other 
positive elements of leadership to him or her. 
The results in the study support the possibility that subordinates operate using 
ILTs. Specifically, the data displayed in Table 8, the simple correlations, reveal a 
consistent view of the manager by subordinates. Most specifically, the correlation 
between manager status and ascribed identity is r = .57, reflecting a strong likelihood that 
subordinates simultaneously view the manager as commanding status throughout the 
broader organization while still enjoying a strong in-group identity. Although it is 
possible, and certainly desirable, that manager enjoys these reputations simultaneously, it 
is equally plausible to expect them to be negatively correlated or, at minimum, 
uncorrelated. That they are strongly positively correlated provides evidence that 
119 
subordinates in this study evaluate their manager under some form of generally formed 
impression. 
I further examine the possibility of this ILT effect in two ways. The first 
approach involves inserting a variable into the predictive models that reflects subordinate 
impressions of the manager's general leadership skill. I conduct a version of this test in 
my core analyses by including transformational leadership as a control variable. 
Transformational leadership may be a proxy in these data for a general positive tendency 
toward leaders by their subordinates. As noted in the methods section, in this data set, 
transformational leadership correlates at r=.59 with initiating structure and r=.ll with 
consideration behaviors. These correlations are consistent with meta-analytic research 
that shows a strong association between transformational leadership and a variety of other 
leadership behaviors and attributions (Judge and Piccolo, 2004). In addition, prior 
research documents that transformational leaders may enjoy rich positive relationships 
with their subordinates (Wang, Law, Hackett, Wang, & Chen, 2005) or rich intra-
organizational networks beyond their workgroups (Bono & Anderson, 2005). As 
reported earlier, all of the analyses reported in this study are controlled by 
transformational leadership with most analyses revealing a strong association between 
managers' transformational leadership behaviors, as measured by subordinates, and the 
intervening and dependent variables of this study, providing additional confirmation of 
the ILT effect. Viewed through this lens, the results presented thus far such suggest that 
the ILT effect may be quite strong such that even after controlling for transformational 
120 
leadership, the sign on the effects of external managerial relationships on ascribed 
identity remains positive. 
The second approach to exploring ILT attempts to isolate the potential negative 
effects of networks on ascribed identity, manager availability and time constraints by 
controlling for the empirically proven benefits, e.g. status and information access. To 
explore this, I extended models 2-5 in tables 14-16 to include information access and 
manager status as predictor variables. If the parameters on managers' external 
relationships predicting ascribed identity or time constraints are negative under this 
approach, it would suggest that (a) there is a strong ILT effect, but (b) after successfully 
controlling for the ILT effect, managers' external relationships do, indeed, predict a 
downside risk or cost. Across these analyses, one parameter estimate did change to 
significantly negative. After controlling for information access and status, LLX-
Contribution negatively predicts ascribed identity (b= -.144, p = .048), suggesting that 
when managers put forth extra effort on behalf of their boss, their subordinates view them 
as less a part of the subordinate work group. In contrast, the analysis of manager 
availability revealed an even stronger positive association between LLX-Affect and 
availability (b — .197, p = .018), reinforcing the finding that when managers have strong 
affective relationships with their bosses, they perceived as more available by their 
subordinates. These results provide evidence of the presence of ILTs among survey 
participants and do provide limited support for the notion that ascribed identity is weaker 
when the manager has more extensive external relationships. 
121 
VI.3.b.iii. Alternative explanation three: Managers' general inclination to 
relationship building 
A third alternative explanation is that the positive relationship (or non-negative 
relationship) between managers' networks and the theorized network costs is the result of 
a hidden variable, an attribute of the manager which leads to a correlation of his/her 
external relationship breadth with the general relationship that he or she has with 
subordinates. For example, managers may simply have good interpersonal skills or 
attributes, perhaps represented by personality attributes, that simultaneously lead to 
strong networks (Klein et al., 2004), strong relationships with subordinates (Nahrgang, 
Morgeson, & Hies, 2009), or performance as a leader (Judge et al., 2002). In effect, if a 
manager is skilled at building interpersonal relationships in general, he or she might have 
strong external networks and strong internal relationships, leading to positive empirical 
relationships between external relationships and subordinate assessments of the manager. 
The Big Five Personality factors can represent a set of third variables that might be 
correlated with an individual's propensity to create ties in general (Klein et al., 2004; 
Totterdall, Holman & Hukin, 2008), displays of transformational leadership (Judge & 
Bono, 2000), and LMX (Nahrang et al., 2009). For example, managers who are high in 
extroversion or emotional stability may simultaneously (a) have rich networks, (b) be 
viewed as transformational leaders, and (c) be viewed generally positively by their 
subordinates. 
At time 1,1 asked managers to complete the Big Five Personality Inventory. As 
displayed in Table 25, manager personality, as represented by the Big Five personality 
122 
inventory, is not generally correlated with either the manager external relationship 
variables or transformational leadership. In addition, in post hoc analyses, I constructed 
a version of the stage 1 models (e.g. managers' external relationships predict network 
benefits and costs) intended to isolate and highlight the effects of managers' personality 
on subordinate assessments of the mediating variables. These models, reported in Table 
26, predict the network benefits and costs as a function of the subordinate and manager 
level controls (except for transformational leadership) and the manager's personality. 
Table 26 reports the parameter estimates for the effects of the manager's personality on 
each of the theorized network benefits or costs. As reflected in this table, the manager's 
personality is not, by and large, a significant predictor of subordinate perceptions of the 
network benefits and costs. These supplemental analyses do not support the theory of a 
hidden manager level variable as reflected by the manager's personality. 
VL3.b.iv. Alternative explanation four: Lack of a salient out-group 
The theory that suggests that managers with rich external relationships may suffer 
weaker ingroup identities is built upon Social Identity Theory (Tajfel & Turner, 1986). 
Social Identity Theory is a theory of intergroup conflict and competition in which groups 
vie for status and resources and strive, at times, to preserve and accentuate the boundaries 
and distinctiveness between each other. Part of the logic that underlies the theory of 
ascribed identities espoused here is that the boundaries between the work group and other 
groups are salient to organization members and that membership in one group detracts, to 
ol I excluded transformational leadership in order to independently assess the association of managers' 
personality with the intervening variables. 
123 
some degree, from membership in another. In extreme cases, the boundaries between the 
groups are highlighted by tension or conflict between them. For example, in two studies 
in which seemingly desirable leadership behaviors triggered negative out-group 
categorizations, the managerial out-groups were viewed negatively by front line 
subordinates (Shamir et al., 1998; Mehra et al., 2006). The lack of a negative relationship 
in the current study may reflect a lack of salient boundaries or conflict between the 
subordinate work groups in the study and the out-group represented by higher level 
management. I have no evidence from the research sites to suggest this conflict or 
tension exists. 
VI. 3.c. The effects of ascribed identity, manager availability, and time constraints on 
subordinate outcomes 
That managers' external relationships do not appear to erode or be negatively 
related to ascribed identity, manager availability or time constraints is an important 
finding, as my analyses show that they are, to varying degrees, strong predictors of 
subordinate performance and commitment and the LMX relationship between manager 
and subordinate. 
My analyses suggest that ascribed identity—the extent to which subordinates 
perceive their manager as part of the workgroup—does positively predict LMX. This 
findings are consistent with and represent a strong test of Social Identity Theory of 
Leadership (e.g. Hogg, 2001), in which prototypical members are perceived as more 
effective and aligned leaders than those who are not. These findings make an additional 
124 
contribution to SIT theory in that SIT researchers do not frequently measure the 
mediating variable, ascribed identity. These findings suggest that managers, then, must 
straddle the boundary line between the workgroup and the remainder of the organization. 
On the one hand, they must be somewhat separate from the work group, making 
decisions that affect all in the group and providing leadership and inspiration (Antonakis 
& Atwater, 2002). And, as my earlier results suggest, they must command enough 
differential attention to be perceived as high status. On the other hand, they must 
continue to be perceived as part of the group, working "for us" rather than "for them" 
(Haslam et al., 2001). Ascribed identity is an construct internal to the work group, but is 
very much part of the discussion of the manager's boundary spanning role. In straddling 
the line between the work group and the rest of the organization, the results suggest that 
successful managers will achieve in-group solidarity AND external status. 
Similarly, manager availability looms large in predicting subordinate outcomes. 
In the current analyses, manager availability positively predicts all of subordinate 
commitment, performance, and LMX. In essence, the ability of the manager to be 
physically present for the subordinates represents a key managerial lever. LMX 
relationships are built on trust (Graen & Uhl-bien, 1995) which require time and 
availability to create. In addition, people who are more available and accessible are more 
frequently targets for requests for help and assistance (Hofmann, Lei, & Grant, 2009). 
For managers who are available, this opens up an avenue for broader LMX relationships 
and greater managerial direction related to performance. 
125 
The analyses presented here suggest that a manager's time constraints do predict 
subordinate performance, but are not associated with either of subordinate commitment 
or the LMX relationship between manager and subordinate. Prior research suggests 
competing hypotheses for the effects of manager time and availability, particularly on 
LMX. On the one hand, managers need time and availability in order to create rich 
relationships with their subordinates (Coglisler & Schreischeim, 2000). Yet, on the other 
hand, when managers experience time constraints, they are forced to provide autonomy to 
subordinates (Kinnicki & Vecchio, 1994) leading to greater levels of LMX. Further 
research is required to unpack the dynamics of managers' time availability. 
VI.4 The direct effects of managers' external relationships on the LMX relationship 
with subordinates, subordinate organizational commitment and organizational 
performance 
In the theory section of this study, I suggested that the benefits and costs of 
managers' external relationships would partially counterbalance each other, with benefits 
increasing at lower levels of relationships and costs materializing with the most extensive 
relationships. The net effect, I hypothesized, would be a curvilinear relationship between 
managers' external relationships and subordinate organizational commitment and 
performance and the LMX relationship between manager and subordinate. However, in 
light of the lack of cost side effects reflected in these analyses, we should be surprised to 
find this curvilinear effect and, indeed, I do not. The analyses presented in this study 
provide no support for a curvilinear relationship. 
126 
Given the existence of network benefits, then, it is plausible to explore the 
possibility that managers' external relationships are positively associated with 
subordinate commitment, performance and LMX. However, in only one instance do the 
results provide support for the notion that managers' external relationships directly 
positively predict subordinate outcomes. The analyses suggest that strong LLX 
relationships between manager and boss result in enhanced subordinate sales 
performance. When the manager and boss enjoy an affective relationship, the analyses 
suggest, subordinates will return greater performance. Athough LLX-Affect predicts 
manager availability, this direct effect is only partially mediated by availability. 
IV.5 An indirect effects model 
The limited number of direct empirical effects linking managers' external 
relationships to LMX and subordinate commitment and performance suggest that the 
model articulated here is an indirect effects model. An indirect effect is one in which the 
independent variable does not directly predict the dependent variable, but, rather, 
influences the dependent variable through its effect on a linking mechanism or 
intervening variable (Mathieu & Taylor, 2006). For example, in this study, a manager's 
centrality in the peer friendship network has an indirect effect on subordinate's 
organizational commitment through the linking mechanism of perceived manager status. 
When managers are central in the friendship network of their peers, the subordinates 
perceive them as high status within the organization. And, when subordinates perceive 
their managers as high status within the organization, they (the subordinates) experience 
127 
higher levels of LMX. Thus, the manager's centrality has an indirect effect on 
subordinates' LMX. LLX-contribution similarly indirectly predicts LMX. 
What then does this tell us? The critical insight here is a subtle shift in theoretical 
and empirical emphasis from manager's external relationships to managers' boundary 
spanning roles. Rather than seeing the network costs and benefits as mediators or 
intervening variables that explain the link between managers' networks and subordinate 
outcomes, this study has implicitly explored the antecedents and outcomes of managers' 
boundary spanning roles. Managers function as boundary spanners, straddling the line 
between their subordinate workgroup and the environment external to workgroup. As 
boundary spanners, managers have decidedly external roles, e.g. acting as conduits of 
information and resources and developing reputations for status throughout their 
organizations. At the same time, by virtue of being on the boundary, the manager must 
also continue to manage his or her ability to be effective within the group. In drawing 
attention to the manager's maintenance of his or her role on the boundary, this study, 
then, explores two related questions. First, how does the manager's execution of the 
boundary spanning role affect subordinates, their experience and their performance? 
Second how do managers' external relationships affect the managers' delivery of their 
boundary management roles? 
The results presented here highlight the notion that subordinates are or may be 
affected by the managers' role position on the boundary between their workgroup and the 
rest of the organization. The extent to which subordinates perceive their managers as 
commanding status beyond the work group is associated with their commitment, 
128 
performance, and LMX. At the same time, to the extent that a manager has control over 
or influence on the flow of information and the allocation of resources from outside to 
inside the group, subordinates are also sensitive to these functions as well. On the other 
hand, subordinates continue to thirst for managers who conjure a reputation as being 
aligned with the interests of the workgroup itself. When managers succeed at this, 
subordinates may be more committed and experience greater LMX. As result, managers 
must strike a balance between the two sides of the boundary spanning role. As table 10 
highlights, however, no one boundary factor is singularly responsible for the subordinate 
outcomes. The challenge for the manager is to determine how to manage these three 
elements of his or her role. Finally, the results presented here continue to highlight the 
strong effects of the more traditional downward, purely internal perspective on 
management and leadership. As displayed in the results of this study, both 
transformational leadership and managerial availability loom large in predicting key 
subordinate outcomes. 
Within this framing, the analyses presented here suggest that the extent to which 
manager's build and make use of external relationships may shape their ability to deliver 
on the boundary spanning role. When managers have rich friendship networks and strong 
exchange relationships with their boss, they are able to provide key information to their 
subordinates and achieve a reputation for high organizational status. As suggested by 
Expectation States Theory, status is likely both a marker of the boundary spanning role 
having been successfully delivered in the past and an assessment of the manager's 
potential to represent the group in the future. At the same time, these analyses suggest 
129 
that managers' external relationships do not impinge upon their ability to manage their 
internal reputations, e.g. ascribed identity, or deliver on their internal roles. In fact, the 
analyses presented here suggest that external relationships, in particular those with the 
manager's direct superior, may create autonomy for the manager which he or she may 
parlay into greater presence with and availability for his or her subordinate employees. 
VI.6. Boss vs. Peer Effects 
Do relationships with the boss matter more than those with peers, vice versa, or 
not? The analyses presented here suggest that both boss and peer relationships are 
associated with subordinate access to critical information and the subordinate's views of 
the manager's status. However one type of external relationships does not appear to be 
more prominent than the other. As reflected in Figures 9 and 10, information access and 
status perceptions are roughly equally sensitive to both types of relationships. So, 
managers may make use of both peers and bosses for information and status management 
purposes, with the effects seeming additive and complementary. The relationship with 
the boss does appear to dominate when considering the direct relationships to 
organizational commitment and performance. While LLX is associated with subordinate 
organizational commitment and performance, peer networks do not. It seems likely that 
the boss relationship is the most visible to the subordinate and that the boss may have 
more ability than peers to take actions that bolster the manager's status and, perhaps, 
information access. In general, the advice one would give to managers then is to build 
130 
and manage constructive relationships with the boss and with peers. That said, 
confronted with a choice, the relationship with the boss is key. 
VI.7 The Role of Transformational Leadership 
Burns (1978) originally conceptualized transformational leadership as a style of 
leadership that orients the follower toward inspiring long term goals that transcend the 
individual. As such, transformational leadership was originally thought of as a desirable 
set of behaviors that were relevant in specific situations, particularly those involving 
large scale organizational change (Yukl, 2006). However, empirical research over the 
past 20 years bears out that transformational leadership is a strong predictor of a variety 
of more common, desirable outcomes, such as subordinate job satisfaction, motivation, 
satisfaction with the leader, and group level performance (Judge & Piccolo, 2004). 
Further, while empirically distinct, transformational leadership behaviors tend to 
correlate with other downward leadership behaviors, such as transactional leadership and 
initiating structure (Judge & Piccolo, 2004). In addition, research suggests that when 
leaders engage in transformational leadership, they achieve greater centrality in 
organizational networks (Bono and Anderson, 2005). 
The inclusion of transformational leadership, then, serves two purposes in this 
study. First, it serves as a general proxy for the manager's downward leadership 
behaviors. The results presented here reveal strong associations between the manager's 
display of transformational leadership and resource access, information access, manager 
status, ascribed identity, manager availability, organizational commitment and LMX. 
131 
These results re-affirm the role that downward leadership behaviors play in shaping the 
subordinate experience. In addition, subordinate assessments of transformational 
leadership may be good proxies of the general impression that a subordinate holds of the 
manager or a representation of the manager's fit with the subordinate's leadership 
schema. 
Second, given the strong associations between transformational leadership and the 
variety of subordinate level outcomes, a strong test of the impact of external relationships 
requires controlling for these internal behaviors, as proxied by transformational 
leadership. As described earlier, all of the analyses in this study were controlled for 
manager level transformational leadership. The analyses suggest that managers' external 
relationships do indeed predict boundary spanning benefits and that the benefits predict 
the outcomes, all after accounting for the impact of transformational leadership. Thus, 
we can conclude that (a) the effects of external relationships are distinct from those of 
downward leadership behaviors and (b) network benefits, such as information access and 
status, are distinct effects on subordinate outcomes from those of downward leadership 
behaviors. 
VL8 Some supplemental findings: boss level networks and mangers' difficult 
relationships 
All three research sites gave me permission to collect data that extended beyond 
the scope of the initial theory section of this dissertation. In particular, I extended the 
network data gathering in two ways. First, rather than restrict the network roster of 
132 
potential ties to managers' horizontal peers, I defined the manager's network to include 
all the bosses in the study, i.e. the supervisors at the next higher level in the hierarchy. I 
also asked the bosses to participate in the network study as well. Thus, in addition to 
horizontal ties (for advice and friendship) and the specific dyadic relationship between 
manager and boss, these data allowed me to ask questions about the broad set of ties a 
manager might have to the next echelon. This next echelon could be useful as they are 
likely to (a) more broadly control resources (Cross & Cummings, 2004), (b) have access 
to more strategic and environmental information (Yukl, 2006), and (c) more directly 
reflect the manager's status within the organization (Berger et al., 1972). Similarly, more 
extensive ties to the upper echelon could contribute to an erosion of the manager's 
ascribed in-group identity, that the more one spends time with and is associated with the 
next echelon of management, the less one is perceived as just one of the work group. 
Second, the research sites allowed me to add a type of interpersonal relationship: 
difficult relationships32 between individuals, both peers and bosses. Difficult 
relationships involve "feelings of discomfort or dislike and may lead to negative 
assessments between individuals and groups (Labianca & Brass, 2006)." When people 
have difficult relationships with each other, they may engage in negative or 
counterproductive behaviors toward each other. Managers in difficult relationships may 
avoid each other, closing off, for those dyads, the flow of information. (Labianca & 
Brass, 2006). At the most extreme, people involved in negative relationships may 
,21 gathered data on difficult relationships by presenting the full roster of names and asking "please check 
the box if you have a difficult relationship with this person." 
133 
actively hinder or undermine each other (Duffy, Ganster, & Pagon, 2002; Sparrowe et al., 
2001). In many organizational settings, managers may not be able to avoid those with 
whom they have difficult relationships, but these interactions likely take a different tone, 
one that is bounded and transactional. For example, Sparrowe et al., (2001) found that, 
within teams, individuals with large numbers of hindrance ties performed worse and that 
teams with overall large numbers of hindrance ties suffered performance aggregate 
shortfalls. Negative or difficult relationships are intriguing because they exhibit negative 
asymmetry, the phenomenon under which negative relationships, may have greater 
effects on individual perceptions than commensurate positive relationships (Labianca & 
Brass, 2006). Where friendships may facilitate managers' information flow and status 
evaluations, difficult relationships may be disproportionate impediments to achieving 
these benefits. 
My analyses using these additional network relationships suggest that both are 
relevant to the present discussion. To be specific, I found that a manager's upward 
difficult relationships also shaped status perceptions but in a negative direction. When 
managers have a preponderance of upward difficult relationships, e.g. don't get along
