with the boss's peers, subordinates hold a lower view of the manager's status. Difficult 
relationships (or at least the reporting of difficult relationships) tend to be rare in 
organizations (Labianca & Brass, 2006), in this study occurring at about 5% the 
frequency of positive ties. Thus, the message here is that a small number of fractured 
relationships with the upper echelons in the organization can be especially challenging 
for one's reputation management, in this case with subordinates. I did not find that 
134 
difficult peer relationships had an impact on either status or information/resource access 
and this was not surprising. It seems likely that the positive and proactive relationships 
among peers will carry the day as it is their volume that opens up resource channels and 
creates status impressions. However, it was perplexing to find that upward friendships 
did not predict perceived manager status. Consistent with the results reported thus far 
regarding horizontal networks, I did not find any positive associations between upward 
advice networks and the intervening variables, nor did I find any direct connections 
between upward or difficult managerial networks and subordinate outcomes. Rather, the 
direct connections, as previously reported, seem to exist from LLX to organizational 
commitment and performance. 
Finally, at Time 2,1 also gathered an additional dependent variable, subordinates' 
performance of helping behaviors, both within and external to the work group. Helping 
is "promotive behavior that emphasizes small acts of consideration," and "is cooperative 
behavior that is noncontroversial." (Van Dyne & Lepine, 1998, p. 109). In including 
helping behaviors, I theorized that one of the benefits of managers' external 
relationships—information access—would shape subordinate helping behaviors. 
Individuals make an expectancy based calculation about potential helping behaviors 
(Morrison & Phelps, 1999), assessing whether their actions will create benefits for their 
targets. Greater organizational information access shapes this expectancy calculation, I 
" Subordinates completed a six item helping scale adapted from Van Dyne & Lepine (1998). Participants 
assessed how frequently they engaged in the list of helping behaviors on a 5 point Likert scale ranging from 
"1"= "never or not at all" to "5" = "very frequently." Sample items included "I provide assistance to 
people outside of my work group so that they can get their work done" and "I help people outside of my 
work group solve work related problems." 
135 
theorized, in two ways. First, better informed employees view themselves as more 
competent, which in turn allows them to see that they, indeed, have value to bring to the 
other party (Parker, Williams, & Turner, 2006). Second, the better informed member has 
a greater understanding of how his or her resources may be useful to the other party. 
Well informed members, then, may expect that that their helping behaviors are more 
likely to pay off. The supplemental analyses support this theory, with information access 
(b = .33, p <01), but not perceived status, positively predicting helping behaviors. When 
subordinates are better armed with information, they can see a path to productively 
helping and engage in those behaviors. 
VI. 8 Summary of findings and contributions 
Summarizing, this study makes three contributions to the literatures on leadership 
and social networks. First, as framed by Mintzberg (1973) and others (Aconca & 
Caldwell, 1992; Sayles, 1965; Likert, 1961; Graen, 1976), managers' roles include an 
external component in which they manage the boundary between their subordinate 
employees and the rest of the organization. Where leadership research typically assesses 
the downward leadership behaviors and styles of leaders, this study examines the roles 
managers play at the boundary of their groups and the effect these roles have on the 
subordinate experience. By accessing and synthesizing information beyond the 
workgroup, managers provide critical context for their subordinates, putting their work 
and contribution in context, ultimately enhancing subordinates' connection to the broader 
organization. Managers also have the potential to access critical resources for their 
136 
subordinates. The results from this study make clear that when subordinates believe they 
have the necessary resources to do their jobs, they are more committed to the 
organization and experience stronger bonds to their managers. And, managers, in pursuit 
of their external role, develop reputations for status that are of value in the management 
of their subordinate employees. The results from this study highlight that when 
subordinates see their managers as commanding status and influence, they (the 
subordinates) are more committed to the organization, enjoy a better relationship with the 
manager, and, ultimately, deliver better task performance. 
At the same time, this study highlights that boundary spanning roles are just that, 
at the boundary between their work group and others. Managers do indeed straddle this 
boundary, and, as such, the management of one's internal identity remains as important 
as one's external activities. As suggested by social identity theory, the LMX relationship 
between manager and subordinate is quite contingent on the degree to which subordinates 
perceive the manager as aligned with the workgroup and as a member of the same. Thus 
a manager must carefully manage both sides of his or her boundary spanning role, 
accessing information and resources externally, commanding status and influence 
externally, while remaining perceived as a member of the immediate team. 
And, this study re-affirms managers' internal roles and the place of the boundary 
spanning role. First, boundary spanning roles are a complement to, not substitutes for 
purely internal roles. This study highlights the power of transformational leadership 
behaviors and manager availability as tools and levers of managerial effectiveness. In 
addition, by including downward leadership behaviors in the study, the external 
137 
perspective is held to a rigorous test. This study, then, paints an increasingly complex 
picture of the demands placed upon a manager. While reaffirming the importance of 
traditionally explored downward leadership dimensions, it highlights the potential value 
of the manager's execution of the boundary spanning role, both outside and within the 
workgroup. 
Second, the results from this study highlight the prominent role that managers' 
external relationships—the interpersonal relationships beyond their workgroup—play in 
facilitating these boundary spanning roles. In particular, this study highlights the 
relationships managers may have with their direct bosses and with horizontal peers. The 
theory and analyses presented here suggest that both of these types of relationships are 
necessary to capture the boundary spanning benefits. These working relationships appear 
to have a flavor of social exchange, in which managers step up and provide extra effort 
on behalf of the boss, while, in return, the boss brings the manager into his or her inner 
circle, exposing him or her to key organizational information and lending the manager 
some of his or her organizational status. Managers also enjoy these "network" benefits 
when they have rich horizontal networks of friendships among peers. At the same time, 
the results from this study lead us to downplay concerns about the potential costs or risks 
of managers' external relationships. Indeed, managers' external relationships do not 
seem to interfere with their internal roles, showing limited effect on their identity as a 
group member or their time constraints. In fact, my analyses suggest some benefits to the 
internal role that come from a rich relationship with one's direct superior. When 
managers have strong affective relationships with their bosses, they are perceived as 
138 
more available to their subordinates, which, in turn, may facilitate subordinate 
performance. 
Finally, this study extends the networks literature by examining the intervening 
effects that link networks and LMX to key outcomes. In the networks literature, 
intervening effects such as information access or status are often referred to as part of 
researchers' logic, but seldom directly analyzed. Similarly, mention is made of potential 
costs, but definitive exploration is often deferred. In this study, I explicitly theorize about 
and measure these intervening effects. As such, the study sheds light on the manner in 
which networks provide benefits and the degree to which they create costs. My findings 
point toward two significant findings. First, while much prior research emphasizes the 
resource conduit effects of networks, this study highlights the more prominent role that 
networks may place as status markers. Second, in assessing three different potential 
dimensions of the costs of external networks and finding them lacking, this study 
provides a more definitive view that the costs are not major factors in our assessments of 
social networks. 
VI. 9. Limitations and extensions 
VI. 9. a Limitations 
Although I gathered data in multiple time periods, the study is essentially a cross 
sectional study. Thus, although the theory points to effects operating in a particular 
direction, I cannot make a strong empirical claim of causality, leaving open the 
possibility of reverse causality. For example, subordinates' access to information and/or 
139 
perceptions of their manager's status could be reflections of sources of value that the 
manager brings to the organization which in turn make him or her a more attractive 
network partner particularly to his or her peers. Similarly, when subordinates enjoy a 
strong LMX relationship with their manager, they may naturally ascribe a stronger in-
group identity to him or her. Finally, the association between LLX-Affect and 
subordinate performance may also operate in the reverse direction from that 
hypothesized. Bosses may be motivated to build strong relationships with those 
subordinate managers whose employees deliver high performance. As cross sectional 
study, these results cannot rule out the possible of reverse causality in these areas. This is 
a limitation shared by similar studies (e.g. Mehra et al., 2006; Venkataramani et al., 
2010). Future research should include a true longitudinal design with three waves in 
order to fully test the intervening effects model presented here (Mathieu & Taylor, 2007). 
In addition, some of the open issues of reverse causuality may be resolved through testing 
some of the specific effects in an experimental setting. Also, I note that while there are 
many intriguing significant results concentrated in particular parts of the theoretical 
model, there are also substantial areas of non-findings. The volume of tests conducted 
here raises the possibility that some of the results may due to random chance. 
In addition, I do not capture the network of relationships among subordinates 
(Venkatramani et al., 2010) or the centrality of the manager in the subordinate work 
group (Mehra et al., 2006). The primary reason for this omission was a desire to manage 
survey length with my research sites. I committed to an aggregate survey length and time 
to complete and chose to make a rich exploration of intervening factors and dependent 
140 
variables, the topic of the study, in exchange for forgoing the examining of the networks 
within the work group. While it may have served as an additional control variable in the 
analyses, and may very well predict a subordinate's organizational commitment or 
turnover intentions (Mitchell, Holtom, Lee, Sablynski, & Erez, 2001) or information 
access, subordinate network structure seemed unlikely to predict three of the four 
intervening variables which described either their views of their manager or the outcomes 
of the role the manager played. It may be the case, however, that assessments of the 
manager and LMX between manager and subordinate are shaped by the manager's work 
group centrality. Certainly, this is a prediction of Simmel (1908 [1955]) and proponents 
of the social identity of leadership (Hogg, 2001). Further, we know that team level 
viability and performance are enhanced with a highly central leader (Balkundi & 
Harrison, 2006). I believe that I have proxied this effect by including transformational 
leadership as a control and through conducting additional analyses with the managers' 
personality as a control. 
Finally, although I executed the empirical study in multiple settings, the study 
may still suffer from boundary limitations or range restriction. In particular, I failed to 
find a negative effect of external relationships on ascribed identity. In two prior studies, 
Shamir et al. (1998) and Mehra et al. (2006), seemingly desirable leadership behaviors or 
external relationships were negatively correlated with subordinate perceptions of the 
leader. However, in each of these studies, substantial conflict or tension existed between 
the subordinates and the echelon of management two or more levels above them. I have 
no reason to believe that this level of conflict exists within the three research sites in this 
141 
study. Under Social Identity Theory, the identification processes are stronger when 
group differences are more salient. Thus, my settings may not have enough group 
salience to identify the ascribed identity effect. 
VI. 9. b Potential extensions 
The study presented here is but a first step in the exploration of managers' 
external relationships and roles, and thus several possible extensions seem appropriate. 
First, this study examined the horizontal and direct vertical relationships of front line 
managers in large corporate organizations with clear hierarchical organization structures. 
Each of these factors presents a potential boundary condition to be tested and explored in 
future studies. For example, how might the dynamics shift for higher level managers, 
such as VP level officers in the same types of companies? As described earlier, it is at 
this level that managers may have a more dramatic effect on resource allocations. Or, 
how might these dynamics change for the senior executives of smaller, more 
entrepreneurial companies, where the relevant networks may be truly external to the 
companies? Finally, we might ask how the dynamics of managers' networks change 
when the organization is more fluid, when managerial reporting roles may change 
frequently, such as in a professional services firm, or when hierarchies are more 
permeable such that subordinates have substantial external networks of their own. 
Underlying this study is the premise that managers serve as broker between their 
subordinates and the rest of the organization. In network parlance, managers occupy 
structural holes between their subordinates and other organization members. But, what 
142 
happens in organizations where that is not so? For example, if subordinates are well 
connected externally themselves, does this provide the opportunity for managers to shift 
their focus with their subordinates from one of boundary spanning to one of coaching and 
development? Or does it place an even higher premium on the manager's role in 
representing the group externally? 
One of the criticisms of network research is that it is more descriptive and static 
than it is causal and dynamic (Watts, 2003). Somehow managers' networks are 
correlated with outcomes, but how? I take the first step here by explicitly exploring the 
intervening variables. Yet, we are still left with several open questions about how 
managers do and could approach their external relationships. For example, do these 
relationships growing organically or do managers take a proactive stance toward them? 
How do managers differ in their use of and manipulation of their networks? Do they 
strive to connect others between whom they are currently brokers or do they maintain 
those holes for themselves (Obstfeld, 2005)? In the context of this study, this is about 
asking whether managers connect their subordinates with critical alters or keep 
themselves in the pathway as the key point of connection. Further, how do managers 
differ in their perceptions of the "value" of their networks? Do some understate the 
possible in their networks and what are the costs of this? 
By focusing on certain intervening variables and outcomes, I also implicitly open 
the door to others. My study highlights the strong association between a manager's 
reputation as one of the workgroup and the organizational commitment of his or her 
subordinates as well as the LMX relationship between manager and subordinate. But, 
143 
what of his or her reputation of being part of the management structure of the 
organization. Might being perceived as "one of them," distinct from "one of us," have a 
detrimental effect? Or might subordinates want a manager who successfully straddles the 
line between the two? Similarly, in a cross sectional study, it was difficult to explore the 
career outcomes associated with working for a particular manager, yet it seems likely that 
this is one of the key benefits of working for a well networked manager. And, what are 
the affective or mood related effects for both managers and subordinates? And, what role 
do subordinates' perceptions of their managers' networks play in this model? In 
particular, might network perceptions, more than actual network positions, shape 
subordinate status and identity assessments? 
While examining the manager's networks and relationships, this study does not 
explore the situation from the manager's perspective, choosing instead to examine 
subordinate level reactions and outcomes. Yet, the manager, of course, looms large in 
these discussions. How does the manager experience his or her set of network 
relationships, both external and internal to the workgroup? And, similarly, how does he 
or she perceive the distinction between internal and external roles? How do these 
differences shape the manner in which the manager conducts him or herself, building 
relationships, utilizing them, and managing the trade-offs between internal and external? 
Finally, this study has explored manager's external networks and their impact on 
subordinates. But what of the opposite direction? How do managers' peers and bosses 
perceive their relationships with subordinates? If the manager has tight relationships with 
subordinates or is central in subordinate networks, do superiors perceive that positively or 
144 
negatively? And, finally, what of a manager's mentoring relationships with other junior 
members who may not be his or her direct subordinates? 
VI. 10. Practical Implications 
This study provides substantial guidance to practicing managers and human 
resource professionals within organizations. Much of the advice that scholars and other 
counselors provide to managers emphasizes the activities that occur within the 
workgroup, between leaders and individual members or between leaders and the group as 
a whole. "Leadership" is conceptualized as the set of activities, conversations, and 
interpersonal interactions that occur between leaders and their followers, between 
managers and subordinates (e.g Kouzes & Posner, 2008; Robbins, 2003). In contrast, this 
study highlights the notion that the manager plays a key role in managing the boundary 
between his or her subordinates and their external environment. Managers can enhance 
the performance and attitudes of their subordinates by ensuring that they bring into the 
group relevant information and resources. To some extent, this is part of the message 
that managers receive, but it is often secondary. A cursory review of the practitioner 
literature on management and leadership finds that the boundary spanning activities of 
managers takes on a far less prominent role than the downward supervisory and 
leadership activities. Thus, my study contributes to the advice we give managers and the 
competencies required for successful management. Similarly, this study provides clear 
evidence that subordinates value the intra-organizational status of their managers and, on 
balance, prefer to work for those who are high status. That one's status may be a 
145 
managerial tool in and of itself is not something that has been considered in the 
practitioner literature and, in fact, may be discouraged as self-serving. In contrast, this 
study suggests that subordinate constructions of their manager's status are a critical lever 
in subordinates' attitudes and performance. Thus, managers must carefully bring their 
attention to managing their organizational reputation. But, while prior literature would 
suggest that reputation is a tool of career management, this study suggests that it is a 
critical lever of employee attitudes. The major message to managers is to look outward 
while looking inward as a method to managing, leading, and directing the work group. 
The study offers a second set of recommendations for practicing managers. 
Simply put, building and maintaining the right mix of external relationships is key to 
delivering on managers' boundary spanning roles. Managers must establish trusting, 
engaged relationships with their bosses and be prepared to go the extra mile for them. 
And, they must build and maintain rich networks of friendships with their peers. As a 
manager, one must become part of the fabric of the organization. When managers 
accomplish this, they are able to access information and build the reputations for status so 
critical to the assessments of their subordinates. In the past, commentators have 
suggested to any member of an organization that he or she must build, manage, and make 
use of external networks, but this advice has been positioned as a career management 
tool. To wit, you must build networks to get ahead, to get the promotion, to get the next 
job (e.g. Darling, 2005). While not denying these benefits—indeed, they too are 
supported in the scholarly literature (Granovetter, 1973; Seibert et al., 2001; Podolny & 
Baron, 1997)—the insight presented here is that these same networks are critical to being 
146 
a more effective manager of one's subordinates. Thus, the two activities become one. 
Managing one's career and managing one's subordinates are both enhanced through 
managing external networks. 
At the same time, the study reinforces the powerful impact of various aspects of 
the manager's downward or internal behavior. Leadership style remains important to 
subordinate perceptions and outcomes as does the manager's ability to be perceived as 
aligned with subordinates and their interests and to be available and open to their 
inquiries and requests for input and assistance. Managing networks and boundary roles 
represents an additional set of duties for a manager, not an alternative, thus making the 
manager's role that much more complex, demanding, and difficult. Fortunately, my 
study, as with others, does not find significant costs or downsides to adding the external 
role. Rather, it provides mild support for the notion that managing well up and out may 
have benefits across the board for one's internal role. 
Finally, these insights regarding managers' external roles may become additional 
inputs into organizations' management development processes. External relationship 
management may become an additional entry into company competency models and 
performance evaluations. And, with their addition to the development menu, they may 
become fodder for coaching, development, and training opportunities for managers. 
VII. CONCLUSION 
This dissertation has examined the boundary spanning roles of managers within 
organizations and the external relationships that managers make use of to execute on 
147 
these roles. The theory and empirical results support the notion that the manner in which 
managers enact their boundary spanning roles may have substantial impacts on the 
instrumental and socio-emotional outcomes for the subordinate employees. Similarly, the 
theory and empirical results highlight the role that external relationships have in shaping 
the boundary spanning roles. While initially framed decades ago (Mintzberg, 1973; 
Sayles, 1964), research that examines managers' boundary spanning roles is relatively 
sparse. In addition to the specific contributions described above, my hope is that this 
study spurs further exploration of this critical role of organizational managers. 
148

Appendix A: Survey Scales 
LMX-MDM scale (Liden and Maslyn, 1998) 
Utilized to measure LLX and LMX 
(Up: Subordinate perspective) 
Instructions: In this section, we ask you to consider the nature of your relationship your 
direct supervisor. Please read each statement and assess the degree to which you agree 
with it. l=strongly disagree to 5 = strongly agree. 
Affect 
1. I like my supervisor very much as a person. 
2. My supervisor is the kind of person one would like to have as a friend. 
3. My supervisor is a lot of fun to work with. 
Loyalty 
4. My supervisor defends my work actions to my superiors, even without complete 
knowledge of the issue in question. 
5. My supervisor would come to my defense if I were 'attacked' by others. 
6. My supervisor would defend me to others in the organization if I made an honest 
mistake. 
Contribution 
7. I do work for my supervisor that goes beyond what is specified in my job 
description. 
8. I am willing to apply extra efforts, beyond those normally required, to meet my 
supervisor's work goals. 
9. I do not mind working my hardest for my supervisor. 
Professional Respect 
10.1 am impressed with my supervisor's knowledge of his/her job. 
11.1 respect my supervisor's knowledge of and competence on the job. 
12.1 admire my supervisor's professional skills. 
(Down: Supervisor perspective) 
Instructions: In this section, we ask you to consider the nature of your relationship with 
this specific employee. Please read each statement and assess the degree to which you 
agree with it. l=strongly disagree to 5 = strongly agree. 
Affect 
1. I like this employee very much as a person. 
2. This employee is the kind of person one would like to have as a friend. 
216 
3. This employee is a lot of fun to work with. 
Loyalty 
4. This employee defends my work actions to my superiors, even without complete 
knowledge of the issue in question. 
5. This employee would come to my defense if I were 'attacked' by others. 
6. This employee would defend me to others in the organization if I made an honest 
mistake. 
Contribution 
7. This employee does work for me that goes beyond what is specified in his or her 
job description. 
8. This employee is willing to apply extra efforts, beyond those normally required, 
to meet my work goals. 
9. This employee does not mind working his or her hardest for me. 
Professional Respect 
10.1 am impressed with this employee's knowledge of his/her job. 
11.1 respect this employee's knowledge of and competence on the job. 
12.1 admire this employee's professional skills. 
217 
Subordinate access to information and resources (Spreitzer, 1996) 
Completed by Subordinates at Tl 
Instructions: In this section, you are asked about the information and resources available 
to you in your job. Please assess the extent to which you agree with each statement 
below. l=strongly disagree and 5=strongly agree. 
Subordinate access organization information 
1. I understand the strategies and goals of the organization 
2. I understand top management's vision of the organization 
3. I have access to the strategic information I need to do my job well 
Subordinate access to resources 
1. I can obtain the resources necessary to support new ideas 
2. When I need additional resources to do my job, I can usually get them. 
3. I have access to the resources I need to do my job well. 
218 
Perceived Supervisor Status (Eisenberger et al, 2002) 
(Completed by subordinates at Tl) 
Instructions: In this section, you are asked to consider the status your supervisor holds 
within the organization. Please assess the extent to which you agree with each of the 
following statements. l=strongly disagree and 5=strongly agree. 
1. The organization holds my supervisor in high regard. 
2. The organization gives my supervisor the chance to make important decisions. 
3. The organization values my supervisor's contributions. 
4. The organization gives my supervisor the authority to try new things. 
5. The organization supports decisions made by my supervisor. 
6. My supervisor participates in decisions that affect the entire organization. 
219 
Ascribed Identity, Items Surveyed, partially adapted from Hogg, Hains, and Mason 
(1998) and Mael and Ashforth (1992) 
Instructions: In this section, you are asked to consider how you perceive your supervisor 
in relationship to the work group. Please read each item below and assess the extent to 
which you agree with the statement. l=strongly disagree, 5=strongly agree. 
1. I identify my supervisor with our work group. 
2. My supervisor is "one of us", one of the group. 
3. My supervisor sees him/herself as a member of our work group. 
4. When I think about our work group, I envision my supervisor as one of the 
members. 
220 
Manager Accessibility, partially adapted from Janssen (2001) 
Instructions: This section asks you to consider the time availability and access you have 
to your manager. Please read each question and assess the degree to which you agree 
with each statement. 1= strongly disagree, 5= strongly agree 
Manager Availability 
1. My manager is not always available when a member of my work group needs him 
or her. (R) 
2. My manager is able to make time available to meet with his or her subordinates 
when they need it. 
3. My manager does not have enough time to provide the group the direction it 
needs. 
4. If a member of my work group has an issue, he or she can get access to our 
manager to deal with it. 
5. My manager is present for members of my work group at critical times. 
6. When a member of my work group needs input from our manager, he or she can 
usually get it in a timely fashion. 
Manager Time Constraints 
1. My manager faces significant pressures on his or her time. 
2. My manager feels a lot of time demands from his or her job. 
3. My manager feels a lot of time demands from his or her job. 
4. My manager has too many demands on his or her time 
221 
Affective Organizational Commitment (Allen and Meyer, 1990) 
Instructions: Please read the following statements and assess the degree to which you 
agree with each one. 1= strongly disagree to 5 = strongly agree 
1. I would be very happy to spend the rest of my career with this organization 
2. I enjoy discussing my organization with people outside it 
3. I really feel as if this organization's problems are my own 
4. I think that I could easily become as attached to another organization as I am to 
this one (R) 
5. I do not feel like 'part of the family' at my organization (R) 
6. I do not feel 'emotionally attached' to this organization (R) 
7. This organization has a great deal of personal meaning for me 
8. I do not feel a strong sense of belonging to my organization (R) 
222 
Subordinate Task Performance (Manager Assessed at Time 2) 
1. Relative to an absolute standard, please rate this employee's performance of the 
specific tasks required of his or her job (1 = poor to 5 = excellent) 
2. Relative to your expectations, how does this employee perform on the specific 
tasks of the job (1 = well below expectations to 5 = well above expectations) 
3. This employee uses appropriate methods and behaviors in performing his or her 
work (1= strongly disagree to 5= strongly agree) 
223
